
The Great Gatsby
了不起的盖茨比

By F. Scott Fitzgerald
作者：F·斯科特·菲茨杰拉德

Then wear the gold hat, if that will move her;
那就戴上金帽子吧，如果这能打动她的话；

If you can bounce high, bounce for her too,
如果你能跳得很高，也为她跳起来，

Till she cry ‘Lover, gold-hatted, high-bouncing lover,
直到她喊道：“情人，戴金帽子的高弹情人，

I must have you!’
我必须拥有你！

—THOMAS PARKE D’INVILLIERS
——托马斯·帕克·丹维利耶

Chapter 1
第1章

In my younger and more vulnerable years my father gave me some advice that I’ve been turning over in my mind ever since.
在我年轻、脆弱的时候，父亲给了我一些建议，从那时起我就一直在思考这些建议。

‘Whenever you feel like criticizing any one,’ he told me, ‘just remember that all the people in this world haven’t had the advantages that you’ve had.’
“每当你想批评任何人时，”他告诉我，“请记住，这个世界上并不是所有人都拥有你所拥有的优势。”

He didn’t say any more but we’ve always been unusually communicative in a reserved way, and I understood that he meant a great deal more than that. In consequence I’m inclined to reserve all judgments, a habit that has opened up many curious natures to me and also made me the victim of not a few veteran bores. The abnormal mind is quick to detect and attach itself to this quality when it appears in a normal person, and so it came about that in college I was unjustly accused of being a politician, because I was privy to the secret griefs of wild, unknown men. Most of the confidences were unsought—frequently I have feigned sleep, preoccupation, or a hostile levity when I realized by some unmistakable sign that an intimate revelation was quivering on the horizon—for the intimate revelations of young men or at least the terms in which they express them are usually plagiaristic and marred by obvious suppressions. Reserving judgments is a matter of infinite hope. I am still a little afraid of missing something if I forget that, as my father snobbishly suggested, and I snobbishly repeat a sense of the fundamental decencies is parcelled out unequally at birth.
他没有再说什么，但我们总是以一种不同寻常的含蓄方式进行交流，我明白他的意思远不止于此。因此，我倾向于保留所有判断，这种习惯让我产生了许多好奇的本性，也使我成为不少老手无聊的受害者。当这种品质出现在正常人身上时，异常的头脑很快就会发现并依恋它，因此，在大学里，我被不公正地指责为政治家，因为我了解狂野的、未知的秘密悲伤。男人。大多数的秘密都是不请自来的——当我意识到某种明显的迹象表明即将发生亲密的揭露时，我经常假装睡觉、全神贯注或充满敌意的轻率——对于年轻人的亲密揭露，或者至少是在其中的条款。他们表示自己的作品通常是抄袭的，并且受到明显的压制。保留判断是一件充满无限希望的事情。我仍然有点害怕如果我忘记了这一点，就会错过一些东西，正如我父亲势利地建议的那样，我势利地重复了一种基本的礼仪在出生时分配不平等的感觉。

And, after boasting this way of my tolerance, I come to the admission that it has a limit. Conduct may be founded on the hard rock or the wet marshes but after a certain point I don’t care what it’s founded on. When I came back from the East last autumn I felt that I wanted the world to be in uniform and at a sort of moral attention forever; I wanted no more riotous excursions with privileged glimpses into the human heart. Only Gatsby, the man who gives his name to this book, was exempt from my reaction—Gatsby who represented everything for which I have an unaffected scorn. If personality is an unbroken series of successful gestures, then there was something gorgeous about him, some heightened sensitivity to the promises of life, as if he were related to one of those intricate machines that register earthquakes ten thousand miles away. This responsiveness had nothing to do with that flabby impressionability which is dignified under the name of the ‘creative temperament’—it was an extraordinary gift for hope, a romantic readiness such as I have never found in any other person and which it is not likely I shall ever find again. No—Gatsby turned out all right at the end; it is what preyed on Gatsby, what foul dust floated in the wake of his dreams that temporarily closed out my interest in the abortive sorrows and short-winded elations of men.
而且，在夸耀我的这种宽容之后，我承认它是有限度的。行为可能建立在坚硬的岩石或潮湿的沼泽之上，但在某一点之后，我不在乎它的基础是什么。去年秋天，当我从东方回来时，我觉得我希望世界永远穿着制服并受到某种道德关注；我不想再进行喧闹的游览，以特权一睹人类的内心。只有盖茨比，这个以他的名字命名这本书的人，没有受到我的反应的影响——盖茨比代表了我不加掩饰的蔑视的一切。如果个性是一系列不间断的成功姿态，那么他身上就有一些华丽的东西，对生命的承诺有一种高度的敏感性，就好像他与那些记录一万英里外地震的复杂机器有关。这种反应能力与那种被称为“创造性气质”的软弱易感性无关——它是一种非凡的希望天赋，一种浪漫的准备，这是我从未在其他人身上发现过的，也是不可能的。我一定会再次找到。不——盖茨比最终表现得很好。正是这种东西掠夺了盖茨比，正是他的梦想之后飘浮的肮脏尘埃，暂时关闭了我对人类未遂的悲伤和短暂的欢欣的兴趣。

My family have been prominent, well-to-do people in this middle-western city for three generations. The Carraways are something of a clan and we have a tradition that we’re descended from the Dukes of Buccleuch, but the actual founder of my line was my grandfather’s brother who came here in fifty-one, sent a substitute to the Civil War and started the wholesale hardware business that my father carries on today.
我的家族在这座中西部城市已经是三代显赫、富裕的人了。卡罗威家族是一个氏族，我们有一个传统，我们是巴克卢公爵的后裔，但我的血统的真正创始人是我祖父的兄弟，他五十一岁来到这里，派了一个替补参加内战，开始了我父亲今天经营的批发五金生意。

I never saw this great-uncle but I’m supposed to look like him—with special reference to the rather hard-boiled painting that hangs in Father’s office. I graduated from New Haven in 1915, just a quarter of a century after my father, and a little later I participated in that delayed Teutonic migration known as the Great War. I enjoyed the counter-raid so thoroughly that I came back restless. Instead of being the warm center of the world the middle-west now seemed like the ragged edge of the universe—so I decided to go east and learn the bond business. Everybody I knew was in the bond business so I supposed it could support one more single man. All my aunts and uncles talked it over as if they were choosing a prep-school for me and finally said, ‘Why—ye-es’ with very grave, hesitant faces. Father agreed to finance me for a year and after various delays I came east, permanently, I thought, in the spring of twenty-two.
我从未见过这位叔祖父，但我应该看起来像他——特别是父亲办公室里挂着的那幅相当冷酷的画。 1915 年，我从纽黑文毕业，仅仅比我父亲晚了四分之一个世纪，不久之后，我参加了被称为“伟大战争”的条顿族延迟迁徙。我非常享受这次反袭击，以至于回来后焦躁不安。中西部不再是世界温暖的中心，现在看起来像是宇宙的粗糙边缘——所以我决定去东方学习债券业务。我认识的每个人都从事债券行业，所以我想这可以支持另一个单身男人。我所有的叔叔阿姨们都在谈论这件事，就好像他们正在为我选择一所预科学校一样，最后他们一脸严肃、犹豫地说道：“为什么——是的”。父亲同意资助我一年，经过多次拖延后，我在二十二岁的春天永久地来到了东部。

The practical thing was to find rooms in the city but it was a warm season and I had just left a country of wide lawns and friendly trees, so when a young man at the office suggested that we take a house together in a commuting town it sounded like a great idea. He found the house, a weather beaten cardboard bungalow at eighty a month, but at the last minute the firm ordered him to Washington and I went out to the country alone. I had a dog, at least I had him for a few days until he ran away, and an old Dodge and a Finnish woman who made my bed and cooked breakfast and muttered Finnish wisdom to herself over the electric stove.
实际的事情是在城市里找到房间，但当时是一个温暖的季节，而且我刚刚离开一个拥有宽阔草坪和友好树木的国家，所以当办公室的一个年轻人建议我们在一个通勤小镇一起买一套房子时，听起来是个好主意。他找到了房子，是一栋饱经风霜的纸板平房，每月租金八十美元，但在最后一刻，公司命令他去华盛顿，而我独自一人去了乡下。我养了一只狗，至少养了他几天，直到他逃跑，还有一辆老道奇车和一位芬兰女人，她为我铺床、煮早餐，在电炉上自言自语地念叨着芬兰人的智慧。

It was lonely for a day or so until one morning some man, more recently arrived than I, stopped me on the road.
我孤独地待了一天左右，直到有一天早上，一个比我来得更晚的人在路上拦住了我。

‘How do you get to West Egg village?’ he asked helplessly.
“怎么去西卵村？”他无奈地问道。

I told him. And as I walked on I was lonely no longer. I was a guide, a pathfinder, an original settler. He had casually conferred on me the freedom of the neighborhood.
我告诉他了。当我继续前行时，我不再感到孤独。我是一个向导，一个探路者，一个原始的定居者。他漫不经心地给了我邻里的自由。

And so with the sunshine and the great bursts of leaves growing on the trees—just as things grow in fast movies—I had that familiar conviction that life was beginning over again with the summer.
因此，伴随着阳光和树上长出的叶子——就像快速电影中的事物生长一样——我有一种熟悉的信念，即夏天的生活又开始了。

There was so much to read for one thing and so much fine health to be pulled down out of the young breath-giving air. I bought a dozen volumes on banking and credit and investment securities and they stood on my shelf in red and gold like new money from the mint, promising to unfold the shining secrets that only Midas and Morgan and Maecenas knew. And I had the high intention of reading many other books besides. I was rather literary in college—one year I wrote a series of very solemn and obvious editorials for the ‘Yale News’—and now I was going to bring back all such things into my life and become again that most limited of all specialists, the ‘well-rounded man.’ This isn’t just an epigram—life is much more successfully looked at from a single window, after all.
有一件事有太多值得读的东西，有太多美好的健康需要从年轻的呼吸空气中汲取。我买了十几本关于银行、信贷和投资证券的书，它们放在我的书架上，红色和金色的，就像铸币厂的新钱一样，承诺揭开只有迈达斯、摩根和梅塞纳斯知道的闪亮秘密。除此之外，我还有很多其他书籍的强烈愿望。我在大学时颇有文学天赋——有一年我为《耶鲁新闻》写了一系列非常严肃和明显的社论——现在我要把所有这些东西带回到我的生活中，并再次成为所有专家中最有限的， “多才多艺的人”。这不仅仅是一句警句——毕竟，从一个窗口来看生活要成功得多。

It was a matter of chance that I should have rented a house in one of the strangest communities in North America. It was on that slender riotous island which extends itself due east of New York and where there are, among other natural curiosities, two unusual formations of land. Twenty miles from the city a pair of enormous eggs, identical in contour and separated only by a courtesy bay, jut out into the most domesticated body of salt water in the Western Hemisphere, the great wet barnyard of Long Island Sound. They are not perfect ovals—like the egg in the Columbus story they are both crushed flat at the contact end—but their physical resemblance must be a source of perpetual confusion to the gulls that fly overhead. To the wingless a more arresting phenomenon is their dissimilarity in every particular except shape and size.
我本来应该在北美最奇怪的社区之一租一套房子，这纯属偶然。它位于纽约正东延伸的那个细长的、喧闹的岛屿上，除了其他自然奇观之外，那里还有两种不寻常的陆地结构。距离城市二十英里，一对巨大的蛋，轮廓相同，仅隔一个礼貌的海湾，伸入西半球最驯化的咸水体，长岛海峡的大潮湿谷仓。它们不是完美的椭圆形——就像哥伦布故事中的鸡蛋一样，它们在接触端都被压扁了——但它们的物理相似性一定会让飞过头顶的海鸥永远感到困惑。对于无翼生物来说，一个更引人注目的现象是，除了形状和大小之外，它们在每个细节上都存在差异。

I lived at West Egg, the—well, the less fashionable of the two, though this is a most superficial tag to express the bizarre and not a little sinister contrast between them. My house was at the very tip of the egg, only fifty yards from the Sound, and squeezed between two huge places that rented for twelve or fifteen thousand a season. The one on my right was a colossal affair by any standard—it was a factual imitation of some Hôtel de Ville in Normandy, with a tower on one side, spanking new under a thin beard of raw ivy, and a marble swimming pool and more than forty acres of lawn and garden. It was Gatsby’s mansion. Or rather, as I didn’t know Mr. Gatsby it was a mansion inhabited by a gentleman of that name. My own house was an eye-sore, but it was a small eye-sore, and it had been overlooked, so I had a view of the water, a partial view of my neighbor’s lawn, and the consoling proximity of millionaires—all for eighty dollars a month.
我住在西埃格，嗯，是两者中不太时尚的一个，尽管这是一个最肤浅的标签，用来表达它们之间的奇异而不是一点险恶的对比。我的房子位于鸡蛋的最顶端，距离海湾只有五十码，挤在两个每季租金十二或一万五千美元的大地方之间。我右边的那个无论以什么标准来看都是一个巨大的建筑——它实际上是对诺曼底某个市政厅的模仿，一侧有一座塔楼，在稀疏的常春藤胡须下焕然一新，还有一个大理石游泳池等等超过四十英亩的草坪和花园。那是盖茨比的豪宅。或者更确切地说，因为我不认识盖茨比先生，那是一座宅邸，居住着一位同名的绅士。我自己的房子是一个碍眼的地方，但它是一个小碍眼的地方，而且它被忽视了，所以我可以看到水，看到邻居草坪的部分景色，以及令人安慰的百万富翁的接近——所有这些都是为了每月八十美元。

Across the courtesy bay the white palaces of fashionable East Egg glittered along the water, and the history of the summer really begins on the evening I drove over there to have dinner with the Tom Buchanans. Daisy was my second cousin once removed and I’d known Tom in college. And just after the war I spent two days with them in Chicago.
穿过礼貌海湾，时尚东卵的白色宫殿沿着水面闪闪发光，夏天的历史真正开始于我开车去那里与汤姆·布坎南一家共进晚餐的那个晚上。黛西是我的远房表弟，我在大学时就认识汤姆了。战后不久，我在芝加哥和他们一起度过了两天。

Her husband, among various physical accomplishments, had been one of the most powerful ends that ever played football at New Haven—a national figure in a way, one of those men who reach such an acute limited excellence at twenty-one that everything afterward savors of anti-climax. His family were enormously wealthy—even in college his freedom with money was a matter for reproach—but now he’d left Chicago and come east in a fashion that rather took your breath away: for instance he’d brought down a string of polo ponies from Lake Forest. It was hard to realize that a man in my own generation was wealthy enough to do that.
她的丈夫在各种身体成就中，曾是纽黑文足球史上最强大的终结者之一——在某种程度上是一个全国性的人物，是那些在二十一岁就达到如此敏锐有限的卓越的人之一，以至于后来的一切都令人回味。反高潮的。他的家庭非常富有——即使在大学里，他在金钱上的自由也是值得谴责的——但现在他离开了芝加哥，以一种令人惊叹的方式来到东部：例如，他拉下了一串马球衫。来自森林湖的小马。很难想象我们这一代人有足够的财富做到这一点。

Why they came east I don’t know. They had spent a year in France, for no particular reason, and then drifted here and there unrestfully wherever people played polo and were rich together. This was a permanent move, said Daisy over the telephone, but I didn’t believe it—I had no sight into Daisy’s heart but I felt that Tom would drift on forever seeking a little wistfully for the dramatic turbulence of some irrecoverable football game.
我不知道他们为什么来到东方。他们无缘无故地在法国呆了一年，然后不安地漂泊到有人打马球、一起发财的地方。这是永久性的举动，黛西在电话里说，但我不相信这一点——我看不到黛西的内心，但我觉得汤姆会永远漂流，对一些无法挽回的足球比赛的戏剧性动荡寻求一点渴望。

And so it happened that on a warm windy evening I drove over to East Egg to see two old friends whom I scarcely knew at all. Their house was even more elaborate than I expected, a cheerful red and white Georgian Colonial mansion overlooking the bay. The lawn started at the beach and ran toward the front door for a quarter of a mile, jumping over sun-dials and brick walks and burning gardens—finally when it reached the house drifting up the side in bright vines as though from the momentum of its run. The front was broken by a line of French windows, glowing now with reflected gold, and wide open to the warm windy afternoon, and Tom Buchanan in riding clothes was standing with his legs apart on the front porch.
就这样，在一个温暖、有风的夜晚，我开车去东卵看望两位我几乎不认识的老朋友。他们的房子比我想象的还要精致，是一栋令人愉快的红白相间的乔治亚殖民地豪宅，俯瞰着海湾。草坪从海滩开始，朝前门延伸了四分之一英里，跳过了日晷、砖砌小道和燃烧的花园——最后，当它到达房子时，明亮的藤蔓从侧面飘过，仿佛受到了它的运行。前面被一排落地窗打破，现在反射着金色，在温暖的多风午后，汤姆·布坎南穿着骑马服，双腿分开地站在前廊上。

He had changed since his New Haven years. Now he was a sturdy, straw haired man of thirty with a rather hard mouth and a supercilious manner. Two shining, arrogant eyes had established dominance over his face and gave him the appearance of always leaning aggressively forward. Not even the effeminate swank of his riding clothes could hide the enormous power of that body—he seemed to fill those glistening boots until he strained the top lacing and you could see a great pack of muscle shifting when his shoulder moved under his thin coat. It was a body capable of enormous leverage—a cruel body.
与纽黑文时期相比，他已经发生了变化。现在他是一个三十岁的壮实、稻草头发的男人，嘴巴相当严厉，态度也很傲慢。两只闪亮而傲慢的眼睛在他的脸上占据了主导地位，让他看起来总是咄咄逼人地前倾。即使是他那女性化的骑行服也无法掩盖那身体的巨大力量——他似乎充满了那些闪闪发光的靴子，直到他拉紧了顶部的鞋带，当他的肩膀在薄外套下移动时，你可以看到一大群肌肉在移动。这是一个具有巨大影响力的身体——一个残酷的身体。

His speaking voice, a gruff husky tenor, added to the impression of fractiousness he conveyed. There was a touch of paternal contempt in it, even toward people he liked—and there were men at New Haven who had hated his guts.
他说话的声音是粗哑沙哑的男高音，更增添了他暴躁的印象。其中有一丝父亲般的蔑视，甚至是对他喜欢的人——而且纽黑文有些人对他恨之入骨。

‘Now, don’t think my opinion on these matters is final,’ he seemed to say, ‘just because I’m stronger and more of a man than you are.’ We were in the same Senior Society, and while we were never intimate I always had the impression that he approved of me and wanted me to like him with some harsh, defiant wistfulness of his own.
“现在，不要认为我对这些问题的意见是最终的，”他似乎在说，“只是因为我比你更强大，更有男子气概。”我们在同一个老年协会，而我们在从不亲密，我总有这样的印象：他认可我，希望我喜欢他，同时也带着他自己的某种严厉、挑衅的渴望。

We talked for a few minutes on the sunny porch.
我们在阳光明媚的门廊上聊了几分钟。

‘I’ve got a nice place here,’ he said, his eyes flashing about restlessly.
“我在这里有一个不错的地方，”他说道，他的眼睛不安地闪烁着。

Turning me around by one arm he moved a broad flat hand along the front vista, including in its sweep a sunken Italian garden, a half acre of deep pungent roses and a snub-nosed motor boat that bumped the tide off shore.
他用一只手臂把我转过身，一只宽阔平坦的手沿着前面的景色移动，包括一个下沉的意大利花园、半英亩深的辛辣玫瑰和一艘把潮水冲离岸边的扁头摩托艇。

‘It belonged to Demaine the oil man.’ He turned me around again, politely and abruptly. ‘We’ll go inside.’
“它属于石油大亨德梅因。”他再次把我转过身来，礼貌而突然。 “我们进去吧。”

We walked through a high hallway into a bright rosy-colored space, fragilely bound into the house by French windows at either end. The windows were ajar and gleaming white against the fresh grass outside that seemed to grow a little way into the house. A breeze blew through the room, blew curtains in at one end and out the other like pale flags, twisting them up toward the frosted wedding cake of the ceiling—and then rippled over the wine-colored rug, making a shadow on it as wind does on the sea.
我们穿过一条高高的走廊，进入一个明亮的玫瑰色空间，两端的落地窗脆弱地与房子相连。窗户半开着，在外面新鲜的青草映衬下闪闪发光，青草似乎已经长到了房子里。一阵微风吹过房间，像苍白的旗帜一样把窗帘的一端吹进来，另一端吹出来，把它们卷向天花板上结霜的婚礼蛋糕——然后在酒红色的地毯上荡漾，在上面留下风一样的影子。在海上。

The only completely stationary object in the room was an enormous couch on which two young women were buoyed up as though upon an anchored balloon. They were both in white and their dresses were rippling and fluttering as if they had just been blown back in after a short flight around the house. I must have stood for a few moments listening to the whip and snap of the curtains and the groan of a picture on the wall. Then there was a boom as Tom Buchanan shut the rear windows and the caught wind died out about the room and the curtains and the rugs and the two young women ballooned slowly to the floor.
房间里唯一完全静止的物体是一张巨大的沙发，上面漂浮着两个年轻女子，就像在一个固定的气球上一样。她们都穿着白色的衣服，裙子随风飘扬，仿佛她们刚绕着房子飞了一小段才被风吹回来。我一定站了一会儿，听着窗帘的鞭打声和墙上的画的呻吟声。然后，汤姆·布坎南关上后窗，发出一声巨响，房间、窗帘和地毯周围的风都消失了，两个年轻女子慢慢地飘到了地板上。

The younger of the two was a stranger to me. She was extended full length at her end of the divan, completely motionless and with her chin raised a little as if she were balancing something on it which was quite likely to fall. If she saw me out of the corner of her eyes she gave no hint of it—indeed, I was almost surprised into murmuring an apology for having disturbed her by coming in.
两个人中较年轻的一个对我来说很陌生。她在沙发的一端完全伸展开来，一动不动，下巴微微抬起，好像在平衡什么很可能会掉下来的东西。即使她用眼角的余光看到了我，她也没有给出任何暗示——事实上，我几乎惊讶地低声道歉，因为进来打扰了她。

The other girl, Daisy, made an attempt to rise—she leaned slightly forward with a conscientious expression—then she laughed, an absurd, charming little laugh, and I laughed too and came forward into the room.
另一个女孩，黛西，试图站起来——她微微前倾，一脸认真的表情——然后她笑了，一种荒谬而迷人的小笑，我也笑了，走进了房间。

‘I’m p-paralyzed with happiness.’
“我幸福得瘫痪了。”

She laughed again, as if she said something very witty, and held my hand for a moment, looking up into my face, promising that there was no one in the world she so much wanted to see. That was a way she had. She hinted in a murmur that the surname of the balancing girl was Baker. (I’ve heard it said that Daisy’s murmur was only to make people lean toward her; an irrelevant criticism that made it no less charming.)
她又笑了，好像说了些什么非常诙谐的话，握住我的手一会儿，抬头看着我的脸，保证世界上没有人是她那么想见的。这就是她的方式。她低声暗示平衡女孩姓贝克。 （我听说黛西的低语只是为了让人们向她倾斜；一种无关紧要的批评，却丝毫不减其魅力。）

At any rate Miss Baker’s lips fluttered, she nodded at me almost imperceptibly and then quickly tipped her head back again—the object she was balancing had obviously tottered a little and given her something of a fright. Again a sort of apology arose to my lips. Almost any exhibition of complete self sufficiency draws a stunned tribute from me.
无论如何，贝克小姐的嘴唇动了动，她几乎难以察觉地向我点了点头，然后很快又把头向后仰了——她平衡的物体显然有点摇晃，让她有些害怕。我的嘴里再次出现了一种歉意。几乎所有完全自给自足的展览都会引起我的震惊。

I looked back at my cousin who began to ask me questions in her low, thrilling voice. It was the kind of voice that the ear follows up and down as if each speech is an arrangement of notes that will never be played again. Her face was sad and lovely with bright things in it, bright eyes and a bright passionate mouth—but there was an excitement in her voice that men who had cared for her found difficult to forget: a singing compulsion, a whispered ‘Listen,’ a promise that she had done gay, exciting things just a while since and that there were gay, exciting things hovering in the next hour.
我回头看着表姐，她开始用她低沉而激动的声音问我问题。这是一种耳朵会上下跟随的声音，就好像每次讲话都是一个永远不会再被演奏的音符的排列。她的脸悲伤而可爱，里面有明亮的东西，明亮的眼睛和明亮而热情的嘴——但她的声音中有一种兴奋，让关心她的男人很难忘记：一种唱歌的冲动，一种低声的“听”，她承诺，从那以后不久，她就做了一些快乐、令人兴奋的事情，并且在接下来的一个小时里，还会发生一些快乐、令人兴奋的事情。

I told her how I had stopped off in Chicago for a day on my way east and how a dozen people had sent their love through me.
我告诉她我在向东的途中如何在芝加哥停留了一天，以及十几个人如何通过我表达了他们的爱。

‘Do they miss me?’ she cried ecstatically.
“他们想我吗？”她欣喜若狂地喊道。

‘The whole town is desolate. All the cars have the left rear wheel painted black as a mourning wreath and there’s a persistent wail all night along the North Shore.’
‘整个城镇一片荒凉。所有汽车的左后轮都被漆成黑色，就像哀悼花圈一样，北岸整晚都传来持续不断的哀号声。

‘How gorgeous! Let’s go back, Tom. Tomorrow!’ Then she added irrelevantly, ‘You ought to see the baby.’
‘多漂亮呀！我们回去吧，汤姆。明天！”然后她漫不经心地补充道，“你应该看看孩子。”

‘I’d like to.’
'我想。'

‘She’s asleep. She’s two years old. Haven’t you ever seen her?’
‘她睡着了。她两岁了。你没见过她吗？

‘Never.’
'绝不。'

‘Well, you ought to see her. She’s——‘
‘嗯，你应该去看看她。她是——‘

Tom Buchanan who had been hovering restlessly about the room stopped and rested his hand on my shoulder.
汤姆·布坎南一直在房间里不安地徘徊，他停下来，把手搭在我的肩膀上。

‘What you doing, Nick?’
“你在做什么，尼克？”

‘I’m a bond man.’
“我是一个债券人。”

‘Who with?’
'谁与？'

I told him.
我告诉他了。

‘Never heard of them,’ he remarked decisively.
“从来没有听说过他们，”他果断地说。

This annoyed me.
这让我很恼火。

‘You will,’ I answered shortly. ‘You will if you stay in the East.’
“你会的，”我简短地回答。 “如果你留在东部，你就会的。”

‘Oh, I’ll stay in the East, don’t you worry,’ he said, glancing at Daisy and then back at me, as if he were alert for something more. ‘I’d be a God Damned fool to live anywhere else.’
“哦，我会留在东部，你不用担心，”他说，看了一眼黛西，然后又看了看我，好像他在警惕更多的事情。 “如果我住在其他地方，我就是个该死的傻瓜。”

At this point Miss Baker said ‘Absolutely!’ with such suddenness that I started—it was the first word she uttered since I came into the room. Evidently it surprised her as much as it did me, for she yawned and with a series of rapid, deft movements stood up into the room.
这时，贝克小姐突然说“当然！”，这让我吃了一惊——这是我进房间后她说的第一句话。显然，这让她和我一样感到惊讶，因为她打了个哈欠，并以一系列快速、灵巧的动作站进了房间。

‘I’m stiff,’ she complained, ‘I’ve been lying on that sofa for as long as I can remember.’
“我浑身僵硬，”她抱怨道，“从我记事起，我就一直躺在那张沙发上。”

‘Don’t look at me,’ Daisy retorted. ‘I’ve been trying to get you to New York all afternoon.’
“别看着我，”黛西反驳道。 “整个下午我都在想办法送你去纽约。”

‘No, thanks,’ said Miss Baker to the four cocktails just in from the pantry, ‘I’m absolutely in training.’
“不，谢谢，”贝克小姐对着从食品储藏室送来的四杯鸡尾酒说道，“我绝对在接受训练。”

Her host looked at her incredulously.
主人不可置信地看着她。

‘You are!’ He took down his drink as if it were a drop in the bottom of a glass. ‘How you ever get anything done is beyond me.’
“你是！”他把饮料倒了下去，就好像它是杯底的一滴水一样。 “我无法理解你如何完成任何事情。”

I looked at Miss Baker wondering what it was she ‘got done.’ I enjoyed looking at her. She was a slender, small-breasted girl, with an erect carriage which she accentuated by throwing her body backward at the shoulders like a young cadet. Her grey sun-strained eyes looked back at me with polite reciprocal curiosity out of a wan, charming discontented face. It occurred to me now that I had seen her, or a picture of her, somewhere before.
我看着贝克小姐，想知道她“完成了”什么。我喜欢看着她。她是个身材苗条、胸脯较小的女孩，身材挺拔，像年轻的学员一样，身体向后靠在肩膀上，更加突出了这一点。她那双灰色的眼睛在阳光下显得紧张，苍白、迷人、不满的脸上带着礼貌的相互好奇，回望着我。现在我突然想起我以前在什么地方见过她，或者她的照片。

‘You live in West Egg,’ she remarked contemptuously. ‘I know somebody there.’
“你住在西埃格，”她轻蔑地说。 “我认识那里的一个人。”

‘I don’t know a single——‘
“我一个也不认识——”

‘You must know Gatsby.’
“你一定认识盖茨比。”

‘Gatsby?’ demanded Daisy. ‘What Gatsby?’
“盖茨比？”黛西问道。 “什么盖茨比？”

Before I could reply that he was my neighbor dinner was announced; wedging his tense arm imperatively under mine Tom Buchanan compelled me from the room as though he were moving a checker to another square.
我还没来得及回答他是我的邻居，晚餐就被宣布了；汤姆·布坎南用他紧张的手臂强制地把我压在我的下面，迫使我离开房间，就好像他正在把棋子移到另一个格子上一样。

Slenderly, languidly, their hands set lightly on their hips the two young women preceded us out onto a rosy-colored porch open toward the sunset where four candles flickered on the table in the diminished wind.
两位年轻女子纤细而慵懒地把手轻轻放在臀部上，在我们前面走到玫瑰色的门廊上，门廊朝向日落，四支蜡烛在微弱的风中在桌子上闪烁。

‘Why CANDLES?’ objected Daisy, frowning. She snapped them out with her fingers. ‘In two weeks it’ll be the longest day in the year.’ She looked at us all radiantly. ‘Do you always watch for the longest day of the year and then miss it? I always watch for the longest day in the year and then miss it.’
“为什么是蜡烛？”黛西皱着眉头反对道。她用手指把它们折断。 “两周后，这将是一年中最长的一天。”她满面笑容地看着我们大家。 “你是否总是注视着一年中最长的一天，然后就错过了？我总是期待一年中最长的一天，然后就错过了。”

‘We ought to plan something,’ yawned Miss Baker, sitting down at the table as if she were getting into bed.
“我们应该计划一些事情，”贝克小姐打着哈欠，坐在桌边，好像要上床睡觉一样。

‘All right,’ said Daisy. ‘What’ll we plan?’ She turned to me helplessly. ‘What do people plan?’
“好吧，”黛西说。 “我们打算怎么办？”她无助地转向我。 “人们有什么计划？”

Before I could answer her eyes fastened with an awed expression on her little finger.
我还没来得及回答，她的眼睛就带着敬畏的表情盯着她的小指。

‘Look!’ she complained. ‘I hurt it.’
“看！”她抱怨道。 “我伤害了它。”

We all looked—the knuckle was black and blue.
我们都看了看——指关节是青一块紫一块的。

‘You did it, Tom,’ she said accusingly. ‘I know you didn’t mean to but you DID do it. That’s what I get for marrying a brute of a man, a great big hulking physical specimen of a——‘
“你做到了，汤姆，”她指责道。 “我知道你不是故意的，但你确实这么做了。这就是我嫁给一个粗野的男人所得到的结果，一个巨大的、笨重的身体样本——”

‘I hate that word hulking,’ objected Tom crossly, ‘even in kidding.’
“我讨厌‘笨重’这个词，”汤姆生气地反对，“即使是在开玩笑。”

‘Hulking,’ insisted Daisy.
“笨重的，”黛西坚持说。

Sometimes she and Miss Baker talked at once, unobtrusively and with a bantering inconsequence that was never quite chatter, that was as cool as their white dresses and their impersonal eyes in the absence of all desire. They were here—and they accepted Tom and me, making only a polite pleasant effort to entertain or to be entertained. They knew that presently dinner would be over and a little later the evening too would be over and casually put away. It was sharply different from the West where an evening was hurried from phase to phase toward its close in a continually disappointed anticipation or else in sheer nervous dread of the moment itself.
有时她和贝克小姐同时说话，不引人注意，带着一种无关紧要的玩笑，从来都不是闲聊，就像她们的白色裙子和没有任何欲望的客观眼睛一样冷静。他们在这里——他们接受了汤姆和我，只是礼貌地愉快地努力招待或接受招待。他们知道晚餐很快就会结束，过一会儿晚上也会结束并随意收起。这与西方截然不同，在西方，晚上总是在不断失望的期待中，或者在对那一刻本身的紧张恐惧中，从一个阶段匆匆走向结束。

‘You make me feel uncivilized, Daisy,’ I confessed on my second glass of corky but rather impressive claret. ‘Can’t you talk about crops or something?’
“你让我感觉不文明，黛西，”我在喝第二杯软木塞但令人印象深刻的红葡萄酒时承认。 “你不能谈谈庄稼什么的吗？”

I meant nothing in particular by this remark but it was taken up in an unexpected way.
我这句话没有什么特别的意思，但它却以一种意想不到的方式被提及。

‘Civilization’s going to pieces,’ broke out Tom violently. ‘I’ve gotten to be a terrible pessimist about things. Have you read ‘The Rise of the Coloured Empires’ by this man Goddard?’
“文明正在分崩离析。”汤姆猛烈地说。 “我对事情变得非常悲观。你读过戈达德写的《有色帝国的崛起》吗？

‘Why, no,’ I answered, rather surprised by his tone.
“为什么，不，”我回答道，对他的语气感到相当惊讶。

‘Well, it’s a fine book, and everybody ought to read it. The idea is if we don’t look out the white race will be—will be utterly submerged. It’s all scientific stuff; it’s been proved.’
“嗯，这是一本好书，每个人都应该读一读。这个想法是，如果我们不注意的话，白人种族将会被完全淹没。这都是科学的东西；这已经被证明了。

‘Tom’s getting very profound,’ said Daisy with an expression of unthoughtful sadness. ‘He reads deep books with long words in them. What was that word we——‘
“汤姆变得非常深刻了，”黛西说道，脸上带着不经意的悲伤。 “他读的书很深，里面有很长的文字。我们这个词是什么——‘

‘Well, these books are all scientific,’ insisted Tom, glancing at her impatiently. ‘This fellow has worked out the whole thing. It’s up to us who are the dominant race to watch out or these other races will have control of things.’
“嗯，这些书都是科学的，”汤姆不耐烦地看了她一眼，坚持说。 ‘这家伙已经解决了整件事。这取决于我们谁是占主导地位的种族，需要警惕，否则这些其他种族将控制一切。

‘We’ve got to beat them down,’ whispered Daisy, winking ferociously toward the fervent sun.
“我们必须打败他们，”黛西低声说道，对着炽热的太阳猛烈地眨着眼睛。

‘You ought to live in California—’ began Miss Baker but Tom interrupted her by shifting heavily in his chair.
“你应该住在加利福尼亚州——”贝克小姐开口说道，但汤姆在椅子上重重地移动了一下，打断了她。

‘This idea is that we’re Nordics. I am, and you are and you are and——’ After an infinitesimal hesitation he included Daisy with a slight nod and she winked at me again. ‘—and we’ve produced all the things that go to make civilization—oh, science and art and all that. Do you see?’
“这个想法是我们是北欧人。我是，你是，你是——”在一阵无限的犹豫之后，他轻轻地点了点头，将黛西包括在内，她又对我眨了眨眼。 “——我们已经创造了所有能够创造文明的东西——哦，科学和艺术等等。你有看到？'

There was something pathetic in his concentration as if his complacency, more acute than of old, was not enough to him any more. When, almost immediately, the telephone rang inside and the butler left the porch Daisy seized upon the momentary interruption and leaned toward me.
他的注意力集中得有些可悲，仿佛他的自满情绪比以前更加强烈，但对他来说已经不够了。几乎立刻，电话铃响了，管家离开了门廊，黛西抓住了这短暂的中断，靠向我。

‘I’ll tell you a family secret,’ she whispered enthusiastically. ‘It’s about the butler’s nose. Do you want to hear about the butler’s nose?’
“我要告诉你一个家庭秘密，”她热情地低声说道。 “是关于管家的鼻子的。”你想听听管家鼻子的事吗？

‘That’s why I came over tonight.’
“这就是我今晚过来的原因。”

‘Well, he wasn’t always a butler; he used to be the silver polisher for some people in New York that had a silver service for two hundred people. He had to polish it from morning till night until finally it began to affect his nose——‘
“嗯，他并不总是一名管家；他是一名管家。”他曾经是纽约一些人的银器抛光师，为两百人提供银器服务。他必须从早到晚地打磨它，直到最后它开始影响他的鼻子——’

‘Things went from bad to worse,’ suggested Miss Baker.
“事情变得越来越糟，”贝克小姐建议道。

‘Yes. Things went from bad to worse until finally he had to give up his position.’
'是的。事情变得越来越糟，直到最后他不得不放弃自己的职位。

For a moment the last sunshine fell with romantic affection upon her glowing face; her voice compelled me forward breathlessly as I listened—then the glow faded, each light deserting her with lingering regret like children leaving a pleasant street at dusk.
刹那间，最后的阳光带着浪漫的爱意落在她容光焕发的脸上。当我聆听时，她的声音迫使我气喘吁吁地向前走——然后光芒消失了，每一道光都带着挥之不去的遗憾离开了她，就像孩子们在黄昏时离开了一条宜人的街道。

The butler came back and murmured something close to Tom’s ear whereupon Tom frowned, pushed back his chair and without a word went inside. As if his absence quickened something within her Daisy leaned forward again, her voice glowing and singing.
管家回来了，在汤姆耳边低声说了些什么，汤姆皱起眉头，推开椅子，一言不发地走了进去。仿佛他的缺席加速了黛西体内的某些东西，黛西再次向前倾身，她的声音闪闪发光，歌唱着。

‘I love to see you at my table, Nick. You remind me of a—of a rose, an absolute rose. Doesn’t he?’ She turned to Miss Baker for confirmation. ‘An absolute rose?’
“我很高兴在我的餐桌上见到你，尼克。你让我想起一朵玫瑰，一朵绝对的玫瑰。不是吗？”她转向贝克小姐寻求确认。 “绝对的玫瑰？”

This was untrue. I am not even faintly like a rose. She was only extemporizing but a stirring warmth flowed from her as if her heart was trying to come out to you concealed in one of those breathless, thrilling words. Then suddenly she threw her napkin on the table and excused herself and went into the house.
这是不真实的。我一点也不像玫瑰。她只是即兴发挥，但她却从她身上流露出一股激动人心的温暖，仿佛她的心正试图向你倾诉，藏在那些令人喘不过气来的激动人心的话语中。然后她突然把餐巾扔在桌子上，告辞走进屋里。

Miss Baker and I exchanged a short glance consciously devoid of meaning. I was about to speak when she sat up alertly and said ‘Sh!’ in a warning voice. A subdued impassioned murmur was audible in the room beyond and Miss Baker leaned forward, unashamed, trying to hear. The murmur trembled on the verge of coherence, sank down, mounted excitedly, and then ceased altogether.
贝克小姐和我有意识地交换了一眼，但毫无意义。我正要说话，她警觉地坐了起来，用警告的声音说“嘘！”。远处的房间里传来一阵低沉而热情的低语声，贝克小姐毫不羞愧地向前倾身，试图听听。低语声在连贯的边缘颤抖着，低沉下来，兴奋地上升，然后完全停止。

‘This Mr. Gatsby you spoke of is my neighbor——’ I said.
“你说的这位盖茨比先生是我的邻居——”我说。

‘Don’t talk. I want to hear what happens.’
‘别说话。我想听听会发生什么。

‘Is something happening?’ I inquired innocently.
“有什么事吗？”我天真地问道。

‘You mean to say you don’t know?’ said Miss Baker, honestly surprised. ‘I thought everybody knew.’
“你的意思是说你不知道？”贝克小姐说，她真的很惊讶。 “我以为每个人都知道。”

‘I don’t.’
'我不。'

‘Why——’ she said hesitantly, ‘Tom’s got some woman in New York.’
“为什么——”她犹豫地说，“汤姆在纽约有一个女人。”

‘Got some woman?’ I repeated blankly.
“有女人吗？”我茫然地重复道。

Miss Baker nodded.
贝克小姐点点头。

‘She might have the decency not to telephone him at dinner-time. Don’t you think?’
“她也许有礼貌，不会在晚餐时间给他打电话。你不觉得吗？

Almost before I had grasped her meaning there was the flutter of a dress and the crunch of leather boots and Tom and Daisy were back at the table.
几乎在我明白她的意思之前，裙子飘动，皮靴嘎吱作响，汤姆和黛西回到了桌边。

‘It couldn’t be helped!’ cried Daisy with tense gayety.
“没办法！”黛西紧张而高兴地喊道。

She sat down, glanced searchingly at Miss Baker and then at me and continued: ‘I looked outdoors for a minute and it’s very romantic outdoors. There’s a bird on the lawn that I think must be a nightingale come over on the Cunard or White Star Line. He’s singing away——’ her voice sang ‘——It’s romantic, isn’t it, Tom?’
她坐下来，探寻地看了一眼贝克小姐，然后又看了我一眼，继续说道：“我看了一会儿户外，户外非常浪漫。”草坪上有一只鸟，我想它一定是冠达邮轮或白星航运公司过来的夜莺。他在唱歌——”她的声音唱道，“——这很浪漫，不是吗，汤姆？

‘Very romantic,’ he said, and then miserably to me: ‘If it’s light enough after dinner I want to take you down to the stables.’
“非常浪漫，”他说，然后痛苦地对我说：“如果晚饭后天色足够亮的话，我想带你去马厩。”

The telephone rang inside, startlingly, and as Daisy shook her head decisively at Tom the subject of the stables, in fact all subjects, vanished into air. Among the broken fragments of the last five minutes at table I remember the candles being lit again, pointlessly, and I was conscious of wanting to look squarely at every one and yet to avoid all eyes. I couldn’t guess what Daisy and Tom were thinking but I doubt if even Miss Baker who seemed to have mastered a certain hardy skepticism was able utterly to put this fifth guest’s shrill metallic urgency out of mind. To a certain temperament the situation might have seemed intriguing—my own instinct was to telephone immediately for the police.
电话铃响了，令人吃惊，当黛西果断地向汤姆摇摇头时，马厩的话题，事实上所有的话题，都消失在空气中。在餐桌上最后五分钟的碎片中，我记得蜡烛再次毫无意义地被点燃，我意识到想要正视每一个人，但又要避开所有人的目光。我无法猜测黛西和汤姆在想什么，但我怀疑即使是似乎掌握了某种顽强怀疑论的贝克小姐也能完全忘记第五位客人尖锐的金属紧迫感。对于某些人来说，这种情况可能看起来很有趣——我自己的本能是立即打电话报警。

The horses, needless to say, were not mentioned again. Tom and Miss Baker, with several feet of twilight between them strolled back into the library, as if to a vigil beside a perfectly tangible body, while trying to look pleasantly interested and a little deaf I followed Daisy around a chain of connecting verandas to the porch in front. In its deep gloom we sat down side by side on a wicker settee.
不用说，再也没有提到过马匹。汤姆和贝克小姐，隔着几英尺的暮光，漫步回到图书馆，仿佛在一具完全有形的身体旁边守夜，同时试图表现出愉快的兴趣，但有点聋，我跟着黛西绕了一串连接阳台到图书馆。门廊在前面。在幽暗的夜色中，我们并排坐在一张柳条长椅上。

Daisy took her face in her hands, as if feeling its lovely shape, and her eyes moved gradually out into the velvet dusk. I saw that turbulent emotions possessed her, so I asked what I thought would be some sedative questions about her little girl.
黛西双手捧着脸，仿佛感受着它可爱的形状，她的眼睛逐渐移向天鹅绒般的暮色。我看到她的情绪激动不已，所以我问了一些我认为可以让她的小女儿平静下来的问题。

‘We don’t know each other very well, Nick,’ she said suddenly. ‘Even if we are cousins. You didn’t come to my wedding.’
“我们彼此不太了解，尼克，”她突然说道。 ‘即使我们是表兄弟。你没有来参加我的婚礼。

‘I wasn’t back from the war.’
“我还没有从战争中回来。”

‘That’s true.’ She hesitated. ‘Well, I’ve had a very bad time, Nick, and I’m pretty cynical about everything.’
“确实如此。”她犹豫了一下。 “嗯，我过得很糟糕，尼克，我对一切都很愤世嫉俗。”

Evidently she had reason to be. I waited but she didn’t say any more, and after a moment I returned rather feebly to the subject of her daughter.
显然她有理由这样做。我等了一会儿，但她没有再说什么，过了一会儿，我又无力地回到她女儿的话题上。

‘I suppose she talks, and—eats, and everything.’
“我想她会说话，并且——吃东西，等等。”

‘Oh, yes.’ She looked at me absently. ‘Listen, Nick; let me tell you what I said when she was born. Would you like to hear?’
“哦，是的。”她心不在焉地看着我。 “听着，尼克；让我告诉你她出生时我说了什么。你想听吗？

‘Very much.’
'非常。'

‘It’ll show you how I’ve gotten to feel about—things. Well, she was less than an hour old and Tom was God knows where. I woke up out of the ether with an utterly abandoned feeling and asked the nurse right away if it was a boy or a girl. She told me it was a girl, and so I turned my head away and wept. ‘All right,’ I said, ‘I’m glad it’s a girl. And I hope she’ll be a fool—that’s the best thing a girl can be in this world, a beautiful little fool.’
“它会告诉你我对——事物的感受。”好吧，她出生还不到一个小时，而汤姆天知道在哪里。我从乙醚中醒来，有一种完全被遗弃的感觉，我立即问护士是男孩还是女孩。她告诉我那是个女孩，所以我转过头哭了。 “好吧，”我说，“我很高兴是个女孩。”我希望她是个傻瓜——这是一个女孩在这个世界上能做的最好的事情，一个美丽的小傻瓜。”

‘You see I think everything’s terrible anyhow,’ she went on in a convinced way. ‘Everybody thinks so—the most advanced people. And I KNOW. I’ve been everywhere and seen everything and done everything.’ Her eyes flashed around her in a defiant way, rather like Tom’s, and she laughed with thrilling scorn. ‘Sophisticated—God, I’m sophisticated!’
“你看，无论如何，我认为一切都很糟糕，”她以一种令人信服的方式继续说道。 ‘每个人都这么认为——最先进的人。而且我知道。 “我去过所有地方，见过所有事情，做过所有事情。”她的眼睛以一种挑衅的方式扫视着自己，很像汤姆的眼睛，她带着令人毛骨悚然的轻蔑笑了。 “老练——天哪，我老练了！”

The instant her voice broke off, ceasing to compel my attention, my belief, I felt the basic insincerity of what she had said. It made me uneasy, as though the whole evening had been a trick of some sort to exact a contributory emotion from me. I waited, and sure enough, in a moment she looked at me with an absolute smirk on her lovely face as if she had asserted her membership in a rather distinguished secret society to which she and Tom belonged.
当她的声音戛然而止，不再吸引我的注意力、我的信念时，我感觉到她所说的话根本不真诚。这让我感到不安，仿佛整个晚上都是某种骗局，目的是从我身上索取某种情感。我等待着，果然，过了一会儿，她可爱的脸上带着绝对的傻笑看着我，仿佛她已经宣称自己是她和汤姆所属的一个相当著名的秘密社团的成员。

Inside, the crimson room bloomed with light. Tom and Miss Baker sat at either end of the long couch and she read aloud to him from the ‘Saturday Evening Post’—the words, murmurous and uninflected, running together in a soothing tune. The lamp-light, bright on his boots and dull on the autumn-leaf yellow of her hair, glinted along the paper as she turned a page with a flutter of slender muscles in her arms.
里面，猩红的房间里绽放出光芒。汤姆和贝克小姐分别坐在长沙发的两端，她大声朗读《周六晚邮报》的文章给他听，这些文字低声细语，毫无语调，以一种舒缓的曲调连在一起。灯光在他的靴子上明亮，在她秋叶般的黄色头发上暗淡无光，当她翻动一页纸时，手臂上纤细的肌肉在颤动，灯光在纸上闪闪发光。

When we came in she held us silent for a moment with a lifted hand.
当我们进来时，她举起一只手，让我们安静了一会儿。

‘To be continued,’ she said, tossing the magazine on the table, ‘in our very next issue.’
“待续，”她说着，把杂志扔到桌子上，“在我们的下一期中。”

Her body asserted itself with a restless movement of her knee, and she stood up.
她的膝盖不安地运动着，她的身体站了起来。

‘Ten o’clock,’ she remarked, apparently finding the time on the ceiling. ‘Time for this good girl to go to bed.’
“十点钟，”她说道，显然是在天花板上找到了时间。 “这个好女孩该去睡觉了。”

‘Jordan’s going to play in the tournament tomorrow,’ explained Daisy, ‘over at Westchester.’
“乔丹明天将参加比赛，”黛西解释道，“在威彻斯特。”

‘Oh,—you’re JORdan Baker.’
“哦，你是乔丹·贝克。”

I knew now why her face was familiar—its pleasing contemptuous expression had looked out at me from many rotogravure pictures of the sporting life at Asheville and Hot Springs and Palm Beach. I had heard some story of her too, a critical, unpleasant story, but what it was I had forgotten long ago.
我现在知道为什么她的脸很熟悉了——在许多关于阿什维尔、温泉城和棕榈滩体育生活的凹版照片中，她那令人愉悦的轻蔑表情看着我。我也听说过一些关于她的故事，一个批评性的、令人不快的故事，但那是什么我早已忘记了。

‘Good night,’ she said softly. ‘Wake me at eight, won’t you.’
“晚安，”她轻声说。 “八点叫醒我，好吗？”

‘If you’ll get up.’
“如果你能起来的话。”

‘I will. Good night, Mr. Carraway. See you anon.’
'我会。晚安，卡拉威先生。一会儿见。

‘Of course you will,’ confirmed Daisy. ‘In fact I think I’ll arrange a marriage. Come over often, Nick, and I’ll sort of—oh—fling you together. You know—lock you up accidentally in linen closets and push you out to sea in a boat, and all that sort of thing——‘
“你当然会的，”黛西确认道。 “事实上，我想我会安排婚姻。尼克，经常过来，我会——哦——把你们带到一起。你知道的——不小心把你锁在亚麻壁橱里，然后用船把你推到海上，诸如此类的事情——”

‘Good night,’ called Miss Baker from the stairs. ‘I haven’t heard a word.’
“晚安，”贝克小姐在楼梯上喊道。 “我什么也没听到。”

‘She’s a nice girl,’ said Tom after a moment. ‘They oughtn’t to let her run around the country this way.’
“她是一个好女孩，”过了一会儿汤姆说道。 “他们不应该让她这样在全国各地奔跑。”

‘Who oughtn’t to?’ inquired Daisy coldly.
“谁不应该这样做？”黛西冷冷地问道。

‘Her family.’
'她的家人。'

‘Her family is one aunt about a thousand years old. Besides, Nick’s going to look after her, aren’t you, Nick? She’s going to spend lots of week-ends out here this summer. I think the home influence will be very good for her.’
‘她家有一位大约一千岁的阿姨。此外，尼克会照顾她，不是吗，尼克？今年夏天她将在这里度过很多周末。我认为家庭影响力对她来说非常有好处。

Daisy and Tom looked at each other for a moment in silence.
黛西和汤姆沉默地对视了一会儿。

‘Is she from New York?’ I asked quickly.
“她是纽约人吗？”我赶紧问道。

‘From Louisville. Our white girlhood was passed together there. Our beautiful white——‘
“来自路易斯维尔。”我们的白人少女时代就是在那里一起度过的。我们美丽的白色——’

‘Did you give Nick a little heart to heart talk on the veranda?’ demanded Tom suddenly.
“你在阳台上和尼克进行过一次心与心的交谈吗？”汤姆突然问道。

‘Did I?’ She looked at me. ‘I can’t seem to remember, but I think we talked about the Nordic race. Yes, I’m sure we did. It sort of crept up on us and first thing you know——‘
“是吗？”她看着我。 “我似乎不记得了，但我想我们谈论过北欧比赛。是的，我确信我们做到了。它悄然降临到我们身上，你知道的第一件事——”

‘Don’t believe everything you hear, Nick,’ he advised me.
“尼克，不要相信你听到的一切，”他建议我。

I said lightly that I had heard nothing at all, and a few minutes later I got up to go home. They came to the door with me and stood side by side in a cheerful square of light. As I started my motor Daisy peremptorily called ‘Wait!
我淡淡地说我什么也没听到，几分钟后我就起身回家了。他们和我一起来到门口，并排站在一片欢快的灯光下。当我启动我的马达时，黛西专横地叫道：“等等！”

‘I forgot to ask you something, and it’s important. We heard you were engaged to a girl out West.’
“我忘了问你一件事，这很重要。我们听说你和西部的一个女孩订婚了。

‘That’s right,’ corroborated Tom kindly. ‘We heard that you were engaged.’
“没错，”汤姆善意地证实道。 “我们听说你订婚了。”

‘It’s libel. I’m too poor.’
‘这是诽谤。我太穷了。

‘But we heard it,’ insisted Daisy, surprising me by opening up again in a flower-like way. ‘We heard it from three people so it must be true.’
“但我们听到了，”黛西坚持说，她以花朵般的方式再次开放，让我感到惊讶。 “我们从三个人那里听到了这件事，所以这一定是真的。”

Of course I knew what they were referring to, but I wasn’t even vaguely engaged. The fact that gossip had published the banns was one of the reasons I had come east. You can’t stop going with an old friend on account of rumors and on the other hand I had no intention of being rumored into marriage.
我当然知道他们指的是什么，但我什至没有含糊其辞。流言蜚语发布了禁令，这是我来到东部的原因之一。你不能因为谣言而不再和老朋友在一起，而另一方面，我也无意被传言结婚。

Their interest rather touched me and made them less remotely rich—nevertheless, I was confused and a little disgusted as I drove away. It seemed to me that the thing for Daisy to do was to rush out of the house, child in arms—but apparently there were no such intentions in her head. As for Tom, the fact that he ‘had some woman in New York’ was really less surprising than that he had been depressed by a book. Something was making him nibble at the edge of stale ideas as if his sturdy physical egotism no longer nourished his peremptory heart.
他们的兴趣相当触动了我，让他们变得不那么富有了——尽管如此，当我开车离开时，我感到困惑和有点厌恶。在我看来，黛西要做的就是抱着孩子冲出家门——但显然她脑子里并没有这样的意图。至于汤姆，他“在纽约有一个女人”这一事实并不比他因一本书而感到沮丧更令人惊讶。某种东西正在让他蚕食陈旧观念的边缘，仿佛他那坚固的身体自我主义不再滋养他专横的心。

Already it was deep summer on roadhouse roofs and in front of wayside garages, where new red gas-pumps sat out in pools of light, and when I reached my estate at West Egg I ran the car under its shed and sat for a while on an abandoned grass roller in the yard. The wind had blown off, leaving a loud bright night with wings beating in the trees and a persistent organ sound as the full bellows of the earth blew the frogs full of life. The silhouette of a moving cat wavered across the moonlight and turning my head to watch it I saw that I was not alone—fifty feet away a figure had emerged from the shadow of my neighbor’s mansion and was standing with his hands in his pockets regarding the silver pepper of the stars. Something in his leisurely movements and the secure position of his feet upon the lawn suggested that it was Mr. Gatsby himself, come out to determine what share was his of our local heavens.
路边小屋的屋顶和路边车库前已经是深夏了，新的红色加油泵停在光亮的池子里，当我到达西埃格的庄园时，我把车开到棚子下，在车上坐了一会儿。院子里有一个废弃的压草机。风已经停了，留下了一个响亮明亮的夜晚，翅膀在树上拍打，大地的轰鸣声吹响了青蛙的生命，持续不断的风琴声。一只移动的猫的剪影在月光下摇曳，我转过头看它，我发现我并不孤单——五十英尺外，一个人影从我邻居豪宅的阴影中出现，双手插在口袋里站着，看着那座房子。星星的银胡椒。从他悠闲的动作和他的脚在草坪上的稳固位置来看，盖茨比先生本人正在出来，来决定他在我们当地的天空中拥有什么份额。

I decided to call to him. Miss Baker had mentioned him at dinner, and that would do for an introduction. But I didn’t call to him for he gave a sudden intimation that he was content to be alone—he stretched out his arms toward the dark water in a curious way, and far as I was from him I could have sworn he was trembling. Involuntarily I glanced seaward—and distinguished nothing except a single green light, minute and far away, that might have been the end of a dock. When I looked once more for Gatsby he had vanished, and I was alone again in the unquiet darkness.
我决定给他打电话。贝克小姐在晚餐时提到过他，这样就可以作为介绍了。但我没有叫他，因为他突然暗示他很满足于一个人——他以一种奇怪的方式向黑暗的水面伸出双臂，尽管我离他很远，但我可以发誓他正在发抖。 。我不由自主地朝大海看了一眼——除了一盏绿灯，什么也看不见，那盏绿灯又小又远，那可能是码头的尽头。当我再次寻找盖茨比时，他已经消失了，我又独自一人在不安宁的黑暗中。

Chapter 2
第2章

About half way between West Egg and New York the motor-road hastily joins the railroad and runs beside it for a quarter of a mile, so as to shrink away from a certain desolate area of land. This is a valley of ashes—a fantastic farm where ashes grow like wheat into ridges and hills and grotesque gardens where ashes take the forms of houses and chimneys and rising smoke and finally, with a transcendent effort, of men who move dimly and already crumbling through the powdery air. Occasionally a line of grey cars crawls along an invisible track, gives out a ghastly creak and comes to rest, and immediately the ash-grey men swarm up with leaden spades and stir up an impenetrable cloud which screens their obscure operations from your sight.
大约在西埃格和纽约之间的半路上，汽车公路匆忙地与铁路汇合，并在铁路旁边延伸了四分之一英里，以便避开某个荒凉的土地。这是一个灰烬之谷——一个奇妙的农场，灰烬像小麦一样生长在山脊、山丘和怪诞的花园里，灰烬变成了房屋、烟囱和升起的烟雾，最后，在超凡的努力下，人们的行动模糊不清，已经摇摇欲坠。透过粉状的空气。偶尔，一排灰色汽车沿着一条看不见的轨道爬行，发出可怕的吱吱声，然后停下来，然后灰灰色的人立即拿着铅铲蜂拥而上，掀起一片难以穿透的云，将他们晦涩的操作从你的视线中屏蔽掉。

But above the grey land and the spasms of bleak dust which drift endlessly over it, you perceive, after a moment, the eyes of Doctor T. J. Eckleburg. The eyes of Doctor T. J. Eckleburg are blue and gigantic—their retinas are one yard high. They look out of no face but, instead, from a pair of enormous yellow spectacles which pass over a nonexistent nose. Evidently some wild wag of an oculist set them there to fatten his practice in the borough of Queens, and then sank down himself into eternal blindness or forgot them and moved away. But his eyes, dimmed a little by many paintless days under sun and rain, brood on over the solemn dumping ground.
但在灰色的土地和无休无止地飘过的荒凉尘埃之上，片刻之后，你会看到T·J·埃克尔伯格医生的眼睛。 T. J. Eckleburg 医生的眼睛是蓝色的、巨大的——他们的视网膜有一码高。他们不是从脸上看的，而是从一副巨大的黄色眼镜上看的，眼镜架在一个不存在的鼻子上。显然，一位眼科医生的疯狂举动将它们放在那里，以丰富他在皇后区的实践，然后让自己陷入永远的失明，或者忘记它们并搬走。但他的眼睛因日晒雨淋、没有画画的日子而变得有些暗淡，凝视着庄严的垃圾场。

The valley of ashes is bounded on one side by a small foul river, and when the drawbridge is up to let barges through, the passengers on waiting trains can stare at the dismal scene for as long as half an hour. There is always a halt there of at least a minute and it was because of this that I first met Tom Buchanan’s mistress.
灰烬谷的一侧是一条肮脏的小河，当吊桥升起让驳船通过时，等待火车上的乘客可以盯着这凄凉的场景长达半个小时。那里总是至少有一分钟的停留，正是因为这个，我第一次见到了汤姆·布坎南的情妇。

The fact that he had one was insisted upon wherever he was known. His acquaintances resented the fact that he turned up in popular restaurants with her and, leaving her at a table, sauntered about, chatting with whomsoever he knew. Though I was curious to see her I had no desire to meet her—but I did. I went up to New York with Tom on the train one afternoon and when we stopped by the ashheaps he jumped to his feet and taking hold of my elbow literally forced me from the car.
无论他在哪里，人们都坚持认为他拥有一个。他的熟人对他和她一起出现在受欢迎的餐馆，却把她留在一张桌子旁，四处闲逛，与他认识的任何人聊天这一事实感到不满。虽然我很好奇想见她，但我并不想见她——但我还是见了面。一天下午，我和汤姆一起坐火车去纽约，当我们停在灰堆旁时，他跳了起来，抓住我的胳膊肘，几乎把我从车里逼了下去。

‘We’re getting off!’ he insisted. ‘I want you to meet my girl.’
“我们要下车了！”他坚持道。 “我想让你见见我的女孩。”

I think he’d tanked up a good deal at luncheon and his determination to have my company bordered on violence. The supercilious assumption was that on Sunday afternoon I had nothing better to do.
我认为他在午餐时已经吃了很多苦头，他对我的陪伴的决心近乎暴力。傲慢的假设是周日下午我没有更好的事情可做。

I followed him over a low white-washed railroad fence and we walked back a hundred yards along the road under Doctor Eckleburg’s persistent stare. The only building in sight was a small block of yellow brick sitting on the edge of the waste land, a sort of compact Main Street ministering to it and contiguous to absolutely nothing. One of the three shops it contained was for rent and another was an all-night restaurant approached by a trail of ashes; the third was a garage—Repairs. GEORGE B. WILSON. Cars Bought and Sold—and I followed Tom inside.
我跟着他越过一道低矮的白色铁栅栏，在埃克尔伯格医生执着的注视下，我们沿着路往回走了一百码。视野中唯一的建筑是坐落在荒地边缘的一小块黄色砖块，是一条为它服务的紧凑的主街，但与任何东西都相邻。里面的三间店铺中，一间是出租的，另一间是一家通宵营业的餐厅，附近有一条灰烬痕迹。第三个是车库——修理。乔治·B·威尔逊。汽车买卖——我跟着汤姆进去了。

The interior was unprosperous and bare; the only car visible was the dust-covered wreck of a Ford which crouched in a dim corner. It had occurred to me that this shadow of a garage must be a blind and that sumptuous and romantic apartments were concealed overhead when the proprietor himself appeared in the door of an office, wiping his hands on a piece of waste. He was a blonde, spiritless man, anaemic, and faintly handsome. When he saw us a damp gleam of hope sprang into his light blue eyes.
里面破败不堪，空荡荡的。唯一可见的汽车是一辆福特汽车的残骸，它蹲在一个昏暗的角落里，满是灰尘。当老板亲自出现在办公室门口，用一块垃圾擦拭双手时，我突​​然想到车库的影子一定是百叶窗，豪华而浪漫的公寓就隐藏在头顶上。他是一个金发碧眼、毫无精神、贫血、略显英俊的男人。当他看到我们时，淡蓝色的眼睛里闪出了一丝潮湿的希望之光。

‘Hello, Wilson, old man,’ said Tom, slapping him jovially on the shoulder. ‘How’s business?’
“你好，威尔逊，老伙计，”汤姆说着，高兴地拍了拍他的肩膀。 '生意如何？'

‘I can’t complain,’ answered Wilson unconvincingly. ‘When are you going to sell me that car?’
“我不能抱怨，”威尔逊不令人信服地回答。 “你什么时候把那辆车卖给我？”

‘Next week; I’ve got my man working on it now.’
'下周;我现在已经让我的人在做这件事了。

‘Works pretty slow, don’t he?’
“工作很慢，不是吗？”

‘No, he doesn’t,’ said Tom coldly. ‘And if you feel that way about it, maybe I’d better sell it somewhere else after all.’
“不，他不知道，”汤姆冷冷地说。 “如果你有这样的感觉，也许我最好把它卖到别的地方。”

‘I don’t mean that,’ explained Wilson quickly. ‘I just meant——‘
“我不是那个意思。”威尔逊很快解释道。 “我只是想说——”

His voice faded off and Tom glanced impatiently around the garage. Then I heard footsteps on a stairs and in a moment the thickish figure of a woman blocked out the light from the office door. She was in the middle thirties, and faintly stout, but she carried her surplus flesh sensuously as some women can. Her face, above a spotted dress of dark blue crepe-de-chine, contained no facet or gleam of beauty but there was an immediately perceptible vitality about her as if the nerves of her body were continually smouldering. She smiled slowly and walking through her husband as if he were a ghost shook hands with Tom, looking him flush in the eye. Then she wet her lips and without turning around spoke to her husband in a soft, coarse voice:
他的声音渐渐消失，汤姆不耐烦地环视车库。然后我听到楼梯上有脚步声，不一会儿，一个女人粗壮的身影挡住了办公室门外的光线。她三十多岁，有点胖，但她像某些女人一样性感地保留着多余的肉。她的脸罩着一件深蓝色双绉斑点连衣裙，没有任何美感，也没有任何光芒，但她身上立刻就有一种明显的活力，仿佛她身体的神经在不断闷烧。她慢慢地微笑着，走过她的丈夫，就像他是一个幽灵一样，与汤姆握手，看着他红红的眼睛。然后她润湿了嘴唇，头也不回地用柔和而粗哑的声音对丈夫说道：

‘Get some chairs, why don’t you, so somebody can sit down.’
“拿些椅子来，为什么不呢，这样有人就可以坐下来。”

‘Oh, sure,’ agreed Wilson hurriedly and went toward the little office, mingling immediately with the cement color of the walls. A white ashen dust veiled his dark suit and his pale hair as it veiled everything in the vicinity—except his wife, who moved close to Tom.
“哦，当然，”威尔逊急忙同意，然后走向那间小办公室，立即与水泥墙的颜色融为一体。白色的灰烬遮住了他的深色西装和苍白的头发，也遮住了附近的一切——除了他的妻子，她靠近汤姆。

‘I want to see you,’ said Tom intently. ‘Get on the next train.’
“我想见你，”汤姆专心地说。 “乘下一班火车。”

‘All right.’
'好的。'

‘I’ll meet you by the news-stand on the lower level.’
“我会在楼下的报刊亭等你。”

She nodded and moved away from him just as George Wilson emerged with two chairs from his office door.
她点点头，从他身边走开，这时乔治·威尔逊拿着两把椅子从办公室门口出现。

We waited for her down the road and out of sight. It was a few days before the Fourth of July, and a grey, scrawny Italian child was setting torpedoes in a row along the railroad track.
我们在路边等她，直到看不见。距离国庆节还有几天，一个灰白、骨瘦如柴的意大利孩子正在铁轨上排成一排鱼雷。

‘Terrible place, isn’t it,’ said Tom, exchanging a frown with Doctor Eckleburg.
“真是个可怕的地方，不是吗？”汤姆说着，与埃克尔伯格医生皱起了眉头。

‘Awful.’
'可怕。'

‘It does her good to get away.’
“离开对她有好处。”

‘Doesn’t her husband object?’
“她丈夫不反对吗？”

‘Wilson? He thinks she goes to see her sister in New York. He’s so dumb he doesn’t know he’s alive.’
‘威尔逊？他认为她去纽约看她姐姐了。他太蠢了，都不知道自己还活着。”

So Tom Buchanan and his girl and I went up together to New York—or not quite together, for Mrs. Wilson sat discreetly in another car. Tom deferred that much to the sensibilities of those East Eggers who might be on the train.
于是汤姆·布坎南和他的女儿和我一起去了纽约——或者说不是一起去的，因为威尔逊夫人小心翼翼地坐在另一辆车里。汤姆在很大程度上尊重那些可能在火车上的东艾格人的感受。

She had changed her dress to a brown figured muslin which stretched tight over her rather wide hips as Tom helped her to the platform in New York. At the news-stand she bought a copy of ‘Town Tattle’ and a moving-picture magazine and, in the station drug store, some cold cream and a small flask of perfume. Upstairs, in the solemn echoing drive she let four taxi cabs drive away before she selected a new one, lavender-colored with grey upholstery, and in this we slid out from the mass of the station into the glowing sunshine. But immediately she turned sharply from the window and leaning forward tapped on the front glass.
当汤姆扶她登上纽约的站台时，她把裙子换成了一件棕色花纹平纹细布，这条裙子紧紧地遮住了她相当宽阔的臀部。她在报摊买了一本《小镇闲话》和一本电影杂志，还在车站药店买了一些冷霜和一小瓶香水。楼上，在庄严回响的车道上，她让四辆出租车开走了，然后她又选了一辆新的，淡紫色，灰色内饰，就这样，我们从车站的人群中滑了出来，进入了灿烂的阳光。但她立即猛地从窗户转过身来，向前倾身敲击前玻璃。

‘I want to get one of those dogs,’ she said earnestly. ‘I want to get one for the apartment. They’re nice to have—a dog.’
“我想养一只这样的狗，”她认真地说。 “我想为公寓买一件。拥有它们真是太好了——一只狗。

We backed up to a grey old man who bore an absurd resemblance to John D. Rockefeller. In a basket, swung from his neck, cowered a dozen very recent puppies of an indeterminate breed.
我们支持一位头发花白的老人，他与约翰·D·洛克菲勒有着惊人的相似之处。他脖子上挂着一个篮子，里面藏着十几只最近出生的小狗，品种不详。

‘What kind are they?’ asked Mrs. Wilson eagerly as he came to the taxi-window.
“它们是什么种类的？”当他来到出租车窗口时，威尔逊太太急切地问道。

‘All kinds. What kind do you want, lady?’
'所有种类。女士，您想要什么类型的？

‘I’d like to get one of those police dogs; I don’t suppose you got that kind?’
“我想要一只警犬；我想你不会是那种人吧？

The man peered doubtfully into the basket, plunged in his hand and drew one up, wriggling, by the back of the neck.
那人疑惑地朝篮子里看了一眼，伸手一伸，从后颈处抽出一只，蠕动着。

‘That’s no police dog,’ said Tom.
“那不是警犬，”汤姆说。

‘No, it’s not exactly a polICE dog,’ said the man with disappointment in his voice. ‘It’s more of an airedale.’ He passed his hand over the brown wash-rag of a back. ‘Look at that coat. Some coat. That’s a dog that’ll never bother you with catching cold.’
“不，这不完全是警犬。”男子语气失望地说。 “这更像是一只大狗。”他把手放在棕色的抹布背上。 ‘看看那件外套。一些外套。那是一只永远不会让你感冒的狗。

‘I think it’s cute,’ said Mrs. Wilson enthusiastically. ‘How much is it?’
“我觉得它很可爱，”威尔逊夫人热情地说。 '多少钱？'

‘That dog?’ He looked at it admiringly. ‘That dog will cost you ten dollars.’
“那只狗？”他钦佩地看着它。 “那条狗要花你十美元。”

The airedale—undoubtedly there was an airedale concerned in it somewhere though its feet were startlingly white—changed hands and settled down into Mrs. Wilson’s lap, where she fondled the weather-proof coat with rapture.
那只大羚羊——毫无疑问，它的某个地方确实有一只大羚羊，尽管它的脚白得惊人——换了手，落在威尔逊夫人的腿上，她欣喜若狂地抚摸着那件防风雨的外套。

‘Is it a boy or a girl?’ she asked delicately.
“是男孩还是女孩？”她小心翼翼地问道。

‘That dog? That dog’s a boy.’
‘那只狗？那只狗是个男孩。

‘It’s a bitch,’ said Tom decisively. ‘Here’s your money. Go and buy ten more dogs with it.’
“这是个婊子。”汤姆果断地说。 ‘这是你的钱。去再买十只狗吧。

We drove over to Fifth Avenue, so warm and soft, almost pastoral, on the summer Sunday afternoon that I wouldn’t have been surprised to see a great flock of white sheep turn the corner.
我们驱车前往第五大道，这里温暖而柔和，几乎充满田园气息，在夏日的周日下午，如果我看到一大群白色的羊从拐角处转过，我不会感到惊讶。

‘Hold on,’ I said, ‘I have to leave you here.’
“等一下，”我说，“我得把你留在这里了。”

‘No, you don’t,’ interposed Tom quickly. ‘Myrtle’ll be hurt if you don’t come up to the apartment. Won’t you, Myrtle?’
“不，你不知道，”汤姆很快插话道。 “如果你不来公寓，默特尔就会受伤。”你愿意吗，默特尔？

‘Come on,’ she urged. ‘I’ll telephone my sister Catherine. She’s said to be very beautiful by people who ought to know.’
“来吧，”她催促道。 “我会给我姐姐凯瑟琳打电话。应该知道的人都说她非常美丽。

‘Well, I’d like to, but——‘
“嗯，我愿意，但是——”

We went on, cutting back again over the Park toward the West Hundreds. At 158th Street the cab stopped at one slice in a long white cake of apartment houses. Throwing a regal homecoming glance around the neighborhood, Mrs. Wilson gathered up her dog and her other purchases and went haughtily in.
我们继续前行，再次绕道穿过公园，朝西百街前进。到了 158 街，出租车停在一片长长的白色公寓楼的一片区域。威尔逊夫人以富丽堂皇的归家风范环视了附近一圈，然后收拾起她的狗和她买的其他东西，傲慢地走了进去。

‘I’m going to have the McKees come up,’ she announced as we rose in the elevator. ‘And of course I got to call up my sister, too.’
“我要叫麦基一家上来，”当我们乘电梯上升时，她宣布。 “当然，我也得给我姐姐打电话。”

The apartment was on the top floor—a small living room, a small dining room, a small bedroom and a bath. The living room was crowded to the doors with a set of tapestried furniture entirely too large for it so that to move about was to stumble continually over scenes of ladies swinging in the gardens of Versailles. The only picture was an over-enlarged photograph, apparently a hen sitting on a blurred rock. Looked at from a distance however the hen resolved itself into a bonnet and the countenance of a stout old lady beamed down into the room. Several old copies of ‘Town Tattle ‘lay on the table together with a copy of ‘Simon Called Peter’ and some of the small scandal magazines of Broadway. Mrs. Wilson was first concerned with the dog. A reluctant elevator boy went for a box full of straw and some milk to which he added on his own initiative a tin of large hard dog biscuits—one of which decomposed apathetically in the saucer of milk all afternoon. Meanwhile Tom brought out a bottle of whiskey from a locked bureau door.
公寓位于顶楼，有一个小客厅、一个小餐厅、一个小卧室和一个浴室。客厅里的门边挤满了一套挂毯家具，它们太大了，以至于走动就会不断地被凡尔赛花园里的女士们荡秋千的场景绊倒。唯一的照片是一张过度放大的照片，显然是一只母鸡坐在一块模糊的岩石上。然而，从远处看，母鸡变成了一顶帽子，一位粗壮的老太太的面容微笑地照进了房间。桌子上放着几本旧的《小镇闲话》，还有一本《叫彼得的西蒙》和一些百老汇的小丑闻杂志。威尔逊夫人首先关心的是那只狗。一个不情愿的电梯男孩去拿了一个装满稻草和一些牛奶的盒子，他主动在其中添加了一罐大硬狗饼干——其中一个整个下午都在牛奶碟中冷漠地分解了。与此同时，汤姆从锁着的柜门里拿出一瓶威士忌。

I have been drunk just twice in my life and the second time was that afternoon so everything that happened has a dim hazy cast over it although until after eight o’clock the apartment was full of cheerful sun. Sitting on Tom’s lap Mrs. Wilson called up several people on the telephone; then there were no cigarettes and I went out to buy some at the drug store on the corner. When I came back they had disappeared so I sat down discreetly in the living room and read a chapter of ‘Simon Called Peter’—either it was terrible stuff or the whiskey distorted things because it didn’t make any sense to me.
我这辈子只喝醉过两次，第二次是那天下午，所以发生的一切都笼罩在一层朦胧的雾气中，尽管直到八点以后，公寓里都充满了欢快的阳光。威尔逊夫人坐在汤姆的腿上，给几个人打电话。然后没有香烟了，我就出去到街角的药店买了一些。当我回来时，它们已经消失了，所以我小心翼翼地坐在客厅里，读了《西蒙叫彼得》的一章——要么是可怕的东西，要么是威士忌扭曲了事情，因为它对我来说没有任何意义。

Just as Tom and Myrtle—after the first drink Mrs. Wilson and I called each other by our first names—reappeared, company commenced to arrive at the apartment door.
当汤姆和默特尔——在喝完第一杯酒之后，威尔逊夫人和我互相直呼其名——再次出现时，人们开始到达公寓门口。

The sister, Catherine, was a slender, worldly girl of about thirty with a solid sticky bob of red hair and a complexion powdered milky white. Her eyebrows had been plucked and then drawn on again at a more rakish angle but the efforts of nature toward the restoration of the old alignment gave a blurred air to her face. When she moved about there was an incessant clicking as innumerable pottery bracelets jingled up and down upon her arms. She came in with such a proprietary haste and looked around so possessively at the furniture that I wondered if she lived here. But when I asked her she laughed immoderately, repeated my question aloud and told me she lived with a girl friend at a hotel.
妹妹凯瑟琳是个身材苗条、世俗的女孩，三十岁左右，一头浓密的红色短发，肤色呈乳白色。她的眉毛被拔掉了，然后又以更潇洒的角度画了出来，但大自然为恢复原来的排列所做的努力给她的脸带来了一种模糊的感觉。当她走动时，无数陶器手镯在她手臂上上下叮当作响，发出不断的咔哒声。她带着一种特有的匆忙进来，充满占有欲地环顾四周的家具，我怀疑她是否住在这里。但当我问她时，她放肆地笑了，大声重复了我的问题，并告诉我她和一个女朋友住在一家酒店。

Mr. McKee was a pale feminine man from the flat below. He had just shaved for there was a white spot of lather on his cheekbone and he was most respectful in his greeting to everyone in the room. He informed me that he was in the ‘artistic game’ and I gathered later that he was a photographer and had made the dim enlargement of Mrs. Wilson’s mother which hovered like an ectoplasm on the wall. His wife was shrill, languid, handsome and horrible. She told me with pride that her husband had photographed her a hundred and twenty-seven times since they had been married.
麦基先生是来自楼下公寓的一位面色苍白的女性男子。他刚刚刮过胡子，因为他的颧骨上有一块白色的泡沫，他对房间里的每个人都非常尊重地打招呼。他告诉我，他正在玩“艺术游戏”，后来我才知道，他是一名摄影师，把威尔逊夫人的母亲放大了，像灵质一样悬浮在墙上。他的妻子声音尖细，无精打采，英俊却可怕。她自豪地告诉我，自从他们结婚以来，她的丈夫已经给她拍了一百二十七次照片。

Mrs. Wilson had changed her costume some time before and was now attired in an elaborate afternoon dress of cream colored chiffon, which gave out a continual rustle as she swept about the room. With the influence of the dress her personality had also undergone a change. The intense vitality that had been so remarkable in the garage was converted into impressive hauteur. Her laughter, her gestures, her assertions became more violently affected moment by moment and as she expanded the room grew smaller around her until she seemed to be revolving on a noisy, creaking pivot through the smoky air.
威尔逊夫人不久前换过服装，现在穿着一件精致的奶油色雪纺午后连衣裙，当她在房间里走来走去时，裙子发出持续的沙沙声。受到这件衣服的影响，她的性格也发生了变化。车库里原本非凡的旺盛活力，现在变成了令人印象深刻的傲慢。她的笑声、她的手势、她的主张每时每刻都变得更加猛烈，随着她的扩张，她周围的房间变得越来越小，直到她似乎在烟雾弥漫的空气中绕着一个嘈杂、吱吱作响的枢轴旋转。

‘My dear,’ she told her sister in a high mincing shout, ‘most of these fellas will cheat you every time. All they think of is money. I had a woman up here last week to look at my feet and when she gave me the bill you’d of thought she had my appendicitus out.’
“亲爱的，”她装腔作势地大声喊道，“这些家伙大多数每次都会欺骗你。”他们只想到钱。上周有一位女士来我这里检查我的脚，当她给我账单时，你会以为她把我的阑尾切除了。

‘What was the name of the woman?’ asked Mrs. McKee.
“那个女人叫什么名字？”麦基夫人问道。

‘Mrs. Eberhardt. She goes around looking at people’s feet in their own homes.’
'太太。艾伯哈特。她到处观察人们在自己家中的脚部。

‘I like your dress,’ remarked Mrs. McKee, ‘I think it’s adorable.’
“我喜欢你的衣服，”麦基夫人说，“我觉得它很可爱。”

Mrs. Wilson rejected the compliment by raising her eyebrow in disdain.
威尔逊夫人轻蔑地扬起了眉毛，拒绝了这种恭维。

‘It’s just a crazy old thing,’ she said. ‘I just slip it on sometimes when I don’t care what I look like.’
“这只是一个疯狂的老东西，”她说。 “有时当我不在乎自己的样子时，我就会戴上它。”

‘But it looks wonderful on you, if you know what I mean,’ pursued Mrs. McKee. ‘If Chester could only get you in that pose I think he could make something of it.’
“但如果你明白我的意思的话，你穿上它看起来很棒，”麦基夫人继续说道。 “如果切斯特能让你摆出那样的姿势，我想他就能有所作为。”

We all looked in silence at Mrs. Wilson who removed a strand of hair from over her eyes and looked back at us with a brilliant smile. Mr. McKee regarded her intently with his head on one side and then moved his hand back and forth slowly in front of his face.
我们都默默地看着威尔逊夫人，她拨开遮住眼睛的一缕头发，带着灿烂的微笑回头看着我们。麦基先生侧着头专注地看着她，然后把手在脸前慢慢地来回移动。

‘I should change the light,’ he said after a moment. ‘I’d like to bring out the modelling of the features. And I’d try to get hold of all the back hair.’
“我应该换一下灯，”过了一会儿他说道。 “我想展示特征的建模。我会尽力抓住所有后背的头发。

‘I wouldn’t think of changing the light,’ cried Mrs. McKee. ‘I think it’s——‘
“我不会考虑改变灯光，”麦基夫人喊道。 '我想这是 - '

Her husband said ‘SH!’ and we all looked at the subject again whereupon Tom Buchanan yawned audibly and got to his feet.
她丈夫说“嘘！”我们都再次看着这个话题，汤姆·布坎南打了个哈欠，站了起来。

‘You McKees have something to drink,’ he said. ‘Get some more ice and mineral water, Myrtle, before everybody goes to sleep.’
“你们麦基有东西喝，”他说。 “默特尔，在大家睡觉之前，再拿点冰块和矿泉水。”

‘I told that boy about the ice.’ Myrtle raised her eyebrows in despair at the shiftlessness of the lower orders. ‘These people! You have to keep after them all the time.’
“我跟那个男孩说了冰的事。”默特尔对下层的无能感到绝望，扬起了眉毛。 '这些人！你必须一直跟踪他们。

She looked at me and laughed pointlessly. Then she flounced over to the dog, kissed it with ecstasy and swept into the kitchen, implying that a dozen chefs awaited her orders there.
她看着我，毫无意义地笑了。然后她扑向狗，欣喜若狂地吻了它，然后快步走进厨房，暗示有十几名厨师在那里等待她的命令。

‘I’ve done some nice things out on Long Island,’ asserted Mr. McKee.
“我在长岛做了一些好事，”麦基先生断言。

Tom looked at him blankly.
汤姆茫然地看着他。

‘Two of them we have framed downstairs.’
“其中两幅我们已经在楼下装裱起来了。”

‘Two what?’ demanded Tom.
“两个什么？”汤姆问道。

‘Two studies. One of them I call ‘Montauk Point—the Gulls,’ and the other I call ‘Montauk Point—the Sea.’ ‘
'两项研究。其中一个我称之为“蒙托克角——海鸥”，另一个我称之为“蒙托克角——大海”。

The sister Catherine sat down beside me on the couch.
凯瑟琳修女坐在我旁边的沙发上。

‘Do you live down on Long Island, too?’ she inquired.
“你也住在长岛吗？”她问道。

‘I live at West Egg.’
“我住在西埃格。”

‘Really? I was down there at a party about a month ago. At a man named Gatsby’s. Do you know him?’
'真的吗？大约一个月前，我参加了一个聚会。一个叫盖茨比的男人。你认识他吗？'

‘I live next door to him.’
“我住在他隔壁。”

‘Well, they say he’s a nephew or a cousin of Kaiser Wilhelm’s. That’s where all his money comes from.’
“嗯，他们说他是威廉皇帝的侄子或表弟。这就是他所有的钱的来源。

‘Really?’
'真的吗？'

She nodded.
她点点头。

‘I’m scared of him. I’d hate to have him get anything on me.’
‘我害怕他。我不想让他在我身上得到任何东西。

This absorbing information about my neighbor was interrupted by Mrs. McKee’s pointing suddenly at Catherine:
关于我邻居的有趣信息被麦基夫人突然指着凯瑟琳打断了：

‘Chester, I think you could do something with HER,’ she broke out, but Mr. McKee only nodded in a bored way and turned his attention to Tom.
“切斯特，我想你可以对她做点什么，”她突然说道，但麦基先生只是无聊地点点头，然后把注意力转向汤姆。

‘I’d like to do more work on Long Island if I could get the entry. All I ask is that they should give me a start.’
“如果我能获得参赛资格，我想在长岛做更多的工作。我所要求的只是他们应该给我一个开始。

‘Ask Myrtle,’ said Tom, breaking into a short shout of laughter as Mrs. Wilson entered with a tray. ‘She’ll give you a letter of introduction, won’t you, Myrtle?’
“去问默特尔吧。”威尔逊夫人端着托盘进来时，汤姆突然大笑起来。 “她会给你一封介绍信，不是吗，默特尔？”

‘Do what?’ she asked, startled.
“做什么？”她惊讶地问道。

‘You’ll give McKee a letter of introduction to your husband, so he can do some studies of him.’ His lips moved silently for a moment as he invented. ‘ ‘George B. Wilson at the Gasoline Pump,’ or something like that.’
“你会给麦基一封介绍信给你的丈夫，这样他就可以对他进行一些研究。”他的嘴唇默默地动了一会儿，说道。 “‘乔治·B·威尔逊在汽油泵旁’，或者类似的话。”

Catherine leaned close to me and whispered in my ear: ‘Neither of them can stand the person they’re married to.’
凯瑟琳靠近我，在我耳边低声说道：“他们都无法忍受自己的结婚对象。”

‘Can’t they?’
“他们不能吗？”

‘Can’t STAND them.’ She looked at Myrtle and then at Tom. ‘What I say is, why go on living with them if they can’t stand them? If I was them I’d get a divorce and get married to each other right away.’
“受不了他们。”她看看默特尔，又看看汤姆。 ‘我想说的是，如果他们无法忍受，为什么还要继续和他们住在一起呢？如果我是他们，我会离婚并立即结婚。

‘Doesn’t she like Wilson either?’
“她也不喜欢威尔逊吗？”

The answer to this was unexpected. It came from Myrtle who had overheard the question and it was violent and obscene.
这个答案出乎意料。这是默特尔无意中听到的问题，内容充满暴力和淫秽。

‘You see?’ cried Catherine triumphantly. She lowered her voice again. ‘It’s really his wife that’s keeping them apart. She’s a Catholic and they don’t believe in divorce.’
“你明白了吗？”凯瑟琳得意地喊道。她又压低了声音。 “实际上是他的妻子让他们分开了。她是天主教徒，他们不相信离婚。”

Daisy was not a Catholic and I was a little shocked at the elaborateness of the lie.
黛西不是天主教徒，我对这个谎言的精心设计感到有点震惊。

‘When they do get married,’ continued Catherine, ‘they’re going west to live for a while until it blows over.’
“当他们结婚时，”凯瑟琳继续说道，“他们会去西部生活一段时间，直到风平浪静。”

‘It’d be more discreet to go to Europe.’
“去欧洲会更谨慎。”

‘Oh, do you like Europe?’ she exclaimed surprisingly. ‘I just got back from Monte Carlo.’
“哦，你喜欢欧洲吗？”她惊讶地喊道。 “我刚从蒙特卡洛回来。”

‘Really.’
'真的。'

‘Just last year. I went over there with another girl.’
就在去年。我和另一个女孩一起去了那里。

‘Stay long?’
'久留？'

‘No, we just went to Monte Carlo and back. We went by way of Marseilles. We had over twelve hundred dollars when we started but we got gypped out of it all in two days in the private rooms. We had an awful time getting back, I can tell you. God, how I hated that town!’
“不，我们只是去了蒙特卡洛然后回来了。我们途经马赛。刚开始的时候我们有一千二百多美元，但两天后我们在私人房间里就被骗光了。我可以告诉你，我们回来的时候过得很糟糕。天哪，我多么讨厌那个小镇啊！

The late afternoon sky bloomed in the window for a moment like the blue honey of the Mediterranean—then the shrill voice of Mrs. McKee called me back into the room.
午后的天空在窗外绽放了一会儿，就像地中海的蓝色蜂蜜一样——然后麦基夫人尖利的声音把我叫回了房间。

‘I almost made a mistake, too,’ she declared vigorously. ‘I almost married a little kyke who’d been after me for years. I knew he was below me. Everybody kept saying to me: ‘Lucille, that man’s way below you!’ But if I hadn’t met Chester, he’d of got me sure.’
“我也差点犯了一个错误，”她有力地宣称。 “我差点就嫁给了一个跟踪我多年的小混蛋。我知道他在我之下。每个人都一直对我说：“露西尔，那个人比你低得多！”但如果我没有遇到切斯特，他肯定会明白我的意思。

‘Yes, but listen,’ said Myrtle Wilson, nodding her head up and down, ‘at least you didn’t marry him.’
“是的，但是听着，”默特尔·威尔逊上下点头说，“至少你没有嫁给他。”

‘I know I didn’t.’
“我知道我没有。”

‘Well, I married him,’ said Myrtle, ambiguously. ‘And that’s the difference between your case and mine.’
“好吧，我嫁给了他，”默特尔含糊地说。 “这就是你的案子和我的案子的区别。”

‘Why did you, Myrtle?’ demanded Catherine. ‘Nobody forced you to.’
“你为什么这么做，默特尔？”凯瑟琳问道。 “没有人强迫你这么做。”

Myrtle considered.
默特尔想了想。

‘I married him because I thought he was a gentleman,’ she said finally. ‘I thought he knew something about breeding, but he wasn’t fit to lick my shoe.’
“我嫁给他是因为我认为他是一位绅士，”她最后说道。 “我以为他懂得一些育种知识，但他不适合舔我的鞋子。”

‘You were crazy about him for a while,’ said Catherine.
“你有一段时间为他疯狂，”凯瑟琳说。

‘Crazy about him!’ cried Myrtle incredulously. ‘Who said I was crazy about him? I never was any more crazy about him than I was about that man there.’
“为他疯狂！”默特尔难以置信地喊道。 ‘谁说我为他着迷？我对他的迷恋从来没有比对那里的那个人更疯狂的了。”

She pointed suddenly at me, and every one looked at me accusingly. I tried to show by my expression that I had played no part in her past.
她突然指着我，每个人都用指责的目光看着我。我试图通过我的表情表明我在她的过去中没有扮演任何角色。

‘The only CRAZY I was was when I married him. I knew right away I made a mistake. He borrowed somebody’s best suit to get married in and never even told me about it, and the man came after it one day when he was out. She looked around to see who was listening: ‘ ‘Oh, is that your suit?’ I said. ‘This is the first I ever heard about it.’ But I gave it to him and then I lay down and cried to beat the band all afternoon.’
“我唯一疯狂的一次是当我嫁给他的时候。我立刻就知道我犯了一个错误。他借了别人最好的一套衣服去结婚，却从来没有告诉过我这件事，有一天他出去的时候，那个男人来找他了。她环顾四周，看看谁在听：“‘哦，那是你的衣服吗？’我说。” “这是我第一次听说这件事。”但我把它给了他，然后我躺下来哭了一整个下午。

‘She really ought to get away from him,’ resumed Catherine to me. ‘They’ve been living over that garage for eleven years. And Tom’s the first sweetie she ever had.’
“她真的应该离开他，”凯瑟琳对我说道。 “他们在那个车库里住了十一年了。汤姆是她的第一个宝贝。

The bottle of whiskey—a second one—was now in constant demand by all present, excepting Catherine who ‘felt just as good on nothing at all.’ Tom rang for the janitor and sent him for some celebrated sandwiches, which were a complete supper in themselves. I wanted to get out and walk eastward toward the park through the soft twilight but each time I tried to go I became entangled in some wild strident argument which pulled me back, as if with ropes, into my chair. Yet high over the city our line of yellow windows must have contributed their share of human secrecy to the casual watcher in the darkening streets, and I was him too, looking up and wondering. I was within and without, simultaneously enchanted and repelled by the inexhaustible variety of life.
现在，在场所有人都不断要求那瓶威士忌——第二瓶——除了凯瑟琳，她“什么都不喝也感觉很好。”汤姆按铃叫来了看门人，让他拿了一些著名的三明治，这就是一顿完整的晚餐。在他们自己身上。我想出去，在柔和的暮色中向东走到公园，但每次我试图走时，我都会陷入一些激烈的争吵中，这些争吵就像用绳子一样把我拉回椅子上。然而，在城市的高处，我们那一排黄色的窗户一定为黑暗街道上漫不经心的观察者贡献了自己的一份人类秘密，而我也是他，抬起头来，疑惑着。我既在里面又在外面，对生活的无穷无尽的多样性既着迷又排斥。

Myrtle pulled her chair close to mine, and suddenly her warm breath poured over me the story of her first meeting with Tom.
默特尔把椅子拉到我的身边，突然，她温暖的呼吸向我讲述了她第一次见到汤姆的故事。

‘It was on the two little seats facing each other that are always the last ones left on the train. I was going up to New York to see my sister and spend the night. He had on a dress suit and patent leather shoes and I couldn’t keep my eyes off him but every time he looked at me I had to pretend to be looking at the advertisement over his head. When we came into the station he was next to me and his white shirt-front pressed against my arm—and so I told him I’d have to call a policeman, but he knew I lied. I was so excited that when I got into a taxi with him I didn’t hardly know I wasn’t getting into a subway train. All I kept thinking about, over and over, was ‘You can’t live forever, you can’t live forever.’ ‘
“火车上最后剩下的两个小座位总是面对面的。我要去纽约看我姐姐并过夜。他穿着西装和漆皮鞋，我的目光无法从他身上移开，但每次他看着我时，我都必须假装在看他头顶上的广告。当我们进入车站时，他就在我旁边，他的白衬衫前襟压在我的手臂上，所以我告诉他我必须打电话给警察，但他知道我撒了谎。我非常兴奋，以至于当我和他一起坐上出租车时，我几乎不知道自己不是在坐地铁。我一遍又一遍地想的是“你不能永远活着，你不能永远活着。”

She turned to Mrs. McKee and the room rang full of her artificial laughter.
她转向麦基夫人，房间里充满了她假装的笑声。

‘My dear,’ she cried, ‘I’m going to give you this dress as soon as I’m through with it. I’ve got to get another one tomorrow. I’m going to make a list of all the things I’ve got to get. A massage and a wave and a collar for the dog and one of those cute little ash-trays where you touch a spring, and a wreath with a black silk bow for mother’s grave that’ll last all summer. I got to write down a list so I won’t forget all the things I got to do.’
“亲爱的，”她喊道，“我一穿完这件衣服就送给你。”我明天必须再去买一份。我将列出我必须得到的所有东西。为狗做一次按摩、挥手、戴上项圈，还有一个可爱的小烟灰缸，你可以在其中触摸弹簧，还有一个带有黑色丝质蝴蝶结的花环，供母亲的坟墓使用，可以持续整个夏天。我必须写下一个清单，这样我就不会忘记我要做的所有事情。

It was nine o’clock—almost immediately afterward I looked at my watch and found it was ten. Mr. McKee was asleep on a chair with his fists clenched in his lap, like a photograph of a man of action. Taking out my handkerchief I wiped from his cheek the remains of the spot of dried lather that had worried me all the afternoon.
现在是九点钟——几乎是在我看了手表之后，发现已经十点了。麦基先生坐在椅子上睡着了，放在腿上的拳头紧握，就像一张实干家的照片。我拿出手帕擦去他脸颊上残留的干泡沫，这个泡沫让我整个下午都担心不已。

The little dog was sitting on the table looking with blind eyes through the smoke and from time to time groaning faintly. People disappeared, reappeared, made plans to go somewhere, and then lost each other, searched for each other, found each other a few feet away. Some time toward midnight Tom Buchanan and Mrs. Wilson stood face to face discussing in impassioned voices whether Mrs. Wilson had any right to mention Daisy’s name.
小狗坐在桌子上，睁着眼睛透过烟雾看着，不时发出微弱的呻吟声。人们消失了，又出现了，计划去某个地方，然后彼此迷失，互相寻找，在几英尺远的地方找到了彼此。午夜时分，汤姆·布坎南和威尔逊夫人面对面站着，热情地讨论威尔逊夫人是否有权提及黛西的名字。

‘Daisy! Daisy! Daisy!’ shouted Mrs. Wilson. ‘I’ll say it whenever I want to! Daisy! Dai——‘
'雏菊！雏菊！黛西！”威尔逊夫人喊道。 ‘我想说什么时候就说什么！雏菊！戴——‘

Making a short deft movement Tom Buchanan broke her nose with his open hand.
汤姆·布坎南敏捷地做了一个简短的动作，用张开的手打断了她的鼻子。

Then there were bloody towels upon the bathroom floor, and women’s voices scolding, and high over the confusion a long broken wail of pain. Mr. McKee awoke from his doze and started in a daze toward the door. When he had gone half way he turned around and stared at the scene—his wife and Catherine scolding and consoling as they stumbled here and there among the crowded furniture with articles of aid, and the despairing figure on the couch bleeding fluently and trying to spread a copy of ‘Town Tattle’ over the tapestry scenes of Versailles. Then Mr. McKee turned and continued on out the door. Taking my hat from the chandelier I followed.
然后浴室地板上满是血迹斑斑的毛巾，女人的责骂声，混乱中传来长长的断断续续的痛苦哀号。麦基先生从睡梦中醒来，茫然地朝门口走去。当他走了一半时，他转过身来，凝视着这一幕——他的妻子和凯瑟琳一边责骂一边安慰，他们在拥挤的家具中跌跌撞撞地走来走去，手里拿着救援物品，沙发上绝望的身影流着血，试图蔓延开来。凡尔赛宫挂毯场景的《小镇闲谈》的副本。然后麦基先生转身继续往门外走。我从枝形吊灯上取下帽子，跟着走。

‘Come to lunch some day,’ he suggested, as we groaned down in the elevator.
“改天来吃午饭吧，”当我们在电梯里呻吟着时，他建议道。

‘Where?’
'在哪里？'

‘Anywhere.’
“任何地方。”

‘Keep your hands off the lever,’ snapped the elevator boy.
“把手远离操纵杆，”电梯男孩厉声说道。

‘I beg your pardon,’ said Mr. McKee with dignity, ‘I didn’t know I was touching it.’
“请原谅，”麦基先生尊严地说，“我不知道我在碰它。”

‘All right,’ I agreed, ‘I’ll be glad to.’
“好吧，”我同意道，“我很乐意。”

… I was standing beside his bed and he was sitting up between the sheets, clad in his underwear, with a great portfolio in his hands.
......我站在他的床边，他坐在床单之间，穿着内衣，手里拿着一个很大的文件夹。

‘Beauty and the Beast … Loneliness … Old Grocery Horse … Brook’n Bridge ….’
“美女与野兽……孤独……老杂货马……布鲁克桥……”

Then I was lying half asleep in the cold lower level of the Pennsylvania Station, staring at the morning ‘Tribune’ and waiting for the four o’clock train.
然后我半睡半醒地躺在宾夕法尼亚车站寒冷的下层，盯着早上的《论坛报》，等待四点钟的火车。

Chapter 3
第3章

There was music from my neighbor’s house through the summer nights. In his blue gardens men and girls came and went like moths among the whisperings and the champagne and the stars. At high tide in the afternoon I watched his guests diving from the tower of his raft or taking the sun on the hot sand of his beach while his two motor-boats slit the waters of the Sound, drawing aquaplanes over cataracts of foam. On week-ends his Rolls-Royce became an omnibus, bearing parties to and from the city, between nine in the morning and long past midnight, while his station wagon scampered like a brisk yellow bug to meet all trains. And on Mondays eight servants including an extra gardener toiled all day with mops and scrubbing-brushes and hammers and garden-shears, repairing the ravages of the night before.
整个夏天的夜晚，我邻居家都会传来音乐声。在他蓝色的花园里，男人和女孩们像飞蛾一样在窃窃私语、香槟和星星中来来去去。下午涨潮时，我看着他的客人从他的木筏塔上跳水，或者在他海滩的热沙上晒太阳，而他的两艘摩托艇则划过海峡的水域，在泡沫瀑布上划着滑水板。到了周末，他的劳斯莱斯就变成了一辆公共汽车，从早上九点到午夜过后，载着各种派对往返于城市之间，而他的旅行车则像一只活泼的黄色小虫一样蹦蹦跳跳地迎接所有火车。每逢星期一，八名仆人，包括一名额外的园丁，会用拖把、刷子、锤子和园艺剪辛苦工作一整天，修复前一天晚上造成的破坏。

Every Friday five crates of oranges and lemons arrived from a fruiterer in New York—every Monday these same oranges and lemons left his back door in a pyramid of pulpless halves. There was a machine in the kitchen which could extract the juice of two hundred oranges in half an hour, if a little button was pressed two hundred times by a butler’s thumb.
每个星期五，纽约的一位果农都会送来五箱橙子和柠檬，而每周一，这些橙子和柠檬都会以无果肉的金字塔形式从他的后门运出。厨房里有一台机器，只要管家的拇指按一个小按钮两百次，半小时内就能榨出两百个橙子的汁。

At least once a fortnight a corps of caterers came down with several hundred feet of canvas and enough colored lights to make a Christmas tree of Gatsby’s enormous garden. On buffet tables, garnished with glistening hors-d’oeuvre, spiced baked hams crowded against salads of harlequin designs and pastry pigs and turkeys bewitched to a dark gold. In the main hall a bar with a real brass rail was set up, and stocked with gins and liquors and with cordials so long forgotten that most of his female guests were too young to know one from another.
至少每两周一次，一群餐饮承办商会带着数百英尺的帆布和足够的彩灯下来，在盖茨比的巨大花园里制作一棵圣诞树。自助餐桌上摆满了闪闪发光的开胃小菜，五香烤火腿挤满了丑角图案的沙拉，还有被暗金色迷住的猪和火鸡糕点。大厅里设立了一个带有真正黄铜栏杆的酒吧，里面摆满了杜松子酒、烈酒以及早已被遗忘的甜酒，以至于他的大多数女客人都太年轻了，无法相互认识。

By seven o’clock the orchestra has arrived—no thin five-piece affair but a whole pitful of oboes and trombones and saxophones and viols and cornets and piccolos and low and high drums. The last swimmers have come in from the beach now and are dressing upstairs; the cars from New York are parked five deep in the drive, and already the halls and salons and verandas are gaudy with primary colors and hair shorn in strange new ways and shawls beyond the dreams of Castile. The bar is in full swing and floating rounds of cocktails permeate the garden outside until the air is alive with chatter and laughter and casual innuendo and introductions forgotten on the spot and enthusiastic meetings between women who never knew each other’s names.
到了七点，管弦乐队就到了——不是单薄的五人组，而是一大堆双簧管、长号、萨克斯管、中提琴、短号、短笛、低音鼓和高音鼓。最后一批游泳者现在已经从海滩回来，正在楼上穿衣服。来自纽约的汽车在车道上停了五层深，大厅、沙龙和阳台已经用原色装饰得花哨，头发以奇怪的新方式剪了，披肩也超出了卡斯蒂利亚的梦想。酒吧里热闹非凡，一轮又一轮的鸡尾酒弥漫在外面的花园里，直到空气中充满了闲聊和笑声，随意的暗示和当场忘记的介绍，以及互不相识的女性之间的热情会面。

The lights grow brighter as the earth lurches away from the sun and now the orchestra is playing yellow cocktail music and the opera of voices pitches a key higher. Laughter is easier, minute by minute, spilled with prodigality, tipped out at a cheerful word. The groups change more swiftly, swell with new arrivals, dissolve and form in the same breath—already there are wanderers, confident girls who weave here and there among the stouter and more stable, become for a sharp, joyous moment the center of a group and then excited with triumph glide on through the sea-change of faces and voices and color under the constantly changing light.
随着地球逐渐远离太阳，灯光变得越来越亮，现在管弦乐队正在演奏黄色的鸡尾酒音乐，人声歌剧的音调更高。笑声变得更容易了，一分钟一分钟，充满了挥霍，在一个欢快的话语中爆发出来。这些群体变化得更快，随着新来的人而膨胀，同时解散和形成——已经有流浪者、自信的女孩在更坚固、更稳定的人群中到处穿梭，在一个尖锐、欢乐的时刻成为群体的中心然后，在不断变化的光线下，带着胜利的兴奋，在面孔、声音和颜色的巨大变化中滑行。

Suddenly one of these gypsies in trembling opal, seizes a cocktail out of the air, dumps it down for courage and moving her hands like Frisco dances out alone on the canvas platform. A momentary hush; the orchestra leader varies his rhythm obligingly for her and there is a burst of chatter as the erroneous news goes around that she is Gilda Gray’s understudy from the ‘Follies.’ The party has begun.
突然，其中一个穿着颤抖的蛋白石的吉普赛人从空中抓起一杯鸡尾酒，将其倒下以获取勇气，并像弗里斯科独自在帆布平台上跳舞一样移动双手。短暂的寂静；管弦乐队领队亲切地为她改变了节奏，当错误的消息传开她是“愚蠢”乐队吉尔达·格雷的替补时，一阵闲聊声响起。派对开始了。

I believe that on the first night I went to Gatsby’s house I was one of the few guests who had actually been invited. People were not invited—they went there.
