‘I’ve got to go to bed.’
“我得去睡觉了。”

‘All right.’
'好的。'

He waited, looking at me with suppressed eagerness.
他等待着，用压抑的渴望看着我。

‘I talked with Miss Baker,’ I said after a moment. ‘I’m going to call up Daisy tomorrow and invite her over here to tea.’
“我和贝克小姐谈过，”过了一会儿我说道。 “明天我会给黛西打电话，邀请她来这里喝茶。”

‘Oh, that’s all right,’ he said carelessly. ‘I don’t want to put you to any trouble.’
“哦，没关系，”他漫不经心地说。 “我不想给你带来任何麻烦。”

‘What day would suit you?’
“哪一天适合你？”

‘What day would suit YOU?’ he corrected me quickly. ‘I don’t want to put you to any trouble, you see.’
“哪一天适合你？”他很快纠正了我。 “我不想给你带来任何麻烦，你瞧。”

‘How about the day after tomorrow?’ He considered for a moment. Then, with reluctance:
“后天怎么样？”他想了想。然后，不情愿地说：

‘I want to get the grass cut,’ he said.
“我想要把草割掉，”他说。

We both looked at the grass—there was a sharp line where my ragged lawn ended and the darker, well-kept expanse of his began. I suspected that he meant my grass.
我们都看着草地——我那片破烂的草坪和他那片更暗、维护得很好的草坪的起点之间有一条清晰的界限。我怀疑他指的是我的草。

‘There’s another little thing,’ he said uncertainly, and hesitated.
“还有一件小事，”他犹豫着，不确定地说。

‘Would you rather put it off for a few days?’ I asked.
“你愿意推迟几天吗？”我问。

‘Oh, it isn’t about that. At least——’ He fumbled with a series of beginnings. ‘Why, I thought—why, look here, old sport, you don’t make much money, do you?’
‘噢，不是这个问题。至少——”他摸索着一系列的开头。 “为什么，我想——为什么，看这里，老伙计，你赚不了多少钱，是吗？”

‘Not very much.’
'不是很多。'

This seemed to reassure him and he continued more confidently.
这似乎让他放心了，他更加自信地继续说道。

‘I thought you didn’t, if you’ll pardon my—you see, I carry on a little business on the side, a sort of sideline, you understand. And I thought that if you don’t make very much—You’re selling bonds, aren’t you, old sport?’
“我以为你没有，请原谅我的——你看，我还做点小生意，一种副业，你明白的。我想，如果你赚得不多——你就在卖债券，不是吗，老兄？

‘Trying to.’
'尝试去。'

‘Well, this would interest you. It wouldn’t take up much of your time and you might pick up a nice bit of money. It happens to be a rather confidential sort of thing.’
‘好吧，这会让你感兴趣的。它不会占用你太多的时间，而且你可能会赚到一大笔钱。这恰好是一件相当机密的事情。

I realize now that under different circumstances that conversation might have been one of the crises of my life. But, because the offer was obviously and tactlessly for a service to be rendered, I had no choice except to cut him off there.
我现在意识到，在不同的情况下，那次谈话可能是我一生的危机之一。但是，由于这个提议显然是不明智的，是为了提供服务，所以我别无选择，只能打断他。

‘I’ve got my hands full,’ I said. ‘I’m much obliged but I couldn’t take on any more work.’
“我忙得不可开交，”我说。 “我非常感激，但我无法承担更多的工作。”

‘You wouldn’t have to do any business with Wolfshiem.’ Evidently he thought that I was shying away from the ‘gonnegtion’ mentioned at lunch, but I assured him he was wrong. He waited a moment longer, hoping I’d begin a conversation, but I was too absorbed to be responsive, so he went unwillingly home.
“你不必和沃尔夫山做任何生意。”显然他认为我在回避午餐时提到的“gonnegtion”，但我向他保证他错了。他又等了一会儿，希望我能开始谈话，但我太专注了，没有反应，所以他不情愿地回家了。

The evening had made me light-headed and happy; I think I walked into a deep sleep as I entered my front door. So I didn’t know whether or not Gatsby went to Coney Island or for how many hours he ‘glanced into rooms’ while his house blazed gaudily on. I called up Daisy from the office next morning and invited her to come to tea.
那个晚上让我感到头晕目眩，心情愉快。我想当我走进前门时我就进入了深度睡眠。所以我不知道盖茨比是否去了科尼岛，也不知道他在房子灯火通明的情况下“看了一眼房间”了多少个小时。第二天早上，我从办公室给黛西打电话，邀请她来喝茶。

‘Don’t bring Tom,’ I warned her.
“别带汤姆来，”我警告她。

‘What?’
'什么？'

‘Don’t bring Tom.’
“别带汤姆来。”

‘Who is ‘Tom’?’ she asked innocently.
“‘汤姆’是谁？”她天真地问。

The day agreed upon was pouring rain. At eleven o’clock a man in a raincoat dragging a lawn-mower tapped at my front door and said that Mr. Gatsby had sent him over to cut my grass. This reminded me that I had forgotten to tell my Finn to come back so I drove into West Egg Village to search for her among soggy white-washed alleys and to buy some cups and lemons and flowers.
约定的日子，下着倾盆大雨。十一点钟，一个穿着雨衣拖着割草机的男人敲着我的前门，说盖茨比先生派他来给我割草。这让我想起我忘了告诉我的芬兰人回来，所以我开车去西埃格村，在湿漉漉的白色小巷里寻找她，买了一些杯子、柠檬和鲜花。

The flowers were unnecessary, for at two o’clock a greenhouse arrived from Gatsby’s, with innumerable receptacles to contain it. An hour later the front door opened nervously, and Gatsby in a white flannel suit, silver shirt and gold-colored tie hurried in. He was pale and there were dark signs of sleeplessness beneath his eyes.
这些花是不必要的，因为两点钟，一座温室从盖茨比家运来，里面有无数的容器来容纳它。一个小时后，前门紧张地打开了，穿着白色法兰绒西装、银色衬衫和金色领带的盖茨比匆匆走了进来。他脸色苍白，眼睛下面有深色的失眠迹象。

‘Is everything all right?’ he asked immediately.
“一切都好吗？”他立即问道。

‘The grass looks fine, if that’s what you mean.’
“草看起来不错，如果你是这个意思的话。”

‘What grass?’ he inquired blankly. ‘Oh, the grass in the yard.’ He looked out the window at it, but judging from his expression I don’t believe he saw a thing.
“什么草？”他茫然地问道。 “哦，院子里的草。”他看着窗外，但从他的表情来看，我不相信他看到了任何东西。

‘Looks very good,’ he remarked vaguely. ‘One of the papers said they thought the rain would stop about four. I think it was ‘The Journal.’ Have you got everything you need in the shape of—of tea?’
“看起来非常好，”他含糊地说。 “其中一份报纸说，他们认为雨会在四点左右停。我想是《华尔街日报》。你已经得到了你需要的一切——茶的形式吗？

I took him into the pantry where he looked a little reproachfully at the Finn. Together we scrutinized the twelve lemon cakes from the delicatessen shop.
我带他走进食品储藏室，他用一种责备的眼神看着芬兰人。我们一起仔细检查了熟食店的十二个柠檬蛋糕。

‘Will they do?’ I asked.
“他们会这么做吗？”我问。

‘Of course, of course! They’re fine!’ and he added hollowly, ‘…old sport.’
‘当然，当然！他们很好！”他空洞地补充道，“……老兄。”

The rain cooled about half-past three to a damp mist through which occasional thin drops swam like dew. Gatsby looked with vacant eyes through a copy of Clay’s ‘Economics,’ starting at the Finnish tread that shook the kitchen floor and peering toward the bleared windows from time to time as if a series of invisible but alarming happenings were taking place outside. Finally he got up and informed me in an uncertain voice that he was going home.
三点半左右，雨冷却成潮湿的雾气，偶尔有细细的水滴像露珠一样在其中游动。盖茨比目光茫然地翻阅着一本克莱的《经济学》，从震动厨房地板的芬兰人的脚步声开始，时不时地向模糊的窗户望去，仿佛外面正在发生一系列看不见但令人震惊的事情。最后他站起来，用不确定的声音告诉我他要回家了。

‘Why’s that?’
'为什么？'

‘Nobody’s coming to tea. It’s too late!’ He looked at his watch as if there was some pressing demand on his time elsewhere. ‘I can’t wait all day.’
“没有人来喝茶。太晚了！”他看了看手表，好像有什么急事需要他去其他地方。 “我不能等一整天。”

‘Don’t be silly; it’s just two minutes to four.’
‘别傻了；差两分钟就四点了。”

He sat down, miserably, as if I had pushed him, and simultaneously there was the sound of a motor turning into my lane. We both jumped up and, a little harrowed myself, I went out into the yard.
他痛苦地坐下来，好像是我推了他一样，与此同时，传来了一辆汽车驶入我车道的声音。我们俩都跳了起来，我心里有点难过，走到了院子里。

Under the dripping bare lilac trees a large open car was coming up the drive. It stopped. Daisy’s face, tipped sideways beneath a three-cornered lavender hat, looked out at me with a bright ecstatic smile.
在滴着水的光秃秃的紫丁香树下，一辆敞篷大车正沿着车道开过来。停了。黛西的脸在一顶淡紫色三角帽子下倾斜着，带着灿烂的欣喜若狂的微笑看着我。

‘Is this absolutely where you live, my dearest one?’
“这绝对是你住的地方吗，我最亲爱的？”

The exhilarating ripple of her voice was a wild tonic in the rain. I had to follow the sound of it for a moment, up and down, with my ear alone before any words came through. A damp streak of hair lay like a dash of blue paint across her cheek and her hand was wet with glistening drops as I took it to help her from the car.
她的声音令人兴奋，就像雨中的一剂狂野的滋补品。在听到任何话之前，我必须只用我的耳朵，上下追踪它的声音。一缕潮湿的头发像一抹蓝色油漆一样落在她的脸颊上，当我接过她的手去扶她下车时，她的手沾满了闪闪发光的水珠。

‘Are you in love with me,’ she said low in my ear. ‘Or why did I have to come alone?’
“你爱我吗？”她在我耳边低声说道。 “或者为什么我必须一个人来？”

‘That’s the secret of Castle Rackrent. Tell your chauffeur to go far away and spend an hour.’
“这就是拉克伦特城堡的秘密。”告诉你的司机去很远的地方，花一个小时。”

‘Come back in an hour, Ferdie.’ Then in a grave murmur, ‘His name is Ferdie.’
“一个小时后回来，费迪。”然后他用严肃的低语说道，“他的名字叫费迪。”

‘Does the gasoline affect his nose?’
“汽油会影响他的鼻子吗？”

‘I don’t think so,’ she said innocently. ‘Why?’
“我不这么认为，”她天真地说道。 '为什么？'

We went in. To my overwhelming surprise the living room was deserted.
我们进去了。令我惊讶的是，客厅里空无一人。

‘Well, that’s funny!’ I exclaimed.
“嗯，这很有趣！”我惊呼道。

‘What’s funny?’
'有什么好笑的？'

She turned her head as there was a light, dignified knocking at the front door. I went out and opened it. Gatsby, pale as death, with his hands plunged like weights in his coat pockets, was standing in a puddle of water glaring tragically into my eyes.
她转过头，门外响起了轻轻而庄重的敲门声。我出去打开它。盖茨比脸色苍白如死人，双手像重物一样插在外套口袋里，站在一滩水里，悲惨地瞪着我的眼睛。

With his hands still in his coat pockets he stalked by me into the hall, turned sharply as if he were on a wire and disappeared into the living room. It wasn’t a bit funny. Aware of the loud beating of my own heart I pulled the door to against the increasing rain.
他双手仍插在外套口袋里，跟着我走进大厅，猛地转身，消失在客厅里。这一点都不好笑。意识到自己的心跳声很大，我拉开了门，以抵御越来越大的雨。

For half a minute there wasn’t a sound. Then from the living room I heard a sort of choking murmur and part of a laugh followed by Daisy’s voice on a clear artificial note.
半分钟没有声音。然后，我从客厅里听到了一种令人窒息的低语声和笑声，接着是黛西的声音，声音清晰，不自然。

‘I certainly am awfully glad to see you again.’
“我真的很高兴再次见到你。”

A pause; it endured horribly. I had nothing to do in the hall so I went into the room.
停顿一下；它忍受着可怕的痛苦。我在大厅里无事可做，就进了房间。

Gatsby, his hands still in his pockets, was reclining against the mantelpiece in a strained counterfeit of perfect ease, even of boredom. His head leaned back so far that it rested against the face of a defunct mantelpiece clock and from this position his distraught eyes stared down at Daisy who was sitting frightened but graceful on the edge of a stiff chair.
盖茨比的双手仍然插在口袋里，斜倚在壁炉架上，一副非常轻松甚至无聊的样子。他的头向后倾斜得厉害，靠在了一个废弃的壁炉台钟的表面上，从这个位置，他心烦意乱的眼睛低头盯着黛西，黛西正惊恐但优雅地坐在一张硬椅子的边缘。

‘We’ve met before,’ muttered Gatsby. His eyes glanced momentarily at me and his lips parted with an abortive attempt at a laugh. Luckily the clock took this moment to tilt dangerously at the pressure of his head, whereupon he turned and caught it with trembling fingers and set it back in place. Then he sat down, rigidly, his elbow on the arm of the sofa and his chin in his hand.
“我们以前见过。”盖茨比低声说道。他的眼睛瞥了我一眼，嘴唇张开，想笑，但没有成功。幸运的是，时钟在他头部的压力下发生了危险的倾斜，于是他转身用颤抖的手指抓住了它，并将其放回原位。然后他僵硬地坐下来，手肘撑在沙发扶手上，手托着下巴。

‘I’m sorry about the clock,’ he said.
“我对时钟感到抱歉，”他说。

My own face had now assumed a deep tropical burn. I couldn’t muster up a single commonplace out of the thousand in my head.
我自己的脸现在呈现出深深的热带灼烧感。我脑子里有一千个陈词滥调，连一个也想不出来。

‘It’s an old clock,’ I told them idiotically.
“这是一个旧钟，”我白痴地告诉他们。

I think we all believed for a moment that it had smashed in pieces on the floor.
我想我们都曾一度相信它在地板上摔成了碎片。

‘We haven’t met for many years,’ said Daisy, her voice as matter-of-fact as it could ever be.
“我们已经很多年没见面了，”黛西说道，她的声音尽可能的平淡。

‘Five years next November.’
“明年十一月就是五年了。”

The automatic quality of Gatsby’s answer set us all back at least another minute. I had them both on their feet with the desperate suggestion that they help me make tea in the kitchen when the demoniac Finn brought it in on a tray.
盖茨比的回答的机械性让我们大家至少又倒退了一分钟。当恶魔般的芬兰人用托盘把茶端进来时，我让他们俩都站了起来，绝望地建议他们帮我在厨房泡茶。

Amid the welcome confusion of cups and cakes a certain physical decency established itself. Gatsby got himself into a shadow and while Daisy and I talked looked conscientiously from one to the other of us with tense unhappy eyes. However, as calmness wasn’t an end in itself I made an excuse at the first possible moment and got to my feet.
在杯子和蛋糕的混乱中，某种身体礼仪自然而然地建立起来。盖茨比躲进了阴影里，黛西和我谈话时，用紧张、不高兴的眼神认真地看着我们中的一个人又看看另一个人。然而，平静本身并不是目的，我在第一时间找了个借口站了起来。

‘Where are you going?’ demanded Gatsby in immediate alarm.
“你要去哪里？”盖茨比立即惊慌地问道。

‘I’ll be back.’
'我会回来的。'

‘I’ve got to speak to you about something before you go.’
“你走之前我得和你谈谈一件事。”

He followed me wildly into the kitchen, closed the door and whispered: ‘Oh, God!’ in a miserable way.
他疯狂地跟着我走进厨房，关上门，用一种悲惨的方式低声说：“哦，上帝！”

‘What’s the matter?’
'怎么了？'

‘This is a terrible mistake,’ he said, shaking his head from side to side, ‘a terrible, terrible mistake.’
“这是一个可怕的错误，”他左右摇头说，“一个非常非常可怕的错误。”

‘You’re just embarrassed, that’s all,’ and luckily I added: ‘Daisy’s embarrassed too.’
“你只是感到尴尬，仅此而已，”幸运的是我补充道：“黛西也感到尴尬。”

‘She’s embarrassed?’ he repeated incredulously.
“她感到尴尬？”他难以置信地重复道。

‘Just as much as you are.’
“和你一样。”

‘Don’t talk so loud.’
“别说话那么大声。”

‘You’re acting like a little boy,’ I broke out impatiently. ‘Not only that but you’re rude. Daisy’s sitting in there all alone.’
“你表现得像个小男孩。”我不耐烦地说道。 ‘不仅如此，你还很粗鲁。黛西独自坐在那儿。

He raised his hand to stop my words, looked at me with unforgettable reproach and opening the door cautiously went back into the other room.
他举手制止了我的话，用令人难忘的责备看着我，然后小心翼翼地打开门回到了另一个房间。

I walked out the back way—just as Gatsby had when he had made his nervous circuit of the house half an hour before—and ran for a huge black knotted tree whose massed leaves made a fabric against the rain. Once more it was pouring and my irregular lawn, well-shaved by Gatsby’s gardener, abounded in small muddy swamps and prehistoric marshes. There was nothing to look at from under the tree except Gatsby’s enormous house, so I stared at it, like Kant at his church steeple, for half an hour. A brewer had built it early in the ‘period’ craze, a decade before, and there was a story that he’d agreed to pay five years’ taxes on all the neighboring cottages if the owners would have their roofs thatched with straw. Perhaps their refusal took the heart out of his plan to Found a Family—he went into an immediate decline. His children sold his house with the black wreath still on the door. Americans, while occasionally willing to be serfs, have always been obstinate about being peasantry.
我从后门走了出去——就像盖茨比半小时前紧张地在房子里绕了一圈时那样——跑向一棵巨大的黑色多节树，它聚集的叶子就像织物一样挡住了雨水。又下起了倾盆大雨，我那片不规则的草坪被盖茨比的园丁精心修剪过，到处都是小泥泞的沼泽和史前沼泽。从树下看去，除了盖茨比那座巨大的房子，没有什么可看的，所以我盯着它看了半个小时，就像康德盯着他的教堂尖塔一样。十年前，一位啤酒商在“时期”热潮的早期建造了它，有一个故事说，如果业主愿意用稻草覆盖屋顶，他同意为所有邻近的小屋缴纳五年的税。也许他们的拒绝让他失去了建立家庭的计划——他的身体立刻就衰弱了。他的孩子们卖掉了他的房子，门上还挂着黑色花环。美国人虽然偶尔愿意当农奴，但一直顽固地不愿成为农民。

After half an hour the sun shone again and the grocer’s automobile rounded Gatsby’s drive with the raw material for his servants’ dinner—I felt sure he wouldn’t eat a spoonful. A maid began opening the upper windows of his house, appeared momentarily in each, and, leaning from a large central bay, spat meditatively into the garden. It was time I went back. While the rain continued it had seemed like the murmur of their voices, rising and swelling a little, now and the, with gusts of emotion. But in the new silence I felt that silence had fallen within the house too.
半小时后，阳光再次普照，杂货店的汽车在盖茨比的车道上行驶，载着他仆人晚餐的原料——我确信他不会吃一勺。一名女仆开始打开他房子楼上的窗户，在每一扇窗户里出现一会儿，然后从一个大的中央海湾倾斜，若有所思地向花园里吐了口唾沫。我是时候回去了。雨继续下着，他们的声音似乎在低语，时不时地升高、膨胀，带着阵阵情感。但在新的寂静中，我感到屋子里也陷入了寂静。

I went in—after making every possible noise in the kitchen short of pushing over the stove—but I don’t believe they heard a sound. They were sitting at either end of the couch looking at each other as if some question had been asked or was in the air, and every vestige of embarrassment was gone. Daisy’s face was smeared with tears and when I came in she jumped up and began wiping at it with her handkerchief before a mirror. But there was a change in Gatsby that was simply confounding. He literally glowed; without a word or a gesture of exultation a new well-being radiated from him and filled the little room.
我进去了——在厨房里发出了所有可能的声音，除了推倒炉子——但我不相信他们听到任何声音。他们坐在沙发的两端，互相看着对方，好像有人问过什么问题，或者有什么问题悬而未决，所有的尴尬都消失了。黛西的脸上沾满了泪水，当我进来时，她跳了起来，开始在镜子前用手帕擦眼泪。但盖茨比的变化却令人困惑。他确实容光焕发；他没有说一句话，也没有做出任何欣喜的动作，一种新的幸福感从他身上散发出来，充满了这个小房间。

‘Oh, hello, old sport,’ he said, as if he hadn’t seen me for years. I thought for a moment he was going to shake hands.
“哦，你好，老伙计，”他说道，好像他已经好几年没见到我了。有一瞬间我以为他要握手。

‘It’s stopped raining.’
“雨停了。”

‘Has it?’ When he realized what I was talking about, that there were twinkle-bells of sunshine in the room, he smiled like a weather man, like an ecstatic patron of recurrent light, and repeated the news to Daisy. ‘What do you think of that? It’s stopped raining.’
“是吗？”当他意识到我在说什么，房间里有闪烁的阳光时，他像天气预报员一样微笑，像一个循环光的欣喜若狂的赞助人，并向黛西重复了这个消息。 '你对那个怎么想的？雨已经停了。

‘I’m glad, Jay.’ Her throat, full of aching, grieving beauty, told only of her unexpected joy.
“我很高兴，杰伊。”她的喉咙充满了疼痛、悲伤的美感，只诉说着她意想不到的喜悦。

‘I want you and Daisy to come over to my house,’ he said, ‘I’d like to show her around.’
“我想让你和黛西来我家，”他说，“我想带她参观一下。”

‘You’re sure you want me to come?’
“你确定要我来吗？”

‘Absolutely, old sport.’
“当然，老兄。”

Daisy went upstairs to wash her face—too late I thought with humiliation of my towels—while Gatsby and I waited on the lawn.
黛西上楼去洗脸——我觉得自己的毛巾很丢脸，但为时已晚——而盖茨比和我则在草坪上等着。

‘My house looks well, doesn’t it?’ he demanded. ‘See how the whole front of it catches the light.’
“我的房子看起来不错，不是吗？”他问道。 “看看它的整个正面是如何捕捉到光线的。”

I agreed that it was splendid.
我同意这真是太棒了。

‘Yes.’ His eyes went over it, every arched door and square tower. ‘It took me just three years to earn the money that bought it.’
“是的。”他的目光扫过每一扇拱门和方塔。 “我只花了三年时间就赚到了买它的钱。”

‘I thought you inherited your money.’
“我以为你继承了你的钱。”

‘I did, old sport,’ he said automatically, ‘but I lost most of it in the big panic—the panic of the war.’
“我做到了，老兄，”他不假思索地说，“但我在巨大的恐慌中失去了大部分——战争的恐慌。”

I think he hardly knew what he was saying, for when I asked him what business he was in he answered ‘That’s my affair,’ before he realized that it wasn’t the appropriate reply.
我想他几乎不知道自己在说什么，因为当我问他从事什么行业时，他回答说“那是我的事”，然后他意识到这不是合适的回答。

‘Oh, I’ve been in several things,’ he corrected himself. ‘I was in the drug business and then I was in the oil business. But I’m not in either one now.’ He looked at me with more attention. ‘Do you mean you’ve been thinking over what I proposed the other night?’
“哦，我做过几件事，”他纠正道。 “我从事过毒品生意，然后又从事过石油生意。但我现在不属于任何一种。”他更加专注地看着我。 “你的意思是你一直在思考我那天晚上提出的建议吗？”

Before I could answer, Daisy came out of the house and two rows of brass buttons on her dress gleamed in the sunlight.
我还没来得及回答，黛西就从屋里出来了，她衣服上的两排黄铜纽扣在阳光下闪闪发光。

‘That huge place THERE?’ she cried pointing.
“那儿有这么大的地方吗？”她指着那儿喊道。

‘Do you like it?’
'你喜欢它？'

‘I love it, but I don’t see how you live there all alone.’
“我喜欢它，但我不明白你怎么一个人住在那里。”

‘I keep it always full of interesting people, night and day. People who do interesting things. Celebrated people.’
“我让这里无论白天黑夜都挤满了有趣的人。做有趣事情的人。名人。”

Instead of taking the short cut along the Sound we went down the road and entered by the big postern. With enchanting murmurs Daisy admired this aspect or that of the feudal silhouette against the sky, admired the gardens, the sparkling odor of jonquils and the frothy odor of hawthorn and plum blossoms and the pale gold odor of kiss-me-at-the-gate. It was strange to reach the marble steps and find no stir of bright dresses in and out the door, and hear no sound but bird voices in the trees.
我们没有沿着海峡抄近路，而是沿着路，从大后门进入。黛西怀着迷人的呢喃，欣赏着天空映衬下的封建剪影的这一面或那一幕，欣赏着花园、黄水仙的闪闪发光的气味、山楂花和梅花的泡沫气味以及在门口吻我的淡金色气味。 。奇怪的是，走到大理石台阶上，门外没有任何鲜艳衣服的动静，除了树上的鸟叫声之外，什么声音也听不到。

And inside as we wandered through Marie Antoinette music rooms and Restoration salons I felt that there were guests concealed behind every couch and table, under orders to be breathlessly silent until we had passed through. As Gatsby closed the door of ‘the Merton College Library’ I could have sworn I heard the owl-eyed man break into ghostly laughter.
当我们漫步穿过玛丽·安托瓦内特的音乐室和复辟时期的沙龙时，我感觉到每张沙发和桌子后面都藏着客人，他们被命令保持安静，直到我们通过为止。当盖茨比关上“默顿学院图书馆”的门时，我可以发誓我听到了那个猫头鹰眼的男人发出幽灵般的笑声。

We went upstairs, through period bedrooms swathed in rose and lavender silk and vivid with new flowers, through dressing rooms and poolrooms, and bathrooms with sunken baths—intruding into one chamber where a dishevelled man in pajamas was doing liver exercises on the floor. It was Mr. Klipspringer, the ‘boarder.’ I had seen him wandering hungrily about the beach that morning. Finally we came to Gatsby’s own apartment, a bedroom and a bath and an Adam study, where we sat down and drank a glass of some Chartreuse he took from a cupboard in the wall.
我们上楼，穿过铺满玫瑰和薰衣草丝绸、鲜艳夺目的新花的仿古卧室，穿过更衣室和台球室，以及带下沉式浴缸的浴室——闯入一间房间，里面有一个穿着睡衣的衣衫不整的男人正在地板上做肝脏练习。那是克利普斯普林格先生，“寄宿生”。那天早上我看到他在海滩上饥肠辘辘地闲逛。最后，我们来到了盖茨比自己的公寓，有一间卧室、一间浴室和一间亚当书房，我们坐下来喝了一杯他从墙上的橱柜里拿出来的查特勒酒。

He hadn’t once ceased looking at Daisy and I think he revalued everything in his house according to the measure of response it drew from her well-loved eyes. Sometimes, too, he stared around at his possessions in a dazed way as though in her actual and astounding presence none of it was any longer real. Once he nearly toppled down a flight of stairs.
他一直没有停止注视黛西，我想他根据黛西深爱的眼睛所引起的反应来重新评估家里的一切。有时，他也会茫然地环顾四周，看着自己的财产，仿佛在她真实而令人震惊的存在下，一切都不再真实了。有一次他差点从楼梯上摔下来。

His bedroom was the simplest room of all—except where the dresser was garnished with a toilet set of pure dull gold. Daisy took the brush with delight and smoothed her hair, whereupon Gatsby sat down and shaded his eyes and began to laugh.
他的卧室是所有房间中最简单的——除了梳妆台上装饰着纯金色的马桶套件。黛西高兴地拿起刷子，抚平头发，盖茨比坐下来，遮住眼睛，开始大笑。

‘It’s the funniest thing, old sport,’ he said hilariously. ‘I can’t—when I try to——‘
“这是最有趣的事情，老伙计，”他滑稽地说。 “我不能——当我试图——”

He had passed visibly through two states and was entering upon a third. After his embarrassment and his unreasoning joy he was consumed with wonder at her presence. He had been full of the idea so long, dreamed it right through to the end, waited with his teeth set, so to speak, at an inconceivable pitch of intensity. Now, in the reaction, he was running down like an overwound clock.
他显然已经经历了两种状态，正在进入第三种状态。在经历了尴尬和莫名的喜悦之后，他对她的出现感到惊讶不已。他长期以来一直充满这个想法，一直梦想到最后，可以说，他咬牙切齿地等待着。现在，他的反应是像一只上发条的时钟一样往下跑。

Recovering himself in a minute he opened for us two hulking patent cabinets which held his massed suits and dressing-gowns and ties, and his shirts, piled like bricks in stacks a dozen high.
他很快就恢复了过来，为我们打开了两个巨大的专利柜，里面装满了他的西装、晨衣和领带，还有他的衬衫，像砖头一样堆成十几叠高。

‘I’ve got a man in England who buys me clothes. He sends over a selection of things at the beginning of each season, spring and fall.’
“我在英国有一个男人给我买衣服。他在每个季节（春季和秋季）开始时都会发送一些精选的东西。

He took out a pile of shirts and began throwing them, one by one before us, shirts of sheer linen and thick silk and fine flannel which lost their folds as they fell and covered the table in many-colored disarray. While we admired he brought more and the soft rich heap mounted higher—shirts with stripes and scrolls and plaids in coral and apple-green and lavender and faint orange with monograms of Indian blue. Suddenly with a strained sound, Daisy bent her head into the shirts and began to cry stormily.
他拿出一堆衬衫，开始把它们一件一件地扔到我们面前，这些衬衫是由纯亚麻布、厚丝绸和细法兰绒制成的，它们在掉落时失去了褶皱，杂乱地覆盖了桌子。在我们钦佩的同时，他带来了更多，柔软而丰富的衣服堆得更高——珊瑚色、苹果绿、薰衣草色和淡橙色的条纹、卷轴和格子衬衫，上面印有印度蓝色的花押字。突然，黛西发出一声紧张的声音，把头埋进衬衫里，开始嚎啕大哭。

‘They’re such beautiful shirts,’ she sobbed, her voice muffled in the thick folds. ‘It makes me sad because I’ve never seen such—such beautiful shirts before.’
“它们真是漂亮的衬衫，”她抽泣着，声音被厚厚的褶皱压住了。 “这让我很难过，因为我以前从未见过如此美丽的衬衫。”

After the house, we were to see the grounds and the swimming pool, and the hydroplane and the midsummer flowers—but outside Gatsby’s window it began to rain again so we stood in a row looking at the corrugated surface of the Sound.
离开房子后，我们要参观庭院、游泳池、水上飞机和盛夏的花朵——但盖茨比的窗外又开始下雨了，所以我们站成一排，看着海湾的波纹状表面。

‘If it wasn’t for the mist we could see your home across the bay,’ said Gatsby. ‘You always have a green light that burns all night at the end of your dock.’
“如果没有雾气，我们可以看到海湾对面你的家，”盖茨比说。 “码头的尽头总是有一盏绿灯整夜亮着。”

Daisy put her arm through his abruptly but he seemed absorbed in what he had just said. Possibly it had occurred to him that the colossal significance of that light had now vanished forever. Compared to the great distance that had separated him from Daisy it had seemed very near to her, almost touching her. It had seemed as close as a star to the moon. Now it was again a green light on a dock. His count of enchanted objects had diminished by one.
黛西突然挽住他的手臂，但他似乎全神贯注于他刚才说的话。也许他突然意识到那道光的巨大意义现在已经永远消失了。与他和黛西之间的遥远距离相比，它似乎离她很近，几乎触碰到了她。它看起来就像星星离月亮一样近。现在，码头又亮起了绿灯。他的魔法物品数量减少了一件。

I began to walk about the room, examining various indefinite objects in the half darkness. A large photograph of an elderly man in yachting costume attracted me, hung on the wall over his desk.
我开始在房间里走来走去，在半明半暗的环境中检查各种不确定的物体。办公桌上方的墙上挂着一张身穿游艇服装的老人的大幅照片，吸引了我。

‘Who’s this?’
'这是谁？'

‘That? That’s Mr. Dan Cody, old sport.’
'那？那是丹·科迪先生，老兄。

The name sounded faintly familiar.
这个名字听起来有些耳熟。

‘He’s dead now. He used to be my best friend years ago.’
‘他现在死了。多年前他曾经是我最好的朋友。

There was a small picture of Gatsby, also in yachting costume, on the bureau—Gatsby with his head thrown back defiantly—taken apparently when he was about eighteen.
梳妆台上有一张盖茨比的小照片，同样穿着游艇服装——盖茨比挑衅地向后仰着头——显然是在他十八岁时拍摄的。

‘I adore it!’ exclaimed Daisy. ‘The pompadour! You never told me you had a pompadour—or a yacht.’
“我喜欢它！”黛西惊呼道。 ‘蓬巴杜！你从来没有告诉过我你有一个蓬巴杜发型——或者一艘游艇。

‘Look at this,’ said Gatsby quickly. ‘Here’s a lot of clippings—about you.’
“看看这个，”盖茨比很快说道。 “这里有很多关于你的剪报。”

They stood side by side examining it. I was going to ask to see the rubies when the phone rang and Gatsby took up the receiver.
他们并排站着检查它。我正想要求看看红宝石，这时电话响了，盖茨比拿起听筒。

‘Yes…. Well, I can’t talk now…. I can’t talk now, old sport…. I said a SMALL town…. He must know what a small town is…. Well, he’s no use to us if Detroit is his idea of a small town….’
'是的…。好吧，我现在不能说话……我现在不能说话，老兄……我说的是一个小镇……他一定知道什么是小镇……好吧，如果底特律是他心目中的小镇，他对我们就没用了……”

He rang off.
他挂断了电话。

‘Come here QUICK!’ cried Daisy at the window.
“快过来！”黛西在窗边喊道。

The rain was still falling, but the darkness had parted in the west, and there was a pink and golden billow of foamy clouds above the sea.
雨还在下，但西方的黑暗已经分开，海面上有一朵粉红色和金色的泡沫云。

‘Look at that,’ she whispered, and then after a moment: ‘I’d like to just get one of those pink clouds and put you in it and push you around.’
“看那个，”她低声说道，过了一会儿，“我想拿一朵粉红色的云，把你放进去，然后推着你走。”

I tried to go then, but they wouldn’t hear of it; perhaps my presence made them feel more satisfactorily alone.
我当时试图去，但他们不听。也许我的存在让他们感到更满足的孤独感。

‘I know what we’ll do,’ said Gatsby, ‘we’ll have Klipspringer play the piano.’
“我知道我们要做什么，”盖茨比说，“我们会让克利普斯普林格弹钢琴。”

He went out of the room calling ‘Ewing!’ and returned in a few minutes accompanied by an embarrassed, slightly worn young man with shell-rimmed glasses and scanty blonde hair. He was now decently clothed in a ‘sport shirt’ open at the neck, sneakers and duck trousers of a nebulous hue.
他一边喊着“尤因！”一边走出房间，几分钟后就回来了，身边还跟着一个尴尬、略显疲惫的年轻人，他戴着贝壳框眼镜，金发稀疏。他现在穿着一件领口敞开的“运动衫”，脚上穿着运动鞋和颜色模糊的鸭舌裤。

‘Did we interrupt your exercises?’ inquired Daisy politely.
“我们打扰了你的练习吗？”黛西礼貌地问道。

‘I was asleep,’ cried Mr. Klipspringer, in a spasm of embarrassment. ‘That is, I’d BEEN asleep. Then I got up….’
“我睡着了，”克里普斯普林格先生尴尬地抽搐着喊道。 “也就是说，我已经睡着了。然后我就起身了……”

‘Klipspringer plays the piano,’ said Gatsby, cutting him off. ‘Don’t you, Ewing, old sport?’
“克利普斯普林格会弹钢琴，”盖茨比打断了他的话。 “你不是吗，尤因，老伙计？”

‘I don’t play well. I don’t—I hardly play at all. I’m all out of prac——‘
‘我打得不好。我不——我几乎不玩。我完全没有练习了——’

‘We’ll go downstairs,’ interrupted Gatsby. He flipped a switch. The grey windows disappeared as the house glowed full of light.
“我们下楼去。”盖茨比打断道。他按下了开关。当房子充满光线时，灰色的窗户消失了。

In the music room Gatsby turned on a solitary lamp beside the piano. He lit Daisy’s cigarette from a trembling match, and sat down with her on a couch far across the room where there was no light save what the gleaming floor bounced in from the hall.
在音乐室里，盖茨比打开了钢琴旁边的一盏孤灯。他用一根颤抖的火柴点燃了黛西的香烟，然后和她一起坐在房间另一边的沙发上，除了从大厅反射进来的闪闪发光的地板之外，没有任何光线。

When Klipspringer had played ‘The Love Nest’ he turned around on the bench and searched unhappily for Gatsby in the gloom.
当克利普斯普林格演奏完《爱巢》后，他在长凳上转过身来，不高兴地在黑暗中寻找盖茨比。

‘I’m all out of practice, you see. I told you I couldn’t play. I’m all out of prac——‘
“你看，我已经完全没有练习了。我告诉过你我不能玩。我完全没有练习了——’

‘Don’t talk so much, old sport,’ commanded Gatsby. ‘Play!’
“别说那么多了，老兄，”盖茨比命令道。 '玩！'

IN THE MORNING,
在早晨，

IN THE EVENING,
晚上，

AIN’T WE GOT FUN——
我们玩得开心吗——

Outside the wind was loud and there was a faint flow of thunder along the Sound. All the lights were going on in West Egg now; the electric trains, men-carrying, were plunging home through the rain from New York. It was the hour of a profound human change, and excitement was generating on the air.
外面风很大，海峡沿岸隐约传来雷声。现在西埃格所有的灯都亮了；载着乘客的电动火车冒着大雨从纽约驶回家乡。这是人类发生深刻变化的时刻，空气中弥漫着兴奋的气氛。

ONE THING’S SURE AND NOTHING’S SURER
一件事是确定的，没有什么是确定的

THE RICH GET RICHER AND THE POOR GET—CHILDREN.
富人变得更富，穷人得到孩子。

IN THE MEANTIME,
同时，

IN BETWEEN TIME——
时间之间——

As I went over to say goodbye I saw that the expression of bewilderment had come back into Gatsby’s face, as though a faint doubt had occurred to him as to the quality of his present happiness. Almost five years! There must have been moments even that afternoon when Daisy tumbled short of his dreams—not through her own fault but because of the colossal vitality of his illusion. It had gone beyond her, beyond everything. He had thrown himself into it with a creative passion, adding to it all the time, decking it out with every bright feather that drifted his way. No amount of fire or freshness can challenge what a man will store up in his ghostly heart.
当我走过去告别时，我发现盖茨比的脸上又出现了困惑的表情，仿佛他对自己目前的幸福感产生了一丝怀疑。快五年了！就连那天下午，黛西肯定也曾在某些时刻未能实现他的梦想——不是因为她自己的错，而是因为他幻想的巨大活力。它已经超越了她，超越了一切。他以一种创造性的热情全身心投入其中，不断地添加它，用他飘过的每一根明亮的羽毛装饰它。再多的火焰和新鲜感都无法挑战一个人在他幽灵般的心中所储存的东西。

As I watched him he adjusted himself a little, visibly. His hand took hold of hers and as she said something low in his ear he turned toward her with a rush of emotion. I think that voice held him most with its fluctuating, feverish warmth because it couldn’t be over-dreamed—that voice was a deathless song.
当我看着他时，他明显地调整了自己。他握住她的手，当她在他耳边低声说了些什么时，他激动地转向她。我认为那个声音以其波动的、狂热的温暖最吸引他，因为它是不可能过度梦想的——那个声音是一首不朽的歌。

They had forgotten me, but Daisy glanced up and held out her hand; Gatsby didn’t know me now at all. I looked once more at them and they looked back at me, remotely, possessed by intense life. Then I went out of the room and down the marble steps into the rain, leaving them there together.
他们已经忘记了我，但黛西抬起头，伸出了手。盖茨比现在根本不认识我了。我再次看着他们，他们也回望着我，目光遥远，充满了强烈的生命力。然后我走出房间，走下大理石台阶，走进雨中，把他们留在那里。

Chapter 6
第6章

About this time an ambitious young reporter from New York arrived one morning at Gatsby’s door and asked him if he had anything to say.
大约在这个时候，一天早上，一位来自纽约的雄心勃勃的年轻记者来到盖茨比家门口，问他是否有什么话要说。

‘Anything to say about what?’ inquired Gatsby politely.
“有什么要说的吗？”盖茨比礼貌地问道。

‘Why,—any statement to give out.’
“为什么——有什么要发表的声明吗？”

It transpired after a confused five minutes that the man had heard Gatsby’s name around his office in a connection which he either wouldn’t reveal or didn’t fully understand. This was his day off and with laudable initiative he had hurried out ‘to see.’
在混乱的五分钟后，这名男子在他的办公室里听到了盖茨比的名字，但他要么不肯透露，要么不完全理解。今天是他的休息日，他带着值得称赞的主动性匆忙出去“看看”。

It was a random shot, and yet the reporter’s instinct was right. Gatsby’s notoriety, spread about by the hundreds who had accepted his hospitality and so become authorities on his past, had increased all summer until he fell just short of being news. Contemporary legends such as the ‘underground pipe-line to Canada’ attached themselves to him, and there was one persistent story that he didn’t live in a house at all, but in a boat that looked like a house and was moved secretly up and down the Long Island shore. Just why these inventions were a source of satisfaction to James Gatz of North Dakota, isn’t easy to say.
虽然是随手拍的，但记者的直觉是对的。盖茨比的名声在整个夏天都在增加，直到他差点成为新闻人物为止，数百名接受过他的款待并因此成为他过去的权威的人传播开来。诸如“通往加拿大的地下管道”等当代传说都与他息息相关，还有一个流传甚广的故事，即他根本不住在房子里，而是住在一艘看起来像房子的船上，被偷偷搬到了船上。并沿着长岛海岸行驶。很难说清楚为什么这些发明让北达科他州的詹姆斯·盖茨感到满意。

James Gatz—that was really, or at least legally, his name. He had changed it at the age of seventeen and at the specific moment that witnessed the beginning of his career—when he saw Dan Cody’s yacht drop anchor over the most insidious flat on Lake Superior. It was James Gatz who had been loafing along the beach that afternoon in a torn green jersey and a pair of canvas pants, but it was already Jay Gatsby who borrowed a row-boat, pulled out to the TUOLOMEE and informed Cody that a wind might catch him and break him up in half an hour.
詹姆斯·盖茨——这确实是他的名字，或者至少在法律上是他的名字。他在十七岁时改变了这一点，也是在见证他职业生涯开始的特定时刻——当他看到丹·科迪的游艇在苏必利尔湖最阴险的平地上抛锚时。那天下午，詹姆斯·盖茨穿着一件破烂的绿色运动衫和一条帆布裤，在海滩上闲逛，但杰伊·盖茨比已经借了一艘划艇，把船开到了图奥洛米号，并告诉科迪，可能会刮风。半个小时之内抓住他并把他打散。

I suppose he’d had the name ready for a long time, even then. His parents were shiftless and unsuccessful farm people—his imagination had never really accepted them as his parents at all. The truth was that Jay Gatsby, of West Egg, Long Island, sprang from his Platonic conception of himself. He was a son of God—a phrase which, if it means anything, means just that—and he must be about His Father’s Business, the service of a vast, vulgar and meretricious beauty. So he invented just the sort of Jay Gatsby that a seventeen-year-old boy would be likely to invent, and to this conception he was faithful to the end.
我想即使在那时，他也已经准备好了这个名字很长时间了。他的父母都是无能、不成功的农民——他的想象从来没有真正接受过他们是他的父母。事实上，来自长岛西埃格的杰伊·盖茨比源于他柏拉图式的自我概念。他是上帝的儿子——这句话，如果有什么意义的话，就是这个意思——他一定会关心天父的事业，为一位巨大、粗俗而华而不实的美人服务。因此，他创造了一个十七岁男孩可能会创造的杰·盖茨比，并且他始终忠于这个概念。

For over a year he had been beating his way along the south shore of Lake Superior as a clam digger and a salmon fisher or in any other capacity that brought him food and bed. His brown, hardening body lived naturally through the half fierce, half lazy work of the bracing days. He knew women early and since they spoiled him he became contemptuous of them, of young virgins because they were ignorant, of the others because they were hysterical about things which in his overwhelming self-absorption he took for granted.
一年多来，他一直沿着苏必利尔湖南岸打拼，担任挖蛤者和鲑鱼捕捞者，或者从事其他为他带来食物和床铺的工作。他褐色、坚硬的身体自然地度过了那些令人振奋的日子里一半激烈、一半懒惰的工作。他很早就认识女人，自从她们宠坏了他之后，他就开始蔑视她们，蔑视年轻的处女，因为她们无知，蔑视其他人，因为她们对他认为理所当然的事情歇斯底里。

But his heart was in a constant, turbulent riot. The most grotesque and fantastic conceits haunted him in his bed at night. A universe of ineffable gaudiness spun itself out in his brain while the clock ticked on the wash-stand and the moon soaked with wet light his tangled clothes upon the floor. Each night he added to the pattern of his fancies until drowsiness closed down upon some vivid scene with an oblivious embrace. For a while these reveries provided an outlet for his imagination; they were a satisfactory hint of the unreality of reality, a promise that the rock of the world was founded securely on a fairy’s wing.
但他的内心却一直在汹涌澎湃。晚上，最怪异、最荒唐的想法在他的床上萦绕不去。盥洗台上的时钟滴答作响，月亮用湿漉漉的光芒浸湿了地板上缠结的衣服，此时他的脑海中浮现出一个难以言喻的华丽世界。每天晚上，他都增加自己的幻想模式，直到睡意以一种不经意的拥抱关闭了一些生动的场景。有一段时间，这些遐想为他的想象力提供了一个出口。它们令人满意地暗示了现实的不真实性，承诺了世界的基石牢固地建立在仙女的翅膀上。

An instinct toward his future glory had led him, some months before, to the small Lutheran college of St. Olaf in southern Minnesota. He stayed there two weeks, dismayed at its ferocious indifference to the drums of his destiny, to destiny itself, and despising the janitor’s work with which he was to pay his way through. Then he drifted back to Lake Superior, and he was still searching for something to do on the day that Dan Cody’s yacht dropped anchor in the shallows along shore.
几个月前，出于对未来荣耀的本能，他来到了明尼苏达州南部的路德教会圣奥拉夫学院。他在那里呆了两个星期，对它对他命运的鼓声、对命运本身的冷漠感到沮丧，并鄙视他赖以生存的看门人的工作。然后他漂回苏必利尔湖，丹·科迪的游艇在岸边浅滩抛锚的那天，他仍在寻找事情可做。

Cody was fifty years old then, a product of the Nevada silver fields, of the Yukon, of every rush for metal since Seventy-five. The transactions in Montana copper that made him many times a millionaire found him physically robust but on the verge of soft-mindedness, and, suspecting this an infinite number of women tried to separate him from his money. The none too savory ramifications by which Ella Kaye, the newspaper woman, played Madame de Maintenon to his weakness and sent him to sea in a yacht, were common knowledge to the turgid journalism of 1902. He had been coasting along all too hospitable shores for five years when he turned up as James Gatz’s destiny at Little Girl Bay.
科迪当时五十岁了，是内华达银矿、育空地区以及自七十五岁以来每一次金属热潮的产物。蒙大拿州铜矿的交易使他多次成为百万富翁，但他身体强壮，但心智却处于软弱的边缘，并且怀疑这一点的无数妇女试图将他与他的钱分开。女报人艾拉·凯 (Ella Kaye) 扮演德·曼特农夫人 (Madame de Maintenon) 的弱点，并把他送上游艇出海，所造成的后果并不令人愉快，这对于 1902 年浮夸的新闻界来说是众所周知的。五年后，他在小女孩湾成为詹姆斯·盖茨的命运。

To the young Gatz, resting on his oars and looking up at the railed deck, the yacht represented all the beauty and glamor in the world. I suppose he smiled at Cody—he had probably discovered that people liked him when he smiled. At any rate Cody asked him a few questions (one of them elicited the brand new name) and found that he was quick, and extravagantly ambitious. A few days later he took him to Duluth and bought him a blue coat, six pair of white duck trousers and a yachting cap. And when the TUOLOMEE left for the West Indies and the Barbary Coast Gatsby left too.
对于年轻的盖茨来说，坐在桨上，抬头看着有栏杆的甲板，这艘游艇代表了世界上所有的美丽和魅力。我想他对科迪微笑——他可能发现当他微笑时人们喜欢他。无论如何，科迪问了他几个问题（其中一个引出了全新的名字），发现他动作敏捷，而且雄心勃勃。几天后，他带他去了德卢斯，给他买了一件蓝色外套、六条白色鸭绒裤和一顶游艇帽。当TUOLOMEE 前往西印度群岛和巴巴里海岸时，盖茨比也离开了。

He was employed in a vague personal capacity—while he remained with Cody he was in turn steward, mate, skipper, secretary, and even jailor, for Dan Cody sober knew what lavish doings Dan Cody drunk might soon be about and he provided for such contingencies by reposing more and more trust in Gatsby. The arrangement lasted five years during which the boat went three times around the continent. It might have lasted indefinitely except for the fact that Ella Kaye came on board one night in Boston and a week later Dan Cody inhospitably died.
他以模糊的个人身份受雇——当他留在科迪身边时，他依次担任乘务员、大副、船长、秘书，甚至狱卒，因为丹·科迪清醒地知道丹·科迪喝醉后可能很快就会做出什么奢侈的事情，他提供了这样的服务。通过对盖茨比越来越信任来应对突发事件。这种安排持续了五年，期间这艘船绕非洲大陆三圈。这种情况可能会无限期地持续下去，除非埃拉·凯伊在波士顿的一个晚上登上了这艘船，一周后丹·科迪不幸去世。

I remember the portrait of him up in Gatsby’s bedroom, a grey, florid man with a hard empty face—the pioneer debauchee who during one phase of American life brought back to the eastern seaboard the savage violence of the frontier brothel and saloon. It was indirectly due to Cody that Gatsby drank so little. Sometimes in the course of gay parties women used to rub champagne into his hair; for himself he formed the habit of letting liquor alone.
我记得盖茨比卧室里的他的肖像，一个灰色的、红润的男人，一张冷酷而空洞的脸——这位放荡的先锋，在美国生活的某个阶段，他把边境妓院和酒吧的野蛮暴力带回了东海岸。盖茨比喝得这么少，间接也是科迪的功劳。有时，在同性恋聚会上，女人们会用香槟擦他的头发。他自己也养成了不喝酒的习惯。

And it was from Cody that he inherited money—a legacy of twenty-five thousand dollars. He didn’t get it. He never understood the legal device that was used against him but what remained of the millions went intact to Ella Kaye. He was left with his singularly appropriate education; the vague contour of Jay Gatsby had filled out to the substantiality of a man.
他从科迪那里继承了金钱——两万五千美元的遗​​产。他不明白。他从来不理解针对他使用的法律手段，但剩下的数百万美元却完好无损地落到了艾拉·凯的手里。他只接受了非常适当的教育。杰·盖茨比的模糊轮廓已经充实到了一个男人的实质。

He told me all this very much later, but I’ve put it down here with the idea of exploding those first wild rumors about his antecedents, which weren’t even faintly true. Moreover he told it to me at a time of confusion, when I had reached the point of believing everything and nothing about him. So I take advantage of this short halt, while Gatsby, so to speak, caught his breath, to clear this set of misconceptions away.
很久以后他才告诉我这一切，但我把它写在这里，是为了推翻那些关于他的前身的最初的疯狂谣言，这些谣言甚至都不是真实的。而且，他是在我很困惑的时候告诉我这件事的，当时我已经到了对他的一切都相信又不相信的地步。所以我利用这个短暂的停顿，而盖茨比，可以说，喘了口气，澄清了这一系列误解。

It was a halt, too, in my association with his affairs. For several weeks I didn’t see him or hear his voice on the phone—mostly I was in New York, trotting around with Jordan and trying to ingratiate myself with her senile aunt—but finally I went over to his house one Sunday afternoon. I hadn’t been there two minutes when somebody brought Tom Buchanan in for a drink. I was startled, naturally, but the really surprising thing was that it hadn’t happened before.
我也停止了与他的事务的联系。有几个星期我没有见到他，也没有在电话里听到他的声音——大部分时间我都在纽约，和乔丹一起闲逛，试图讨好她年迈的阿姨——但最后一个周日下午我去了他家。我到那里还不到两分钟，就有人带汤姆·布坎南进来喝一杯。我自然感到震惊，但真正令人惊讶的是，这种情况以前从未发生过。

They were a party of three on horseback—Tom and a man named Sloane and a pretty woman in a brown riding habit who had been there previously.
他们一行三人骑在马背上——汤姆、一个名叫斯隆的男人和一个以前来过那里、穿着棕色骑马服的漂亮女人。

‘I’m delighted to see you,’ said Gatsby standing on his porch. ‘I’m delighted that you dropped in.’
“我很高兴见到你，”盖茨比站在门廊上说道。 “我很高兴你能过来。”

As though they cared!
好像他们很关心一样！

‘Sit right down. Have a cigarette or a cigar.’ He walked around the room quickly, ringing bells. ‘I’ll have something to drink for you in just a minute.’
‘快坐下吧。抽支烟或雪茄吧。”他快速地在房间里走来走去，按响门铃。 “我一会儿就给你喝点东西。”

He was profoundly affected by the fact that Tom was there. But he would be uneasy anyhow until he had given them something, realizing in a vague way that that was all they came for. Mr. Sloane wanted nothing. A lemonade? No, thanks. A little champagne? Nothing at all, thanks…. I’m sorry——
汤姆在那里的事实深深地影响了他。但无论如何，在他给他们一些东西之前，他都会感到不安，并模糊地意识到这就是他们来这里的全部目的。斯隆先生什么都不想要。柠檬水？不，谢谢。一点香槟？没什么，谢谢……对不起 -

‘Did you have a nice ride?’
“旅途愉快吗？”

‘Very good roads around here.’
“这里的路况非常好。”

‘I suppose the automobiles——‘
“我想汽车——”

‘Yeah.’
'是的。'

Moved by an irresistible impulse, Gatsby turned to Tom who had accepted the introduction as a stranger.
盖茨比被一种不可抗拒的冲动所感动，转向以陌生人的身份接受介绍的汤姆。

‘I believe we’ve met somewhere before, Mr. Buchanan.’
“我相信我们以前在某个地方见过面，布坎南先生。”

‘Oh, yes,’ said Tom, gruffly polite but obviously not remembering. ‘So we did. I remember very well.’
“哦，是的，”汤姆粗声粗气地礼貌地说，但显然不记得了。 ‘所以我们做到了。我记得很清楚。

‘About two weeks ago.’
“大约两周前。”

‘That’s right. You were with Nick here.’
'这是正确的。你和尼克在一起。

‘I know your wife,’ continued Gatsby, almost aggressively.
“我认识你的妻子，”盖茨比几乎咄咄逼人地继续说道。

‘That so?’
'这么？'

Tom turned to me.
汤姆转向我。

‘You live near here, Nick?’
“尼克，你住附近吗？”

‘Next door.’
'隔壁。'

‘That so?’
'这么？'

Mr. Sloane didn’t enter into the conversation but lounged back haughtily in his chair; the woman said nothing either—until unexpectedly, after two highballs, she became cordial.
斯隆先生没有加入谈话，只是傲慢地靠在椅子上。女人也没有说话，直到喝了两杯威士忌后，她突然变得热情起来。

‘We’ll all come over to your next party, Mr. Gatsby,’ she suggested. ‘What do you say?’
“我们都会来参加你的下一场聚会，盖茨比先生，”她建议道。 '你怎么说？'

‘Certainly. I’d be delighted to have you.’
'当然。我很高兴有你。

‘Be ver’ nice,’ said Mr. Sloane, without gratitude. ‘Well—think ought to be starting home.’
“要非常友善，”斯隆先生毫无感激地说。 “嗯——我想应该开始回家了。”

‘Please don’t hurry,’ Gatsby urged them. He had control of himself now and he wanted to see more of Tom. ‘Why don’t you—why don’t you stay for supper? I wouldn’t be surprised if some other people dropped in from New York.’
“请别着急，”盖茨比催促他们。他现在可以控制自己了，他想多看看汤姆。 “你为什么不——为什么不留下来吃晚饭呢？”如果其他人从纽约过来，我不会感到惊讶。

‘You come to supper with ME,’ said the lady enthusiastically. ‘Both of you.’
“你来和我一起吃晚饭吧，”那位女士热情地说。 '你们俩。'

This included me. Mr. Sloane got to his feet.
这其中也包括我。斯隆先生站了起来。

‘Come along,’ he said—but to her only.
“来吧，”他说——但只对她说。

‘I mean it,’ she insisted. ‘I’d love to have you. Lots of room.’
“我是认真的，”她坚持道。 ‘我很想拥有你。有很多空间。

Gatsby looked at me questioningly. He wanted to go and he didn’t see that Mr. Sloane had determined he shouldn’t.
盖茨比疑惑地看着我。他想去，但没有看到斯隆先生决定他不去。

‘I’m afraid I won’t be able to,’ I said.
“恐怕我做不到，”我说。

‘Well, you come,’ she urged, concentrating on Gatsby.
“好吧，你来吧，”她催促道，注意力集中在盖茨比身上。

Mr. Sloane murmured something close to her ear.
斯隆先生在她耳边低声说了些什么。

‘We won’t be late if we start now,’ she insisted aloud.
“如果我们现在开始就不会迟到，”她大声坚持道。

‘I haven’t got a horse,’ said Gatsby. ‘I used to ride in the army but I’ve never bought a horse. I’ll have to follow you in my car. Excuse me for just a minute.’
盖茨比说：“我没有马。” “我曾经在军队里骑马，但从未买过马。我得开车跟着你。请原谅我一分钟。

The rest of us walked out on the porch, where Sloane and the lady began an impassioned conversation aside.
我们其他人走到门廊上，斯隆和那位女士在一旁开始了热烈的交谈。

‘My God, I believe the man’s coming,’ said Tom. ‘Doesn’t he know she doesn’t want him?’
“天哪，我相信那个人会来，”汤姆说。 “他难道不知道她不想要他吗？”

‘She says she does want him.’
“她说她确实想要他。”

‘She has a big dinner party and he won’t know a soul there.’ He frowned. ‘I wonder where in the devil he met Daisy. By God, I may be old-fashioned in my ideas, but women run around too much these days to suit me. They meet all kinds of crazy fish.’
“她要举办一场盛大的晚宴，而他在那里一个人都不认识。”他皱起眉头。 “我不知道他究竟在哪里遇见了黛西。老天爷啊，我的想法可能有些老套，但现在女人太多了，不适合我。他们遇到了各种疯狂的鱼。

Suddenly Mr. Sloane and the lady walked down the steps and mounted their horses.
突然，斯隆先生和那位女士走下台阶，骑上马。

‘Come on,’ said Mr. Sloane to Tom, ‘we’re late. We’ve got to go.’ And then to me: ‘Tell him we couldn’t wait, will you?’
“快点，”斯隆先生对汤姆说，“我们迟到了。”我们得走了。”然后对我说：“告诉他我们等不及了，好吗？”

Tom and I shook hands, the rest of us exchanged a cool nod and they trotted quickly down the drive, disappearing under the August foliage just as Gatsby with hat and light overcoat in hand came out the front door.
汤姆和我握了握手，我们其他人冷静地点了点头，然后他们快步沿着车道小跑，消失在八月的树叶下，这时盖茨比戴着帽子，手里拿着轻便大衣，从前门走了出来。

Tom was evidently perturbed at Daisy’s running around alone, for on the following Saturday night he came with her to Gatsby’s party. Perhaps his presence gave the evening its peculiar quality of oppressiveness—it stands out in my memory from Gatsby’s other parties that summer. There were the same people, or at least the same sort of people, the same profusion of champagne, the same many-colored, many-keyed commotion, but I felt an unpleasantness in the air, a pervading harshness that hadn’t been there before. Or perhaps I had merely grown used to it, grown to accept West Egg as a world complete in itself, with its own standards and its own great figures, second to nothing because it had no consciousness of being so, and now I was looking at it again, through Daisy’s eyes. It is invariably saddening to look through new eyes at things upon which you have expended your own powers of adjustment.
汤姆显然对黛西独自到处乱跑感到不安，因为在接下来的星期六晚上，他和她一起参加了盖茨比的聚会。也许他的出现给这个夜晚带来了一种独特的压抑感——在我的记忆中，它与盖茨比那年夏天的其他聚会相比显得格外突出。还是同样的人，或者至少是同一类人，同样丰盛的香槟，同样五彩斑斓、多调的骚动，但我感觉到空气中弥漫着一种不愉快的感觉，一种从未有过的粗暴感弥漫在其中。前。或者也许我只是已经习惯了，逐渐接受了西埃格作为一个本身完整的世界，有自己的标准和自己的伟大人物，首屈一指，因为它没有意识到如此，而现在我正在看再次通过黛西的眼睛。当你用新的眼光看待那些你已经耗尽了自己的调整能力的事物时，总是会感到悲伤。

They arrived at twilight and as we strolled out among the sparkling hundreds Daisy’s voice was playing murmurous tricks in her throat.
他们在黄昏时分到达，当我们在闪闪发光的数百人中漫步时，黛西的声音在她的喉咙里低语着。

‘These things excite me SO,’ she whispered. ‘If you want to kiss me any time during the evening, Nick, just let me know and I’ll be glad to arrange it for you. Just mention my name. Or present a green card. I’m giving out green——‘
“这些事情让我非常兴奋，”她低声说道。 “如果你晚上想吻我，尼克，请告诉我，我很乐意为你安排。只要提到我的名字就可以了。或者出示绿卡。我发出绿色——’

‘Look around,’ suggested Gatsby.
“看看周围，”盖茨比建议道。

‘I’m looking around. I’m having a marvelous——‘
‘我在环顾四周。我有一个奇妙的——”

‘You must see the faces of many people you’ve heard about.’
“你一定看到了很多你听说过的人的面孔。”

Tom’s arrogant eyes roamed the crowd.
汤姆傲慢的目光在人群中扫视。

‘We don’t go around very much,’ he said. ‘In fact I was just thinking I don’t know a soul here.’
“我们不常到处走动，”他说。 “事实上，我只是想这里我一个人都不认识。”

‘Perhaps you know that lady.’ Gatsby indicated a gorgeous, scarcely human orchid of a woman who sat in state under a white plum tree. Tom and Daisy stared, with that peculiarly unreal feeling that accompanies the recognition of a hitherto ghostly celebrity of the movies.
“也许你认识那位女士。”盖茨比指着一朵华丽的、几乎不像人的兰花的女人，她端坐在一棵白梅树下。汤姆和黛西凝视着，带着一种特别不真实的感觉，伴随着认出一位迄今为止幽灵般的电影名人。

‘She’s lovely,’ said Daisy.
“她很可爱，”黛西说。

‘The man bending over her is her director.’
“俯身在她身上的男人是她的导演。”

He took them ceremoniously from group to group:
他隆重地带领他们从一组到另一组：

‘Mrs. Buchanan … and Mr. Buchanan——’ After an instant’s hesitation he added: ‘the polo player.’
'太太。布坎南……还有布坎南先生——”犹豫片刻后，他补充道：“马球运动员。”

‘Oh no,’ objected Tom quickly, ‘Not me.’
“哦，不，”汤姆很快反对道，“我不是。”

But evidently the sound of it pleased Gatsby for Tom remained ‘the polo player’ for the rest of the evening.
但显然，这声音让盖茨比很高兴，因为汤姆在整个晚上都保持着“马球运动员”的状态。

‘I’ve never met so many celebrities!’ Daisy exclaimed. ‘I liked that man—what was his name?—with the sort of blue nose.’
“我从来没有见过这么多名人！”黛西惊呼道。 “我喜欢那个男人——他叫什么名字？——有那种蓝色的鼻子。”

Gatsby identified him, adding that he was a small producer.
盖茨比认出了他，并补充说他是一名小制片人。

‘Well, I liked him anyhow.’
“好吧，无论如何我喜欢他。”

‘I’d a little rather not be the polo player,’ said Tom pleasantly, ‘I’d rather look at all these famous people in—in oblivion.’
“我有点不愿意成为马球运动员，”汤姆愉快地说，“我宁愿看着所有这些名人都被遗忘。”

Daisy and Gatsby danced. I remember being surprised by his graceful, conservative fox-trot—I had never seen him dance before. Then they sauntered over to my house and sat on the steps for half an hour while at her request I remained watchfully in the garden: ‘In case there’s a fire or a flood,’ she explained, ‘or any act of God.’
黛西和盖茨比跳舞。我记得我对他优雅而保守的狐步舞感到惊讶——我以前从未见过他跳舞。然后他们漫步到我家，在台阶上坐了半个小时，而应她的要求，我留在花园里保持警惕：“以防发生火灾或洪水，”她解释道，“或任何天灾。”

Tom appeared from his oblivion as we were sitting down to supper together. ‘Do you mind if I eat with some people over here?’ he said. ‘A fellow’s getting off some funny stuff.’
当我们一起坐下来吃晚饭时，汤姆从他的遗忘中出现了。 “你介意我和这里的一些人一起吃​​饭吗？”他说。 “一个家伙正在做一些有趣的事情。”

‘Go ahead,’ answered Daisy genially, ‘And if you want to take down any addresses here’s my little gold pencil….’ She looked around after a moment and told me the girl was ‘common but pretty,’ and I knew that except for the half hour she’d been alone with Gatsby she wasn’t having a good time.
“来吧，”黛西和蔼地回答，“如果你想记下任何地址，这是我的小金铅笔……”过了一会儿，她环顾四周，告诉我这个女孩“很普通，但很漂亮”，我知道除了和盖茨比单独相处的半个小时里，她过得并不愉快。

We were at a particularly tipsy table. That was my fault—Gatsby had been called to the phone and I’d enjoyed these same people only two weeks before. But what had amused me then turned septic on the air now.
我们坐在一张特别醉的桌子上。那是我的错——盖茨比接到电话，而就在两周前，我还喜欢这些人。但当时让我觉得有趣的事情现在却在空气中变得败坏了。

‘How do you feel, Miss Baedeker?’
“贝德克尔小姐，你感觉怎么样？”

The girl addressed was trying, unsuccessfully, to slump against my shoulder. At this inquiry she sat up and opened her eyes.
说话的那个女孩试图靠在我的肩膀上，但没有成功。听到这个询问，她坐了起来，睁开了眼睛。

‘Wha?’
'世界卫生大会？'

A massive and lethargic woman, who had been urging Daisy to play golf with her at the local club tomorrow, spoke in Miss Baedeker’s defence:
一位身材魁梧、昏昏欲睡的女士为贝德克尔小姐辩护：

‘Oh, she’s all right now. When she’s had five or six cocktails she always starts screaming like that. I tell her she ought to leave it alone.’
‘哦，她现在没事了。当她喝了五六杯鸡尾酒时，她总是开始像那样尖叫。我告诉她她应该别管它。

‘I do leave it alone,’ affirmed the accused hollowly.
“我确实不去管它，”被告空洞地肯定道。

‘We heard you yelling, so I said to Doc Civet here: ‘There’s somebody that needs your help, Doc.’ ‘
“我们听到你大喊大叫，所以我在这里对西维特医生说：‘有人需要你的帮助，医生。’”

‘She’s much obliged, I’m sure,’ said another friend, without gratitude. ‘But you got her dress all wet when you stuck her head in the pool.’
“我确信她非常感激，”另一位朋友毫无感激地说。 “但是当你把她的头伸进泳池时，她的衣服都湿透了。”

‘Anything I hate is to get my head stuck in a pool,’ mumbled Miss Baedeker. ‘They almost drowned me once over in New Jersey.’
“我最讨厌的事情就是把头卡在水池里。”贝德克尔小姐咕哝道。 “有一次他们在新泽西差点把我淹死。”

‘Then you ought to leave it alone,’ countered Doctor Civet.
“那你就别管它了，”西维特医生反驳道。

‘Speak for yourself!’ cried Miss Baedeker violently. ‘Your hand shakes. I wouldn’t let you operate on me!’
“你自己说吧！”贝德克尔小姐猛烈地喊道。 ‘你的手在颤抖。我不会让你给我做手术的！

It was like that. Almost the last thing I remember was standing with Daisy and watching the moving picture director and his Star. They were still under the white plum tree and their faces were touching except for a pale thin ray of moonlight between. It occurred to me that he had been very slowly bending toward her all evening to attain this proximity, and even while I watched I saw him stoop one ultimate degree and kiss at her cheek.
就是这样。我记得的最后一件事几乎就是和黛西站在一起，看着电影导演和他的明星。他们还在白梅树下，脸庞贴在一起，中间只隔着一缕淡淡的月光。我突然想到，他整个晚上都在非常缓慢地向她弯腰，以达到这种接近的程度，甚至在我观看的时候，我也看到他弯下最大的角度，亲吻她的脸颊。

‘I like her,’ said Daisy, ‘I think she’s lovely.’
“我喜欢她，”黛西说，“我觉得她很可爱。”

But the rest offended her—and inarguably, because it wasn’t a gesture but an emotion. She was appalled by West Egg, this unprecedented ‘place’ that Broadway had begotten upon a Long Island fishing village—appalled by its raw vigor that chafed under the old euphemisms and by the too obtrusive fate that herded its inhabitants along a short cut from nothing to nothing. She saw something awful in the very simplicity she failed to understand.
但其余的都冒犯了她——毫无疑问，因为这不是一种姿态，而是一种情感。她对西埃格感到震惊，这个百老汇在长岛渔村上诞生的前所未有的“地方”——对它在古老的委婉说法下磨破的原始活力和过于突兀的命运感到震惊，它迫使它的居民走上一条从虚无中走出来的捷径。什么都没有。她在她无法理解的简单性中看到了一些可怕的东西。

I sat on the front steps with them while they waited for their car. It was dark here in front: only the bright door sent ten square feet of light volleying out into the soft black morning. Sometimes a shadow moved against a dressing-room blind above, gave way to another shadow, an indefinite procession of shadows, who rouged and powdered in an invisible glass.
当他们等车时，我和他们一起坐在前面的台阶上。前面一片漆黑：只有明亮的门发出十平方英尺的光线，射入柔和的黑色早晨。有时，一个影子在上面的更衣室百叶窗上移动，让位于另一个影子，一个无限的影子队伍，他们在隐形玻璃中涂胭脂和扑粉。

‘Who is this Gatsby anyhow?’ demanded Tom suddenly. ‘Some big bootlegger?’
“这个盖茨比到底是谁？”汤姆突然问道。 “某个大走私犯？”

‘Where’d you hear that?’ I inquired.
“你从哪里听说的？”我问道。

‘I didn’t hear it. I imagined it. A lot of these newly rich people are just big bootleggers, you know.’
‘我没听见。我想象的。你知道，这些新贵中的很多人都是大走私犯。

‘Not Gatsby,’ I said shortly.
“不是盖茨比，”我简短地说。

He was silent for a moment. The pebbles of the drive crunched under his feet.
他沉默了一会儿。车道上的鹅卵石在他脚下嘎吱作响。

‘Well, he certainly must have strained himself to get this menagerie together.’
“嗯，他一定是竭尽全力才把这个动物园聚集在一起。”

A breeze stirred the grey haze of Daisy’s fur collar.
微风吹动了黛西毛领上灰色的薄雾。

‘At least they’re more interesting than the people we know,’ she said with an effort.
“至少他们比我们认识的人更有趣，”她努力地说。

‘You didn’t look so interested.’
“你看起来不太感兴趣。”

‘Well, I was.’
“嗯，我是。”

Tom laughed and turned to me.
汤姆笑了，转向我。

‘Did you notice Daisy’s face when that girl asked her to put her under a cold shower?’
“当那个女孩让黛西给她洗冷水澡时，你注意到黛西的脸了吗？”

Daisy began to sing with the music in a husky, rhythmic whisper, bringing out a meaning in each word that it had never had before and would never have again. When the melody rose, her voice broke up sweetly, following it, in a way contralto voices have, and each change tipped out a little of her warm human magic upon the air.
黛西开始用沙哑、有节奏的低语随着音乐唱歌，每个词都表达出以前从未有过、也永远不会再有的含义。当旋律响起时，她的声音甜美地响起，跟随它，就像女低音一样，每一个变化都在空气中散发出一点她温暖的人类魔力。

‘Lots of people come who haven’t been invited,’ she said suddenly. ‘That girl hadn’t been invited. They simply force their way in and he’s too polite to object.’
“很多人都没有被邀请来，”她突然说道。 ‘那个女孩没有被邀请。他们只是强行闯入，而他很有礼貌，没有反对。”

‘I’d like to know who he is and what he does,’ insisted Tom. ‘And I think I’ll make a point of finding out.’
“我想知道他是谁以及他做什么，”汤姆坚持说。 “我想我会努力找出答案。”

‘I can tell you right now,’ she answered. ‘He owned some drug stores, a lot of drug stores. He built them up himself.’
“我现在就可以告诉你，”她回答道。 “他拥有一些药店，很多药店。他自己建造了它们。

The dilatory limousine came rolling up the drive.
拖沓的豪华轿车沿着车道驶来。

‘Good night, Nick,’ said Daisy.
“晚安，尼克，”黛西说。

Her glance left me and sought the lighted top of the steps where ‘Three o’Clock in the Morning,’ a neat, sad little waltz of that year, was drifting out the open door. After all, in the very casualness of Gatsby’s party there were romantic possibilities totally absent from her world. What was it up there in the song that seemed to be calling her back inside? What would happen now in the dim incalculable hours? Perhaps some unbelievable guest would arrive, a person infinitely rare and to be marvelled at, some authentically radiant young girl who with one fresh glance at Gatsby, one moment of magical encounter, would blot out those five years of unwavering devotion.
她的目光离开了我，转向了灯火通明的台阶顶，那一年，一首简洁、悲伤的小华尔兹曲《凌晨三点》正从敞开的门里飘出来。毕竟，在盖茨比极其随意的聚会中，她的世界里完全没有浪漫的可能性。歌曲中的什么似乎在召唤她回到内心？在这难以估量的昏暗时间里，现在会发生什么？也许会有一位令人难以置信的客人到来，一位极其罕见且令人惊叹的人，一位真正容光焕发的年轻女孩，她只要再看一眼盖茨比，一次神奇的邂逅，就会抹掉五年来坚定不移的忠诚。

I stayed late that night. Gatsby asked me to wait until he was free and I lingered in the garden until the inevitable swimming party had run up, chilled and exalted, from the black beach, until the lights were extinguished in the guest rooms overhead. When he came down the steps at last the tanned skin was drawn unusually tight on his face, and his eyes were bright and tired.
那天晚上我呆得很晚。盖茨比让我等他有空，我在花园里徘徊，直到不可避免的游泳队从黑沙滩上跑来，寒冷而兴奋，直到头顶客房的灯光熄灭。当他终于走下台阶时，晒黑的皮肤异常紧绷，他的眼睛明亮而疲惫。

‘She didn’t like it,’ he said immediately.
“她不喜欢这样，”他立即说道。

‘Of course she did.’
“她当然这么做了。”

‘She didn’t like it,’ he insisted. ‘She didn’t have a good time.’
“她不喜欢这样，”他坚持说。 “她过得并不愉快。”

He was silent and I guessed at his unutterable depression.
他沉默不语，我猜到了他难言的沮丧。

‘I feel far away from her,’ he said. ‘It’s hard to make her understand.’
他说：“我感觉离她很远。” “很难让她理解。”

‘You mean about the dance?’
“你是说舞蹈吗？”

‘The dance?’ He dismissed all the dances he had given with a snap of his fingers. ‘Old sport, the dance is unimportant.’
“舞蹈？”他打了个响指，就把所有的舞蹈都打消了。 “老兄，舞蹈并不重要。”

He wanted nothing less of Daisy than that she should go to Tom and say: ‘I never loved you.’ After she had obliterated three years with that sentence they could decide upon the more practical measures to be taken. One of them was that, after she was free, they were to go back to Louisville and be married from her house—just as if it were five years ago.
他对黛西的期望就是让她去找汤姆说：“我从来没有爱过你。”在她用这句话抹去三年之后，他们可以决定采取更实际的措施。其中之一是，在她自由后，他们将回到路易斯维尔，在她家结婚——就像五年前一样。

‘And she doesn’t understand,’ he said. ‘She used to be able to understand. We’d sit for hours——‘
“她不明白，”他说。 ‘她以前能够理解。我们会坐几个小时——”

He broke off and began to walk up and down a desolate path of fruit rinds and discarded favors and crushed flowers.
他停下来，开始在一条荒凉的小路上走来走去，小路上布满了果皮、丢弃的恩惠和压碎的鲜花。

‘I wouldn’t ask too much of her,’ I ventured. ‘You can’t repeat the past.’
“我不会对她要求太多，”我大胆地说。 “你不能重复过去。”

‘Can’t repeat the past?’ he cried incredulously. ‘Why of course you can!’
“不能重蹈覆辙吗？”他难以置信地喊道。 “为什么你当然可以！”

He looked around him wildly, as if the past were lurking here in the shadow of his house, just out of reach of his hand.
他疯狂地环顾四周，仿佛过去就潜伏在他房子的阴影里，触手可及。

‘I’m going to fix everything just the way it was before,’ he said, nodding determinedly. ‘She’ll see.’
“我会像以前一样修复一切，”他坚定地点点头说道。 “她会看到的。”

He talked a lot about the past and I gathered that he wanted to recover something, some idea of himself perhaps, that had gone into loving Daisy. His life had been confused and disordered since then, but if he could once return to a certain starting place and go over it all slowly, he could find out what that thing was….
他谈了很多过去的事，我猜想他想找回一些东西，也许是他自己的一些想法，这些东西已经融入了黛西的爱之中。他的生活从此变得混乱无序，但如果他能回到某个起点，慢慢地看一遍，他就能知道那东西是什么……

… One autumn night, five years before, they had been walking down the street when the leaves were falling, and they came to a place where there were no trees and the sidewalk was white with moonlight. They stopped here and turned toward each other. Now it was a cool night with that mysterious excitement in it which comes at the two changes of the year. The quiet lights in the houses were humming out into the darkness and there was a stir and bustle among the stars. Out of the corner of his eye Gatsby saw that the blocks of the sidewalk really formed a ladder and mounted to a secret place above the trees—he could climb to it, if he climbed alone, and once there he could suck on the pap of life, gulp down the incomparable milk of wonder.
……五年前的一个秋夜，他们在树叶飘落的街道上行走，来到一处没有树木的地方，人行道被月光映得雪白。他们停了下来，转身面向对方。现在是一个凉爽的夜晚，带着一年中两次更替时出现的神秘兴奋。房屋里安静的灯光在黑暗中嗡嗡作响，星星间一片喧闹。盖茨比用眼角的余光看到，人行道上的石块确实形成了一架梯子，通向树木上方的一个秘密地方——如果他独自爬上去，他就能爬到那里，一旦到了那里，他就可以吮吸生命，吞下无与伦比的奇迹乳汁。

His heart beat faster and faster as Daisy’s white face came up to his own. He knew that when he kissed this girl, and forever wed his unutterable visions to her perishable breath, his mind would never romp again like the mind of God. So he waited, listening for a moment longer to the tuning fork that had been struck upon a star. Then he kissed her. At his lips’ touch she blossomed for him like a flower and the incarnation was complete.
当黛西苍白的脸贴近他的脸时，他的心跳得越来越快。他知道，当他亲吻这个女孩，并将他难以言喻的幻象永远与她易逝的呼吸结合在一起时，他的思想将永远不会再像上帝的思想那样嬉戏。于是他等待着，又听了一会儿音叉敲击星星的声音。然后他吻了她。在他嘴唇的触碰下，她像一朵花一样为他绽放，化身完成。

Through all he said, even through his appalling sentimentality, I was reminded of something—an elusive rhythm, a fragment of lost words, that I had heard somewhere a long time ago. For a moment a phrase tried to take shape in my mouth and my lips parted like a dumb man’s, as though there was more struggling upon them than a wisp of startled air. But they made no sound and what I had almost remembered was uncommunicable forever.
通过他所说的一切，甚至通过他令人震惊的多愁善感，我想起了一些东西——一种难以捉摸的节奏，一段失落的话语的片段，我很久以前在哪里听过。有一瞬间，一个词句试图在我嘴里成形，我的嘴唇像哑巴一样张开，仿佛除了一缕受惊的空气之外，还有更多的东西在挣扎。但他们没有发出任何声音，而我几乎记住的事情却永远无法传达。

Chapter 7
第7章

It was when curiosity about Gatsby was at its highest that the lights in his house failed to go on one Saturday night—and, as obscurely as it had begun, his career as Trimalchio was over.
就在对盖茨比的好奇心达到最高点的时候，他家的灯在一个周六的晚上没有亮起来——而且，正如它开始时一样模糊，他作为特里马尔基奥的职业生涯结束了。

Only gradually did I become aware that the automobiles which turned expectantly into his drive stayed for just a minute and then drove sulkily away. Wondering if he were sick I went over to find out—an unfamiliar butler with a villainous face squinted at me suspiciously from the door.
渐渐地，我才意识到那些满怀期待地驶入他车道的汽车只停留了一分钟，然后就闷闷不乐地开走了。我想知道他是不是生病了，我就过去一探究竟——一个陌生的管家，一脸凶恶地在门口斜眼看着我。

‘Is Mr. Gatsby sick?’
“盖茨比先生病了吗？”

‘Nope.’ After a pause he added ‘sir’ in a dilatory, grudging way.
“不。”停顿了一下，他拖拖拉拉、勉强地加了一句“先生”。

‘I hadn’t seen him around, and I was rather worried. Tell him Mr. Carraway came over.’
“我没见过他，我很担心。告诉他卡罗威先生过来了。”

‘Who?’ he demanded rudely.
“谁？”他粗鲁地问道。

‘Carraway.’
“卡拉威。”

‘Carraway. All right, I’ll tell him.’ Abruptly he slammed the door.
‘卡拉威。好吧，我会告诉他的。”他突然关上门。

My Finn informed me that Gatsby had dismissed every servant in his house a week ago and replaced them with half a dozen others, who never went into West Egg Village to be bribed by the tradesmen, but ordered moderate supplies over the telephone. The grocery boy reported that the kitchen looked like a pigsty, and the general opinion in the village was that the new people weren’t servants at all.
我的芬兰人告诉我，盖茨比一周前解雇了家里的所有仆人，并用另外六名仆人取而代之，他们从来没有去西卵村接受商人的贿赂，而是通过电话订购了适量的物资。杂货店男孩报告说厨房看起来像猪圈，村里的普遍看法是新来的人根本不是仆人。

Next day Gatsby called me on the phone.
第二天盖茨比给我打电话。

‘Going away?’ I inquired.
“要走吗？”我问道。

‘No, old sport.’
“不，老兄。”

‘I hear you fired all your servants.’
“我听说你解雇了所有仆人。”

‘I wanted somebody who wouldn’t gossip. Daisy comes over quite often—in the afternoons.’
“我想要一个不会八卦的人。黛西经常过来——下午的时候。

So the whole caravansary had fallen in like a card house at the disapproval in her eyes.
因此，整个商队都因为她眼神中的不满而像纸牌屋一样崩溃了。

‘They’re some people Wolfshiem wanted to do something for. They’re all brothers and sisters. They used to run a small hotel.’
“沃尔夫山姆想为他们做点什么。他们都是兄弟姐妹。他们曾经经营一家小旅馆。

‘I see.’
'我懂了。'

He was calling up at Daisy’s request—would I come to lunch at her house tomorrow? Miss Baker would be there. Half an hour later Daisy herself telephoned and seemed relieved to find that I was coming. Something was up. And yet I couldn’t believe that they would choose this occasion for a scene—especially for the rather harrowing scene that Gatsby had outlined in the garden.
他是应黛西的要求打电话的——我明天能去她家吃午饭吗？贝克小姐会在那里。半小时后，黛西亲自打来电话，发现我来了，她似乎松了口气。有事发生了。但我不敢相信他们会选择这个场合来拍摄场景——尤其是盖茨比在花园里描绘的相当悲惨的场景。

The next day was broiling, almost the last, certainly the warmest, of the summer. As my train emerged from the tunnel into sunlight, only the hot whistles of the National Biscuit Company broke the simmering hush at noon. The straw seats of the car hovered on the edge of combustion; the woman next to me perspired delicately for a while into her white shirtwaist, and then, as her newspaper dampened under her fingers, lapsed despairingly into deep heat with a desolate cry. Her pocket-book slapped to the floor.
第二天天气炎热，几乎是夏天的最后一天，当然也是最温暖的一天。当我的火车从隧道驶入阳光下时，只有国家饼干公司的热汽笛声打破了中午的寂静。汽车的稻草座椅悬停在燃烧的边缘；我旁边的女人在她的白色衬衣里流了一会儿汗，然后，当她的报纸在她的手指下沾湿时，她绝望地陷入深热之中，发出一声凄凉的哭泣。她的皮夹掉到了地板上。

‘Oh, my!’ she gasped.
“哦，天哪！”她喘着气说。

I picked it up with a weary bend and handed it back to her, holding it at arm’s length and by the extreme tip of the corners to indicate that I had no designs upon it—but every one near by, including the woman, suspected me just the same.
我疲倦地弯腰捡起它，递还给她，将它保持一臂远的距离，并握住它的角尖，以表明我对它没有任何意图——但附近的每个人，包括那个女人，都怀疑我一样。

‘Hot!’ said the conductor to familiar faces. ‘Some weather! Hot! Hot! Hot! Is it hot enough for you? Is it hot? Is it … ?’
“热！”售票员对熟悉的面孔说道。 ‘天气有些好！热的！热的！热的！对你来说够热吗？热吗？是吗 … ？'

My commutation ticket came back to me with a dark stain from his hand. That any one should care in this heat whose flushed lips he kissed, whose head made damp the pajama pocket over his heart!
返还给我的车票上有他手上留下的深色污渍。在这么热的天气里，任何人都应该关心他亲吻了谁红润的嘴唇，谁的头弄湿了他心口上的睡衣口袋！

… Through the hall of the Buchanans’ house blew a faint wind, carrying the sound of the telephone bell out to Gatsby and me as we waited at the door.
……微弱的风吹过布坎南家的大厅，把电话铃的声音传到了在门口等候的我和盖茨比的耳中。

‘The master’s body!’ roared the butler into the mouthpiece. ‘I’m sorry, madame, but we can’t furnish it—it’s far too hot to touch this noon!’
“主人的尸体！”管家对着话筒吼道。 “对不起，夫人，我们不能提供它——今天中午太热了，无法触摸！”

What he really said was: ‘Yes … yes … I’ll see.’
他真正说的是：“是的……是的……我看看。”

He set down the receiver and came toward us, glistening slightly, to take our stiff straw hats.
他放下听筒，朝我们走来，微微闪闪发亮，接过我们硬邦邦的草帽。

‘Madame expects you in the salon!’ he cried, needlessly indicating the direction. In this heat every extra gesture was an affront to the common store of life.
“夫人在沙龙里等您！”他喊道，不必要地指明了方向。在这么热的天气里，每一个额外的举动都是对普通生命的侮辱。

The room, shadowed well with awnings, was dark and cool. Daisy and Jordan lay upon an enormous couch, like silver idols, weighing down their own white dresses against the singing breeze of the fans.
房间里有遮阳篷，阴暗而凉爽。黛西和乔丹躺在一张巨大的沙发上，就像银色的偶像一样，在歌迷的歌声中压下自己的白色裙子。

‘We can’t move,’ they said together.
“我们不能动，”他们一起说道。

Jordan’s fingers, powdered white over their tan, rested for a moment in mine.
乔丹的手指在晒黑的皮肤上涂着白色的粉末，在我的手指上休息了一会儿。

‘And Mr. Thomas Buchanan, the athlete?’ I inquired.
“那运动员托马斯·布坎南先生呢？”我问道。

Simultaneously I heard his voice, gruff, muffled, husky, at the hall telephone.
与此同时，我在大厅电话里听到了他的声音，粗哑、低沉、沙哑。

Gatsby stood in the center of the crimson carpet and gazed around with fascinated eyes. Daisy watched him and laughed, her sweet, exciting laugh; a tiny gust of powder rose from her bosom into the air.
盖茨比站在深红色的地毯中央，用着迷的眼睛环顾四周。黛西看着他，笑了起来，她的笑声甜美而令人兴奋。一股细小的粉末从她的怀里升到空中。

‘The rumor is,’ whispered Jordan, ‘that that’s Tom’s girl on the telephone.’
“有传言说，”乔丹低声说道，“那是汤姆的女朋友在打电话。”

We were silent. The voice in the hall rose high with annoyance. ‘Very well, then, I won’t sell you the car at all…. I’m under no obligations to you at all…. And as for your bothering me about it at lunch time I won’t stand that at all!’
我们沉默了。大厅里的声音变得高亢，充满了恼怒。 “好吧，那么，我根本不会把车卖给你……”我对你没有任何义务……至于你在午餐时间来打扰我，我根本无法忍受！

‘Holding down the receiver,’ said Daisy cynically.
“按住听筒，”黛西愤世嫉俗地说。

‘No, he’s not,’ I assured her. ‘It’s a bona fide deal. I happen to know about it.’
“不，他不是，”我向她保证。 ‘这是一笔真诚的交易。我碰巧知道这件事。

Tom flung open the door, blocked out its space for a moment with his thick body, and hurried into the room.
汤姆猛地打开门，用他粗壮的身体挡住了片刻的空间，然后就匆匆走进了房间。

‘Mr. Gatsby!’ He put out his broad, flat hand with well-concealed dislike. ‘I’m glad to see you, sir…. Nick….’
'先生。盖茨比！”他伸出宽大扁平的手，脸上掩饰不住的厌恶。 “我很高兴见到你，先生……”缺口…。'

‘Make us a cold drink,’ cried Daisy.
“给我们倒杯冷饮吧，”黛西喊道。

As he left the room again she got up and went over to Gatsby and pulled his face down kissing him on the mouth.
当他再次离开房间时，她站起来走到盖茨比身边，拉下他的脸亲吻他的嘴。

‘You know I love you,’ she murmured.
“你知道我爱你，”她低声说道。

‘You forget there’s a lady present,’ said Jordan.
“你忘了有一位女士在场，”乔丹说。

Daisy looked around doubtfully.
黛西疑惑地环顾四周。

‘You kiss Nick too.’
“你也吻了尼克。”

‘What a low, vulgar girl!’
“真是个低俗粗俗的女孩！”

‘I don’t care!’ cried Daisy and began to clog on the brick fireplace. Then she remembered the heat and sat down guiltily on the couch just as a freshly laundered nurse leading a little girl came into the room.
“我不在乎！”黛西喊道，并开始堵塞砖壁炉。然后她想起了炎热的天气，愧疚地坐在沙发上，这时一名刚洗完澡的护士带着一个小女孩走进了房间。

‘Bles-sed pre-cious,’ she crooned, holding out her arms. ‘Come to your own mother that loves you.’
“天哪，亲爱的，”她低声说道，伸出双臂。 “来到爱你的母亲身边吧。”

The child, relinquished by the nurse, rushed across the room and rooted shyly into her mother’s dress.
孩子被护士抛弃，冲过房间，害羞地扎进妈妈的衣服里。

‘The Bles-sed pre-cious! Did mother get powder on your old yellowy hair? Stand up now, and say How-de-do.’
‘天哪，亲爱的！妈妈是不是在你那黄头发上抹了粉？现在站起来，说“How-de-do”。

Gatsby and I in turn leaned down and took the small reluctant hand. Afterward he kept looking at the child with surprise. I don’t think he had ever really believed in its existence before.
盖茨比和我依次弯下身子，握住那只不情愿的小手。随后他一直惊讶地看着孩子。我认为他以前从未真正相信过它的存在。

‘I got dressed before luncheon,’ said the child, turning eagerly to Daisy.
“我在午饭前穿好衣服了，”孩子急切地转向黛西说道。

‘That’s because your mother wanted to show you off.’ Her face bent into the single wrinkle of the small white neck. ‘You dream, you. You absolute little dream.’
“那是因为你妈妈想炫耀你。”她的脸弯向白色小脖子上的一条皱纹。 ‘你在做梦，你。你绝对是个小梦想。

‘Yes,’ admitted the child calmly. ‘Aunt Jordan’s got on a white dress too.’
“是的。”孩子平静地承认。 “乔丹阿姨也穿了一件白色连衣裙。”

‘How do you like mother’s friends?’ Daisy turned her around so that she faced Gatsby. ‘Do you think they’re pretty?’
“你觉得妈妈的朋友怎么样？”黛西把她转过身，面向盖茨比。 “你觉得它们漂亮吗？”

‘Where’s Daddy?’
“爸爸在哪儿？”

‘She doesn’t look like her father,’ explained Daisy. ‘She looks like me. She’s got my hair and shape of the face.’
“她看起来不像她父亲，”黛西解释道。 ‘她看起来像我。她有我的头发和脸型。

Daisy sat back upon the couch. The nurse took a step forward and held out her hand.
黛西坐回沙发上。护士上前一步，伸出了手。

‘Come, Pammy.’
“来吧，帕米。”

‘Goodbye, sweetheart!’
“再见，亲爱的！”

With a reluctant backward glance the well-disciplined child held to her nurse’s hand and was pulled out the door, just as Tom came back, preceding four gin rickeys that clicked full of ice.
纪律严明的孩子不情愿地回头看了一眼，抓住了护士的手，被拉出了门，这时汤姆回来了，前面是四杯装满冰块的杜松子酒。

Gatsby took up his drink.
盖茨比拿起酒杯。

‘They certainly look cool,’ he said, with visible tension.
“它们看起来确实很酷，”他说道，语气明显紧张。

We drank in long greedy swallows.
我们贪婪地大口喝着酒。

‘I read somewhere that the sun’s getting hotter every year,’ said Tom genially. ‘It seems that pretty soon the earth’s going to fall into the sun—or wait a minute—it’s just the opposite—the sun’s getting colder every year.
“我在哪里读到过，太阳一年比一年热，”汤姆和蔼地说。 “看起来地球很快就会落入太阳的怀抱——或者等一下——事实恰恰相反——太阳每年都在变得更冷。

‘Come outside,’ he suggested to Gatsby, ‘I’d like you to have a look at the place.’
“到外面来吧，”他向盖茨比建议，“我想让你看看这个地方。”

I went with them out to the veranda. On the green Sound, stagnant in the heat, one small sail crawled slowly toward the fresher sea. Gatsby’s eyes followed it momentarily; he raised his hand and pointed across the bay.
我和他们一起走到阳台上。绿色的海峡上，在炎热中停滞不前，一面小帆慢慢地爬向更清新的大海。盖茨比的目光短暂地追随着它。他举起手，指着海湾对面。

‘I’m right across from you.’
“我就在你对面。”

‘So you are.’
“原来如此。”

Our eyes lifted over the rosebeds and the hot lawn and the weedy refuse of the dog days along shore. Slowly the white wings of the boat moved against the blue cool limit of the sky. Ahead lay the scalloped ocean and the abounding blessed isles.
我们的目光扫过玫瑰花坛、炎热的草坪和海岸边的三伏天杂草丛生的垃圾。船的白色翅膀慢慢地在蓝色凉爽的天空边缘移动。前方是扇形的海洋和众多的幸福岛屿。

‘There’s sport for you,’ said Tom, nodding. ‘I’d like to be out there with him for about an hour.’
“有适合你的运动，”汤姆点点头说。 “我想和他一起待大约一个小时。”

We had luncheon in the dining-room, darkened, too, against the heat, and drank down nervous gayety with the cold ale.
我们在餐厅里吃午饭，餐厅里的光线也比较暗，以抵御炎热的天气，我们喝着冰啤酒，喝下了紧张的快乐。

‘What’ll we do with ourselves this afternoon,’ cried Daisy, ‘and the day after that, and the next thirty years?’
“今天下午我们要做什么，”黛西喊道，“还有后天，以及接下来的三十年？”

‘Don’t be morbid,’ Jordan said. ‘Life starts all over again when it gets crisp in the fall.’
“别病态，”乔丹说。 “当秋天变得清爽时，生活又会重新开始。”

‘But it’s so hot,’ insisted Daisy, on the verge of tears, ‘And everything’s so confused. Let’s all go to town!’
“但是天气太热了，”黛西几乎要哭了，她坚持道，“而且一切都那么混乱。”我们都进城吧！

Her voice struggled on through the heat, beating against it, moulding its senselessness into forms.
她的声音在炎热中挣扎着，击打着它，将它的毫无意义塑造成形式。

‘I’ve heard of making a garage out of a stable,’ Tom was saying to Gatsby, ‘but I’m the first man who ever made a stable out of a garage.’
“我听说过用马厩改造成车库，”汤姆对盖茨比说，“但我是第一个用车库改造成马厩的人。”

‘Who wants to go to town?’ demanded Daisy insistently. Gatsby’s eyes floated toward her. ‘Ah,’ she cried, ‘you look so cool.’
“谁想去城里？”黛西坚持问道。盖茨比的目光飘向她。 “啊，”她叫道，“你看起来真酷。”

Their eyes met, and they stared together at each other, alone in space. With an effort she glanced down at the table.
他们的目光交汇在一起，彼此凝视着对方，只剩下空中的一人。她费力地低头看了一眼桌子。

‘You always look so cool,’ she repeated.
“你看起来总是那么酷，”她重复道。

She had told him that she loved him, and Tom Buchanan saw. He was astounded. His mouth opened a little and he looked at Gatsby and then back at Daisy as if he had just recognized her as some one he knew a long time ago.
她告诉他她爱他，汤姆·布坎南看到了。他很惊讶。他的嘴微微张开，看看盖茨比，然后又看看黛西，仿佛他刚刚认出她是他很久以前认识的人。

‘You resemble the advertisement of the man,’ she went on innocently. ‘You know the advertisement of the man——‘
“你就像那个男人的广告。”她天真地继续说道。 “你知道那个男人的广告——”

‘All right,’ broke in Tom quickly, ‘I’m perfectly willing to go to town. Come on—we’re all going to town.’
“好吧，”汤姆很快插话道，“我非常愿意进城。”来吧——我们都要去城里。

He got up, his eyes still flashing between Gatsby and his wife. No one moved.
他站了起来，目光仍然在盖茨比和他的妻子之间闪烁。没有人动。

‘Come on!’ His temper cracked a little. ‘What’s the matter, anyhow? If we’re going to town let’s start.’
“来吧！”他的脾气有点暴躁。 ‘到底是怎么回事？如果我们要去城里，那就开始吧。

His hand, trembling with his effort at self control, bore to his lips the last of his glass of ale. Daisy’s voice got us to our feet and out on to the blazing gravel drive.
他的手因努力控制自己而颤抖，将最后一杯麦酒送到唇边。黛西的声音让我们站起来，走向炽热的碎石路。

‘Are we just going to go?’ she objected. ‘Like this? Aren’t we going to let any one smoke a cigarette first?’
“我们就这样走吗？”她反对道。 '像这样？我们不是要让任何人先抽烟吗？

‘Everybody smoked all through lunch.’
“午餐期间每个人都抽烟。”

‘Oh, let’s have fun,’ she begged him. ‘It’s too hot to fuss.’
“哦，让我们玩得开心吧，”她恳求他。 “太热了，没法大惊小怪。”

He didn’t answer.
他没有回答。

‘Have it your own way,’ she said. ‘Come on, Jordan.’
“按你自己的方式来吧，”她说。 “来吧，乔丹。”

They went upstairs to get ready while we three men stood there shuffling the hot pebbles with our feet. A silver curve of the moon hovered already in the western sky. Gatsby started to speak, changed his mind, but not before Tom wheeled and faced him expectantly.
他们上楼去准备，而我们三个人站在那里用脚拖着热石子。一道银色的弯月已经悬在西边的天空。盖茨比开始说话，但他改变了主意，但汤姆转过身来，满怀期待地面对着他。

‘Have you got your stables here?’ asked Gatsby with an effort.
“你的马厩在这里吗？”盖茨比费力地问道。

‘About a quarter of a mile down the road.’
“沿着路大约四分之一英里。”

‘Oh.’
'哦。'

A pause.
停顿一下。

‘I don’t see the idea of going to town,’ broke out Tom savagely. ‘Women get these notions in their heads——‘
“我不认为有进城的念头。”汤姆粗暴地说道。 “女人脑子里有这些观念——”

‘Shall we take anything to drink?’ called Daisy from an upper window.
“我们要喝点什么吗？”黛西从楼上的窗户喊道。

‘I’ll get some whiskey,’ answered Tom. He went inside.
“我去买点威士忌，”汤姆回答。他进去了。

Gatsby turned to me rigidly:
盖茨比僵硬地转向我：

‘I can’t say anything in his house, old sport.’
“我在他家里什么都不能说，老兄。”

‘She’s got an indiscreet voice,’ I remarked. ‘It’s full of——‘
“她的声音很不检点，”我评论道。 ‘里面充满了——’

I hesitated.
我犹豫了。

‘Her voice is full of money,’ he said suddenly.
“她的声音里充满了金钱，”他突然说道。

That was it. I’d never understood before. It was full of money—that was the inexhaustible charm that rose and fell in it, the jingle of it, the cymbals’ song of it…. High in a white palace the king’s daughter, the golden girl….
就是这样。我以前一直不明白。里面装满了钱——那就是在里面升起又落下的无穷无尽的魅力，它的叮当声，它的铙钹之歌……。在白色宫殿的高处，国王的女儿，金色的女孩......

Tom came out of the house wrapping a quart bottle in a towel, followed by Daisy and Jordan wearing small tight hats of metallic cloth and carrying light capes over their arms.
汤姆用毛巾包裹着一夸脱的瓶子走出家门，黛西和乔丹戴着金属布制成的小紧身帽，手臂上披着浅色斗篷。

‘Shall we all go in my car?’ suggested Gatsby. He felt the hot, green leather of the seat. ‘I ought to have left it in the shade.’
“我们都坐我的车去吧？”盖茨比建议道。他感觉到座椅上滚烫的绿色皮革。 “我应该把它放在阴凉处。”

‘Is it standard shift?’ demanded Tom.
“这是标准班次吗？”汤姆问道。

‘Yes.’
'是的。'

‘Well, you take my coupé and let me drive your car to town.’
“好吧，你开我的双门轿车，让我开你的车去城里。”

The suggestion was distasteful to Gatsby.
这个建议令盖茨比反感。

‘I don’t think there’s much gas,’ he objected.
“我认为没有多少天然气，”他反对道。

‘Plenty of gas,’ said Tom boisterously. He looked at the gauge. ‘And if it runs out I can stop at a drug store. You can buy anything at a drug store nowadays.’
“汽油充足，”汤姆大声说道。他看了看仪表。 “如果药用完了，我可以去药店。”现在你可以在药店买到任何东西。

A pause followed this apparently pointless remark. Daisy looked at Tom frowning and an indefinable expression, at once definitely unfamiliar and vaguely recognizable, as if I had only heard it described in words, passed over Gatsby’s face.
这句显然毫无意义的话之后停顿了一下。黛西皱着眉头看着汤姆，盖茨比脸上掠过一种难以形容的表情，既陌生又依稀可辨，就好像我只听过文字描述一样。

‘Come on, Daisy,’ said Tom, pressing her with his hand toward Gatsby’s car. ‘I’ll take you in this circus wagon.’
“来吧，黛西，”汤姆说着，用手将她推向盖茨比的车。 “我会带你坐这辆马戏团马车。”

He opened the door but she moved out from the circle of his arm.
他打开门，但她却从他的手臂中退了出来。

‘You take Nick and Jordan. We’ll follow you in the coupé.’
‘你就拿尼克和乔丹来说吧。我们会开着双门轿跑车跟着你。

She walked close to Gatsby, touching his coat with her hand. Jordan and Tom and I got into the front seat of Gatsby’s car, Tom pushed the unfamiliar gears tentatively and we shot off into the oppressive heat leaving them out of sight behind.
她走到盖茨比身边，用手抚摸他的外套。乔丹、汤姆和我坐进了盖茨比汽车的前座，汤姆试探性地推动了陌生的齿轮，我们冲进了闷热的天气，把他们抛在了身后。

‘Did you see that?’ demanded Tom.
“你看到了吗？”汤姆问道。

‘See what?’
'看什么？'

He looked at me keenly, realizing that Jordan and I must have known all along.
他敏锐地看着我，意识到乔丹和我一定一直都知道。

‘You think I’m pretty dumb, don’t you?’ he suggested. ‘Perhaps I am, but I have a—almost a second sight, sometimes, that tells me what to do. Maybe you don’t believe that, but science——‘
“你认为我很蠢，不是吗？”他建议道。 “也许我是，但有时，我几乎有第二次洞察力，它告诉我该怎么做。也许你不相信，但科学——”

He paused. The immediate contingency overtook him, pulled him back from the edge of the theoretical abyss.
他停了下来。眼前的意外事件降临到他身上，将他从理论深渊的边缘拉了回来。

‘I’ve made a small investigation of this fellow,’ he continued. ‘I could have gone deeper if I’d known——‘
“我对这个人做了一个小小的调查，”他继续说道。 “如果我知道——”

‘Do you mean you’ve been to a medium?’ inquired Jordan humorously.
“你是说你接触过媒介吗？”乔丹幽默地问道。

‘What?’ Confused, he stared at us as we laughed. ‘A medium?’
“什么？”当我们大笑时，他困惑地盯着我们。 “媒介？”

‘About Gatsby.’
“关于盖茨比。”

‘About Gatsby! No, I haven’t. I said I’d been making a small investigation of his past.’
‘关于盖茨比！不，我没有。我说我一直在对他的过去进行一次小调查。

‘And you found he was an Oxford man,’ said Jordan helpfully.
“你发现他是牛津人，”乔丹乐于助人地说。

‘An Oxford man!’ He was incredulous. ‘Like hell he is! He wears a pink suit.’
“一个牛津人！”他难以置信。 ‘真是见鬼了！他穿着一套粉红色的西装。

‘Nevertheless he’s an Oxford man.’
“尽管如此，他是牛津人。”

‘Oxford, New Mexico,’ snorted Tom contemptuously, ‘or something like that.’
“新墨西哥州的牛津，”汤姆轻蔑地哼了一声，“或者类似的地方。”

‘Listen, Tom. If you’re such a snob, why did you invite him to lunch?’ demanded Jordan crossly.
“听着，汤姆。如果你这么势利，为什么要邀请他共进午餐？”乔丹生气地问道。

‘Daisy invited him; she knew him before we were married—God knows where!’
“黛西邀请了他；她在我们结婚之前就认识他了——天知道在哪里！

We were all irritable now with the fading ale and, aware of it, we drove for a while in silence. Then as Doctor T. J. Eckleburg’s faded eyes came into sight down the road, I remembered Gatsby’s caution about gasoline.
现在，我们都因为啤酒的褪色而感到烦躁，意识到这一点后，我们默默地开了一会儿车。然后，当T·J·埃克尔伯格医生那双褪色的眼睛出现在路上时，我想起了盖茨比对汽油的谨慎态度。

‘We’ve got enough to get us to town,’ said Tom.
“我们的钱足够进城了，”汤姆说。

‘But there’s a garage right here,’ objected Jordan. ‘I don’t want to get stalled in this baking heat.’
“但是这里有一个车库，”乔丹反对道。 “我不想在这么炎热的天气里陷入困境。”

Tom threw on both brakes impatiently and we slid to an abrupt dusty stop under Wilson’s sign. After a moment the proprietor emerged from the interior of his establishment and gazed hollow-eyed at the car.
汤姆不耐烦地踩下了两个刹车，我们在威尔逊的标志下突然停了下来，尘土飞扬。过了一会儿，店主从店里出来，眼神空洞地看着那辆车。

‘Let’s have some gas!’ cried Tom roughly. ‘What do you think we stopped for—to admire the view?’
“我们喝点汽油吧！”汤姆粗声粗气地喊道。 “你认为我们停下来是为了什么——欣赏风景？”

‘I’m sick,’ said Wilson without moving. ‘I been sick all day.’
“我病了。”威尔逊一动不动地说。 “我一整天都病了。”

‘What’s the matter?’
'怎么了？'

‘I’m all run down.’
“我已经精疲力竭了。”

‘Well, shall I help myself?’ Tom demanded. ‘You sounded well enough on the phone.’
“好吧，我要自己动手吗？”汤姆问道。 “你在电话里听起来很好。”

With an effort Wilson left the shade and support of the doorway and, breathing hard, unscrewed the cap of the tank. In the sunlight his face was green.
威尔逊费了好大的劲才离开门口的阴影和支撑物，喘着粗气，拧开了水箱的盖子。阳光下，他的脸呈绿色。

‘I didn’t mean to interrupt your lunch,’ he said. ‘But I need money pretty bad and I was wondering what you were going to do with your old car.’
“我不是故意打扰你吃午饭的，”他说。 “但我非常需要钱，我想知道你打算如何处理你的旧车。”

‘How do you like this one?’ inquired Tom. ‘I bought it last week.’
“你觉得这个怎么样？”汤姆问道。 “我上周买的。”

‘It’s a nice yellow one,’ said Wilson, as he strained at the handle.
“这是一把漂亮的黄色的，”威尔逊一边说，一边用力拉着把手。
